    [Aggaññasutta]{lang="sa"} the Buddha narrates a legend showing how
    these things arise. The point is not that we should not safeguard
    (*[ārakkha]{lang="sa"}*) our possessions. It is, rather, that so
    long as we live in a world where safeguarding possessions is
    necessary, there will be conflict and violence.

[^186]: "Stinginess" is *macchariya*.

[^187]: There are many words in Pali that approximate the English word
    "attachment". Here it is *[ajjhosāna]{lang="sa"}*.

[^188]: "Evaluation" is *vinicchaya*. We like to weigh up and consider
    the pros and cons of different objects of desire.

[^189]: Those who have nothing are grateful for any small thing, and do
    not indulge in picking and choosing.

[^190]: Our senses are tuned to hunt out and acquire pleasure.

[^191]: This is the normal definition of craving in the four noble
    truths, supplementing the previous definition in terms of the six
    senses. Both are included in this sutta to show that they do not
    contradict, but rather reveal different aspects of the same thing.
    It is not just sensual desire that drives acquisition. For example,
    religious people fight over sacred ground or holy objects to gain a
    place in heaven; or else those driven by nihilism go to any lengths
    for alcohol or drugs to erase existence.

[^192]: The threefold analysis of feeling leads to the process of
    acquisition, while the sixfold analysis of feeling leads to
    dependent origination.

[^193]: And now we rejoin the main sequence.

[^194]: People mostly want to enjoy pleasant sensations, in this life
    and the next, but those sensations depend on a constant supply of
    the appropriate stimuli.

[^195]: Name and form are said to be conditions for contact also at [Snp
    4.11:11.1](https://suttacentral.net/snp4.11/en/sujato#11.1).

[^196]: The "set of mental phenomena known as name" is
    *[nāmakāya]{lang="sa"}*. Its function depends not any underlying
    essence, but on the "features" by which it is "made known"; this is
    a phenomenological analysis. \| "Labeling contact" is
    *adhivacanasamphassa*; it is the active process by which the mind
    makes sense of the world by attaching labels to experience. This
    passage reinforces the linguistic significance of
    *[nāma]{lang="sa"}*.

[^197]: The "set of physical phenomena known as form" is
    *[rūpakāya]{lang="sa"}*. \| "Impingement contact" is
    *[paṭighasamphassa]{lang="sa"}*. Here *[paṭigha]{lang="sa"}* refers
    to the "striking" of physical phenomena against each other, such as
    light "hitting" the eye. It most commonly appears in this sense in
    the formula that begins the formless attainments.

[^198]: Labeling moves from the mind to the world; impingement moves
    from the world to the mind. Together they create a dynamic two-way
    process by which we learn about the world and how to make sense of
    it.

[^199]: Contact is fundamentally a meeting, normally expressed as the
    coming together of the sense stimulus (light), the sense organ
    (eye), and sense consciousness. By skipping the direct mention of
    the six senses, the Buddha opens another perspective on this
    process: mental labeling meets sense impingement, each essential to
    the other, and together making contact possible. The analysis itself
    exemplifies this process, as it starts by looking at the process
    from each side, and moves towards integration, seeing them both
    together.

[^200]: Consciousness in dependent origination is normally defined as
    the six kinds of sense consciousness ([SN
    12:6.3](https://suttacentral.net/sn12/en/sujato#6.3)). The purpose
    of this is to emphasize that the process of rebirth and
    transmigration is an empirical process, which depends on the same
    ordinary consciousness we are experiencing now. Here, once again, by
    skipping the six senses, a new mode of analysis opens up, which
    emphasizes the organic growth of the individual.

[^201]: "Conceived" is *okkamissatha*, literally "descend" or "arrive".
    \| "Coagulate" assumes the PTS reading *samucchissatha* (Sanskrit
    *sammurch*). I believe this echoes the belief that the embryo is
    "coagulated" from the mix of blood and semen. \| Linguistically,
    this passage through to [DN
    15:22.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn15/en/sujato#22.2) is marked
    with the extremely rare verbal ending *-issatha*, which is the
    middle form of the third person singular conditional.

[^202]: "Miscarried" is *vokkamissatha*. \| "State of existence" is
    *[itthattā]{lang="sa"}*, which is most commonly found in the
    declaration of the arahant that they will no longer be reborn into
    "this state of existence". \| Here "born" is *abhinibbatti*, which
    is listed along with *[jāti]{lang="sa"}*, *okkanti*, and other terms
    as a synonym in the standard definition of rebirth ([SN
    12.2:4.2](https://suttacentral.net/sn12.2/en/sujato#4.2), [MN
    9:24--26.7](https://suttacentral.net/mn9/en/sujato#24-26.7), [DN
    22:18.4](https://suttacentral.net/dn22/en/sujato#18.4)).

[^203]: The connection between dependent origination and childhood
    development is further explored in [MN
    38:28.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn38/en/sujato#28.1).

[^204]: Now we turn to the mirror side of the pair of conditions.

[^205]: Just as name and form---the organic, sensual, and sense-making
    body---cannot grow without consciousness, so too consciousness must
    acquire a landing or grounding place to be "planted" in name and
    form. \| *Dukkhasamudayasambhavo* ("the coming to be of the origin
    of suffering") might be rendered "the coming to be and origin of
    suffering". However, *dukkhasamudaya* occurs some hundreds of times
    in the sense "origin of suffering" so I take it in the same way
    here. This is supported by the PTS variant reading *dukkhasamudayo
    sambhavo*.

[^206]: This passage continues to employ rare middle forms, this time
    *-etha*, the third person singular optative.

[^207]: This passage explains why the sequence ends here rather than
    proceeding in the usual way to choices and ignorance. Any state of
    being ultimately depends on the codependency of name and form with
    consciousness. Within this key relationship is the extent not only
    of language, but also of wisdom, and the secret to the undoing of
    transmigration itself. One of the many profound implications of this
    is that there is no such thing as a state of pure consciousness
    independent of concepts. \| *[Ettāvatā]{lang="sa"}
    [vaṭṭaṁ]{lang="sa"} vattati [itthattaṁ]{lang="sa"}
    [paññāpanāya]{lang="sa"}* should be read with such passages as [SN
    22.56:5.3](https://suttacentral.net/sn22.56/en/sujato#5.3): *ye
    kevalino [vaṭṭaṁ]{lang="sa"} [tesaṁ]{lang="sa"} natthi
    [paññāpanāya]{lang="sa"}* ("For consummate ones, there is no cycle
    of rebirths to be found"). \| The [Mahāsaṅgīti]{lang="sa"} reading
    *[aññamaññapaccayatā]{lang="sa"} pavattati* is spurious, since it
    inserts an Abhidhamma concept from the commentary.

[^208]: The text now turns to an analysis of theories of "self"
    (*[attā]{lang="sa"}*), which is comparable to some of the passages
    of [DN 1](https://suttacentral.net/dn1/en/sujato). The Buddha began
    his discourse by stating that it is the failure to understand
    dependent origination that keeps beings trapped in transmigration.
    Dependent origination explains transmigration in a purely empirical
    way by inferring from the mental and physical phenomena we
    experience here and now. Self theorists, on the other hand, explain
    transmigration by introducing a new metaphysical principle, the
    "self" or "soul", by which they assume that the individual has an
    eternal underlying essence.

[^209]: "Formed" is *[rūpī]{lang="sa"}* ("possessing form"), identifying
    the self with the first of the five aggregates. If something were
    really the core essence of a person, you would think it is readily
    knowable. But the Buddha shows that theorists describe the self in
    multiple different and incompatible ways. Each of these draws on
    some more-or-less arbitrary aspect of empirical reality, such as
    "form", to describe an unknowable metaphysical entity that is in
    fact just pure supposition.

[^210]: An example of a self that is "physical and limited" would be the
    body.

[^211]: Such as the cosmos.

[^212]: Perhaps this is the self of "limited perception" ([DN
    1:2.38.13](https://suttacentral.net/dn1/en/sujato#2.38.13)). This
    would be where the mind is aware of something limited, and the self
    is identified with the mental dimension of that awareness.

[^213]: Such as the formless dimensions.

[^214]: The three options ("it is", "it will be so", and "I will make it
    be so") illustrate how the theorist resorts to ever more convoluted
    means to justify the lack of empirical support for their pet theory.
    \| *[Bhāviṁ]{lang="sa"}* ("sure to become") is the root
    *[bhū]{lang="sa"}* with the primary affix *-[ī]{lang="sa"}*, which
    connotes an inevitable future state. \| *Tattha* ("in some other
    place", literally "there") is explained by the commentary as
    *paraloke* ("in the next world").

[^215]: Their surface differences rest on the same underlying
    assumption, so if the assumption is disproved there is no need to
    refute each individual theory. \| *[Iccālaṁ]{lang="sa"}* resolves to
    *iti [alaṁ]{lang="sa"}*.

[^216]: This is the Buddha, who does not theorize a metaphysical self.
    Implicit in this argument is Occam's razor, "entities must not be
    multiplied beyond necessity". Since the self theorists want to prove
    the existence of the "self", it is up to them to supply the grounds
    to support their suppositions. Since they fail to do so, the
    rational position is that there is no self. The Buddha is not under
    a similar obligation to prove the non-existence of the "self", since
    it is reasonable to assume that things do not exist until the
    evidence says otherwise.

[^217]: Having asserted a metaphysical "self", the theorists go on to
    make certain observations and interpretations regarding it.

[^218]: The Buddha moves from theories of the self as form to feeling,
    the second of the five aggregates.

[^219]: For example, identifying the self with the supposed eternal
    bliss of heaven. As with the description of self in physical terms,
    the theorist proceeds from a simple assertion of identity to more
    complicated hypotheses.

[^220]: This is the inverse of the previous. The self is still defined
    *in relation to* feeling, but it is a negative relation. Such
    theories are commonly found in the [Upaniṣads]{lang="sa"}, where a
    prominent thread of analysis systematically rejects all the things
    that are *not* the self (*neti*), before finally arriving at what
    *is* the self (eg. [Bṛhadāraṇyaka]{lang="sa"} [Upaniṣad]{lang="sa"}
    4.5.15).

[^221]: Here the theorist describes feeling as a function of the self:
    it is not what the self *is*, but what the self *does*. At [MN
    38:5.11](https://suttacentral.net/mn38/en/sujato#5.11) (= [MN
    2:8.8](https://suttacentral.net/mn2/en/sujato#8.8)),
    [Sāti]{lang="sa"} describes the self as consciousness, "the speaker
    and feeler who experiences the results of good and bad deeds in all
    the different realms".

[^222]: The Buddha goes on to develop specific arguments addressing each
    position.

[^223]: The Buddha points out the universal experience of feeling, thus
    establishing his argument on common ground. This same argument is
    found at [MN 74:10.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn74/en/sujato#10.1).

[^224]: The idea that only one kind of feeling can be experienced at a
    time became an adage of Buddhist psychology, but it is not obvious
    to me that it is the case. Here and at MN 74 it is assumed to be a
    shared belief with non-Buddhists.

[^225]: Feeling is part of the fundamental structure of consciousness.
    This argument comes through more clearly in Pali, for the word for
    "feeling" (*[vedanā]{lang="sa"}*) is derived from and still lies
    close to the sense of "knowing, experiencing". Thus the question is,
    "If there was no mind, would there be the thought 'I am this'?" The
    commentary explains that this refers to the bare material realm
    which is devoid of consciousness. The commentary appears to support
    the variant reading *[ahamasmī]{lang="sa"}* here. This makes sense
    in context, for "I am" is the first and most primordial assertion of
    a self, while "I am this" is a more sophisticated identification of
    the self in relation to the aggregates.

[^226]: The theorist avoids identifying feeling as the self, but they
    must identify *something* as the self (as for example,
    [Sāti]{lang="sa"} said the self was *[viññāṇa]{lang="sa"}*).
    Feeling, however, is deeply wound into the structure of
    consciousness, so if feeling were to be utterly absent, no other
    mental phenomena could continue, and there would therefore be no
    possibility of forming a theory of self.

[^227]: Letting go is not just a conceptual matter, it has immediate
    emotional consequences. *Paritassati* conveys the twin senses of
    desire and agitation, for which "anxiety" seems the best fit.

[^228]: This recalls the similar statement at [DN
    15:22.6](https://suttacentral.net/dn15/en/sujato#22.6). Whereas
    there it was a statement about name and form with consciousness,
    here it is a description of the arahant who has fully realized it.
    Unlike the theorists whose views do not withstand empirical
    scrutiny, the arahant's liberation is based on a direct
    understanding of reality.

[^229]: The Buddha returns once more to the question of rebirth,
    describing various states of rebirth in terms of consciousness. The
    seven planes are also mentioned at [DN
    33:2.3.28](https://suttacentral.net/dn33/en/sujato#2.3.28), [DN
    34:1.8.11](https://suttacentral.net/dn34/en/sujato#1.8.11), and [AN
    7.44:1.1](https://suttacentral.net/an7.44/en/sujato#1.1).

[^230]: "Plane of consciousness" is *[viññāṇaṭṭhiti]{lang="sa"}*, which
    could also be rendered "station".

[^231]: In the first of these dimensions there is no consciousness at
    all, and in the second there is no consciousness in the normal
    sense, which is why they cannot be called "planes of consciousness".

[^232]: *[Abhinandituṁ]{lang="sa"}*, to "take pleasure in", to "relish",
    or to "delight in" appears in the standard formula for the second
    noble truth, where craving "takes pleasure in various realms"
    (*[tatratatrābhinandinī]{lang="sa"}*).

[^233]: One "freed by wisdom" has wisdom as the dominant faculty.

[^234]: The eight liberations (*[vimokkhā]{lang="sa"}*) are an
    alternative way of describing the meditative experiences of
    *[jhāna]{lang="sa"}*. Elsewhere they are listed at [DN
    16:3.33.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#3.33.1), [DN
    33:3.1.168](https://suttacentral.net/dn33/en/sujato#3.1.168), [DN
    34:2.1.191](https://suttacentral.net/dn34/en/sujato#2.1.191),
    [AN:8.66](https://suttacentral.net/an/en/sujato#8.66), [MN
    77:22.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn77/en/sujato#22.1), and referred
    to at [AN 4.189:1.8](https://suttacentral.net/an4.189/en/sujato#1.8)
    and [Thag
    20.1:33.1](https://suttacentral.net/thag20.1/en/sujato#33.1). At [AN
    8.120](https://suttacentral.net/an8.120/en/sujato) and [MN
    137:27.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn137/en/sujato#27.1) they are
    listed but not called the eight liberations.

[^235]: Someone sees a meditative vision based on the perception of
    their own body, such as through mindfulness of breathing or one's
    own body parts. The first three liberations all cover the four
    *[jhānas]{lang="sa"}*.

[^236]: A meditator grounds their practice on some external focus, such
    as a light, the sight of a corpse, or an external element such as
    earth.

[^237]: This is a practice based on wholly pure and exalted meditation,
    such as the meditation on love, or the sight of a pure brilliant
    color like the sky.

[^238]: The "cessation of perception and feeling"
    (*[saññāvedayitanirodha]{lang="sa"}*) is a culminating meditation
    state of supreme subtlety that often leads directly to awakening
    (but see [AN 5.166](https://suttacentral.net/an5.166/en/sujato)).
    The state itself, like all meditation states, is temporary, but
    afterwards the defilements can be eliminated forever. This
    liberating insight is the consequence of the balanced development of
    all eight factors of the path.

[^239]: This passage emphasizes that this person is fully adept and has
    mastered all the states of meditation. The Buddha claimed such
    mastery ([AN
    9.41:16.1](https://suttacentral.net/an9.41/en/sujato#16.1)), and
    retained the ability even on his deathbed ([DN
    16:6.8.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#6.8.1)).

[^240]: Here we see the terms "one who is freed" used in two ways. All
    arahants have "freedom of heart" (by means of
    *[samādhi]{lang="sa"}*) and "freedom by wisdom" (the realization of
    the Dhamma). At the same time, one who emphasizes
    *[samādhi]{lang="sa"}* is said to have "freedom of heart" in
    contrast with one who emphasizes wisdom, who has "freedom by
    wisdom". One who has consummate mastery of both
    *[samādhi]{lang="sa"}* and wisdom is said to be "freed both ways".

[^241]: The longest of all early discourses, this dramatic and moving
    narrative tells the story of the Buddha's slow journey towards his
    final passing.

[^242]: After the events of [DN
    2](https://suttacentral.net/dn2/en/sujato), [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"}
    retained and consolidated his power. Magadha ultimately conquered
    the Vajji Federation and continued to expand until almost all of
    India was under its sway.

[^243]: According to the commentary, [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"}'s anger was
    rooted in a dispute on trade routes. Control of shipping on the
    Ganges was essential for establishing international trade. There was
    a port on the Ganges extending over a league, split half and half
    between Magadha and Vajji. Valuable products were sourced from a
    mountain and brought down for trade, but the Vajjis kept absconding
    with the whole lot. The place is not identified, but Munger, a
    strategic port east of Patna, fits the description. The nearby hills
    have been mined since paleolithic times. It is also likely, as
    maintained in Jain tradition, that the Vajjis disputed
    [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"}'s accession after committing regicide. His
    threatening posture towards the Vajjis is also mentioned in [SN
    20.8:2.2](https://suttacentral.net/sn20.8/en/sujato#2.2).

[^244]: [Vassakāra]{lang="sa"} appears in the suttas as a devoted
    follower of the Buddha.

[^245]: The Vajji Federation harks back to an early settlement founded
    by the legendary [Nābhānediṣṭha]{lang="sa"} in the Vedic period. It
    built its wealth on its extensive fertile plains and the trading
    possibilities opened up by the Gandak and Ganges rivers.

[^246]: While it may seem strange to consult the Buddha on such a
    violent plan, [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"} knows from his experience in
    DN 2 that the Buddha will not hesitate to tell him the truth, even
    if it is bad news. It seems he is trying to avoid the downfall of
    tyrants who are surrounded only with yes men.

[^247]: Reading *[yojapetvā]{lang="sa"}* ("had them harnessed"), which
    is found in the [Mahāsaṅgīti]{lang="sa"} in similar passages, and in
    this passage in the PTS edition.

[^248]: Ānanda cares for the Buddha in his old age. Shortly after these
    events, Ānanda was entrusted with reciting the suttas at the First
    Council. This discourse would have been composed by him in the years
    following his Master's death. More than a simple interlocutor,
    Ānanda shapes the story as its second lead, a relatable character
    with an empathetic point of view. He imbues the discourse with his
    emotional struggles as he deals with the Buddha's passing and helps
    ensure the future survival of the Dhamma, while still developing his
    own meditation practice.

[^249]: As for example at [AN
    8.12:1.2](https://suttacentral.net/an8.12/en/sujato#1.2).

[^250]: Harmony is especially important as the Vajji Federation was
    comprised of several different clans, among them the Licchavis of
    [Vesālī]{lang="sa"}, the [Ñātikas]{lang="sa"} just south of
    [Vesālī]{lang="sa"}, the Uggas of [Hatthigāma]{lang="sa"} (Elephant
    Village), and the Bhogas of Bhoganagara. Some sources say the Vajjis
    proper were another tribe within the alliance. The Videhans of
    [Mithilā]{lang="sa"} are also sometimes included, but several
    sources indicate that they remained an independent, if reduced,
    kingdom until they were conquered by Magadha some years later. The
    Mallas of [Pāvā]{lang="sa"} and [Kusinārā]{lang="sa"} formed a
    closely allied independent republic ([MN
    35:12.8](https://suttacentral.net/mn35/en/sujato#12.8)).

[^251]: The "ancient Vajjian traditions" (*[porāṇe]{lang="sa"}
    vajjidhamme*) would have been established with the founding of the
    Vajji Federation some centuries earlier, which ensured that the
    members of the federation would have a voice in the
    Licchavi-dominated union. Compare with the "ancient traditions of
    the brahmins" (*[porāṇā]{lang="sa"} [brāhmaṇadhammā]{lang="sa"}* at
    [AN 5.191](https://suttacentral.net/an5.191/en/sujato) and [Snp
    2.7](https://suttacentral.net/snp2.7/en/sujato)), the falling away
    from which is said to be the cause of Brahmanical decline.
    Similarly, the Buddha elsewhere scolds the Vajjis for their
    indulgence ([AN 5.143](https://suttacentral.net/an5.143/en/sujato)),
    suggesting that the decline may have already set in.

[^252]: This advocates for legal protection for women from sexual
    violence. \| For various legal regulations regarding women and sex,
    see [Kauṭilya]{lang="sa"}'s [Arthaśāstra]{lang="sa"} 12.

[^253]: The Buddha will stay at several of these shrines later in this
    discourse. They were sacred groves, maintained by the people in
    reverence for the powerful spirits of nature. \| "Spirit-offerings"
    is *bali*. \| The commentary explains "inner or outer" as inside or
    outside the town.

[^254]: It is an old Indian belief that the presence of holy persons
    provides a kind of umbrella effect that protects the realm.

[^255]: This event is recorded at [AN
    7.21](https://suttacentral.net/an7.21/en/sujato), where the Buddha
    addresses the Licchavis. That chapter of the [Aṅguttara]{lang="sa"}
    mostly consists of the teachings found here presented as separate
    suttas. \| The [Sārandada]{lang="sa"} shrine was short walk from
    [Vesālī]{lang="sa"} ([AN
    5.143](https://suttacentral.net/an5.143/en/sujato)).

[^256]: There is a certain tension here: the Buddha taught these
    principles to the Vajjis, one of which is that the Vajjis should not
    adopt new decrees. It seems that a "decree"
    (*[paññatta]{lang="sa"}*) is more like constitutional law, whereas a
    "principle" (*dhamma*) is more like a behavioral guideline.

[^257]: *[Upalāpana]{lang="sa"}* is used a number of times in the
    Vinaya, where it always has the sense of giving someone something to
    get them to do what you want. It has the same sense at [SN
    3.25:4.13](https://suttacentral.net/sn3.25/en/sujato#4.13), where an
    enemy king may be bribed with gold from the royal treasury. \| The
    commentary says that [Vassakāra]{lang="sa"} himself was assigned
    with the task of weakening the Vajjis in this way.

[^258]: There were several monasteries and hermitages around
    [Rājagaha]{lang="sa"} ([Kd
    2:11.1.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd2/en/sujato#11.1.1)).

[^259]: Also at [AN 7.23](https://suttacentral.net/an7.23/en/sujato).

[^260]: This especially refers to the fortnightly *uposatha*. Despite
    the dangers faced in the rugged hills around [Rājagaha]{lang="sa"}
    ([Kd
    2:12.1.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd2/en/sujato#12.1.1)),
    the Buddha insisted that all monks of the locality attended the
    *uposatha* ([Kd
    2:5.3.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd2/en/sujato#5.3.1)).

[^261]: The Buddha encouraged the mendicants to recite the Dhamma in
    harmony ([MN
    103:3.2](https://suttacentral.net/mn103/en/sujato#3.2)), to resolve
    issues in harmony ([MN
    104:14.3](https://suttacentral.net/mn104/en/sujato#14.3)), and on
    the *uposatha* to recite and train in the *[pātimokkha]{lang="sa"}*
    in harmony ([Bu As
    7:4.11](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-as7/en/sujato#4.11)).

[^262]: Picking up from the similar injunction to the Vajjis, this kicks
    off a long narrative arc that binds together the Buddha's invitation
    to abolish the minor rules ([DN
    16:6.3.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#6.3.1)) with the
    bad monk Subhadda's seizing on the Buddha's death as an excuse to
    give up the rules, which was the direct motivation for the First
    Council ([DN
    16:6.20.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#6.20.3) = [Kd
    21:1.1.24](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd21/en/sujato#1.1.24)),
    at the end of which the Sangha agreed not to abolish any rules ([Kd
    21:1.9.20](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd21/en/sujato#1.9.20)),
    a decision that was affirmed a century later at the Second Council
    ([Kd 22](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd22/en/sujato)).

[^263]: The nature of seniority in the [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"} is often
    misunderstood. There is no "hierarchy" (literally "rule of priests")
    in the sense of power-based relationships: no monastic has the
    authority to command another monk or nun. Seniority is owed respect,
    not obedience.

[^264]: This stands in place of the injunction against sexual violence,
    both principles being concerned with the harmful effects of desire.

[^265]: This stands in place of the injunction to maintain the shrines,
    where mendicants would frequently stay.

[^266]: This stands in place of the injunction to look after arahants.

[^267]: Also at [AN 7.24](https://suttacentral.net/an7.24/en/sujato).

[^268]: "Work" is *kamma*, which especially means "building work". Of
    course it is essential to do work, but one should not get too caught
    up in it. The Buddha did not believe that working hard was ennobling
    in and of itself.

[^269]: "Corrupt wishes" (*[pāpicchā]{lang="sa"}*) is defined as when a
    faithless person wishes to be known as faithful, or person otherwise
    lacking good qualities wishes to be known as having them ([AN
    10.23:6.2](https://suttacentral.net/an10.23/en/sujato#6.2)).

[^270]: This item and the preceding two were the downfall of Devadatta
    ([Iti 89](https://suttacentral.net/iti89/en/sujato)).

[^271]: Also at [AN 7.25](https://suttacentral.net/an7.25/en/sujato).

[^272]: Also at [AN 7.26](https://suttacentral.net/an7.26/en/sujato).

[^273]: As at [AN 7.27](https://suttacentral.net/an7.27/en/sujato).
    These "perceptions" are all meditation practices which are described
    at [AN 10.60](https://suttacentral.net/an10.60/en/sujato).

[^274]: These six principles are found in several places, but they are
    not elsewhere called "principles that prevent decline"; for example
    at [AN 6.11](https://suttacentral.net/an6.11/en/sujato) they are
    called "warm-hearted qualities" (*[dhammā]{lang="sa"}
    [sāraṇīyā]{lang="sa"}*). At [AN
    6.22](https://suttacentral.net/an6.22/en/sujato), however, a
    different six qualities are called "principles that prevent
    decline": not relishing work, talk, sleep, and company, being easy
    to admonish, and having good friends.

[^275]: This is the right view of the noble eightfold path, in other
    words, the four noble truths.

[^276]: This epitome of the Dhamma is repeated eight times in this
    discourse, summarizing the teachings in the Gradual Training. It is
    not found in this exact form elsewhere.

[^277]: "Imbued" (*[paribhāvita]{lang="sa"}*) as a mother hen imbues her
    eggs with warmth by sitting on them ([AN
    7.71:2.2](https://suttacentral.net/an7.71/en/sujato#2.2)). See also
    [SN 55.21:2.3](https://suttacentral.net/sn55.21/en/sujato#2.3).

[^278]: Some editions add *[diṭṭhāsava]{lang="sa"}* ("defilement of
    views"), but since that appears to be a late interpolation I follow
    the [Mahāsaṅgīti]{lang="sa"} in omitting it.

[^279]: This is the same rest-house at which the Buddha sojourned on his
    way from [Rājagaha]{lang="sa"} to [Nāḷandā]{lang="sa"} in the
    [Brahmajālasutta]{lang="sa"} ([DN
    1](https://suttacentral.net/dn1/en/sujato)).

[^280]: This was apparently the last time that the Buddha met
    [Sāriputta]{lang="sa"}. This encounter is recorded in an independent
    sutta at [SN 47.12](https://suttacentral.net/sn47.12/en/sujato) and
    expanded into a long discourse at [DN
    28](https://suttacentral.net/dn28/en/sujato). The
    [Saṁyutta]{lang="sa"} follows this meeting with the record of
    [Sāriputta]{lang="sa"}'s passing at [SN
    47.13](https://suttacentral.net/sn47.13/en/sujato), and then the
    lament for the absence of both [Sāriputta]{lang="sa"} and
    [Moggallāna]{lang="sa"} at [SN 47.14
    ](https://suttacentral.net/sn47.14 /en/sujato). For some reason
    these events were omitted from the present discourse, even though
    they fit well thematically.

[^281]: As at [DN
    14:1.37.4](https://suttacentral.net/dn14/en/sujato#1.37.4),
    inference (*anvaya*) is regarded as a valid form of knowledge. The
    Buddha himself is said to rely on inference using the same simile at
    [AN 10.95:10.4](https://suttacentral.net/an10.95/en/sujato#10.4).

[^282]: This is modern Patna. In this account we see how it was
    developed from the simple riverside village of
    [Pāṭaligāma]{lang="sa"} to the citadel of [Pāṭaliputta]{lang="sa"}.
    [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"} ultimately moved the capital of Magadha from
    the defensively-postured [Rājagaha]{lang="sa"} surrounded by hills
    to this trading center on open waters. Under Ashoka it became one of
    the greatest cities of the ancient world. It was named after the
    *[pāṭalī]{lang="sa"}* tree (*Stereospermum chelonoides*), known as
    *patala* in Hindi.

[^283]: Also at [AN 5.213](https://suttacentral.net/an5.213/en/sujato),
    [Ud 8.6:4.1](https://suttacentral.net/ud8.6/en/sujato#4.1), [DN
    33:2.1.36](https://suttacentral.net/dn33/en/sujato#2.1.36), and [Kd
    1:28.4.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#28.4.1).

[^284]: The Buddha begins with the things most obvious and pertinent to
    the audience.

[^285]: Bewildered by fear and regret.

[^286]: *[Suññāgāra]{lang="sa"}* normally means an "empty dwelling", but
    here the commentary describes it as a curtained-off cubicle.

[^287]: [Vassakāra]{lang="sa"} was no dawdler, as he preceded the Buddha
    to [Pāṭaligāma]{lang="sa"}. And not long after, he returned to
    [Rājagaha]{lang="sa"} to build more fortifications there ([MN
    108:6.2](https://suttacentral.net/mn108/en/sujato#6.2)). \| *Nagara*
    here means "citadel, fortress", not "city". It has the same sense as
    *pura*. The construction is defensive and may well have begun before
    [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"} began thinking of invading.

[^288]: The relation between deities and building sites (*vatthu*)
    appears only in this passage in the early texts (repeated at [Ud
    8.6:15.2](https://suttacentral.net/ud8.6/en/sujato#15.2) and [Kd
    1:28.7.3](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#28.7.3)).
    Divination and geomancy (*[vatthuvijjā]{lang="sa"}*) for building
    sites is said to be a wrong livelihood at [DN
    1:1.21.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn1/en/sujato#1.21.2).

[^289]: From this point, later texts always refer to
    [Pāṭaliputta]{lang="sa"}, but the reason for the change of name from
    [Pāṭaligāma]{lang="sa"} is not explained in the commentary. *Putta*
    is a suffix indicating the people of a certain clan or place, so
    *[pāṭaliputta]{lang="sa"}* means "a person from [Pāṭalī]{lang="sa"}
    (village)" or as we might say, "[Pāṭalian]{lang="sa"}". As the city
    became well known and its identity as a village receded, it must
    have become known as the "city of the [Pāṭaliputtas]{lang="sa"}" and
    hence simply [Pāṭaliputta]{lang="sa"}. Compare the uses of
    *[ñātika]{lang="sa"}* below. \| The commentary explains that
    *[puṭabhedanaṁ]{lang="sa"}* refers to the "opening of packages",
    signifying that it was a center of trade. This usage is attested as
    late as the 13th century Jain [Vividhatīrthakalpa]{lang="sa"} of
    [Jinaprabhasūri]{lang="sa"}.

[^290]: Excavations reveal that [Pāṭaliputta]{lang="sa"} was constructed
    of wood, so the danger of fire was real. It is situated on a
    low-lying flood plain of the Ganges, so flooding would also have
    been an obvious danger. As for dissension (*mithubheda*),
    [Vassakāra]{lang="sa"} had indicated that this was a means of
    bringing down the Vajjis, so it would seem that kamma was lying in
    wait for them.

[^291]: This is the *[anumodanā]{lang="sa"}* recited for the meal
    offering. Other examples are found at [Snp
    3.7:34.3](https://suttacentral.net/snp3.7/en/sujato#34.3) = [MN
    92:25.6](https://suttacentral.net/mn92/en/sujato#25.6), [SN
    55.26:20.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn55.26/en/sujato#20.1), [Kd
    1:15.14.4](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#15.14.4),
    and [Kd
    1:1.5.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#1.5.1). \| It
    is worth noting that no early *[anomodanā]{lang="sa"}* uses the
    imperative verb form *-tu* signifying giving a blessing (eg.
    *bhavatu [sabbamaṅgalaṁ]{lang="sa"}*, "may all blessings be"). They
    strictly use the indicative *-ti* to teach cause and effect: if you
    do this, that happens.

[^292]: Buddhism promotes good neighborliness with all beings, seen and
    unseen. Making an offering to the local spirits helps create a
    positive and healthy sense of place.

[^293]: At [AN 4.67:8.3](https://suttacentral.net/an4.67/en/sujato#8.3)
    a similar sentiment is expressed for animals.

[^294]: The "ford" (*tittha*) is symbolic; those who forge a path to
    salvation are called *titthakara*, such as the six leading ascetics
    of [DN 2](https://suttacentral.net/dn2/en/sujato). Today there is a
    5.7 km bridge at this crossing, which is appropriately called the
    Mahatma Gandhi Setu.

[^295]: "Dinghy" translates *[uḷumpa]{lang="sa"}*.

[^296]: In this stock phrase, we sometimes find
    *[paccuṭṭhāsi]{lang="sa"}* ("landed") and sometimes
    *[pāturahosi]{lang="sa"}* ("reappeared").

[^297]: *[Aṇṇavaṁ]{lang="sa"}* cannot mean "sea" here, since it is to be
    crossed with a bridge.

[^298]: [Koṭigāma]{lang="sa"} lay a short distance from the Ganges. The
    Buddha's stay here must therefore have followed the events of [SN
    47.14](https://suttacentral.net/sn47.14/en/sujato) at
    [Ukkacelā]{lang="sa"} on the Vajjian bank of the Ganges, where he
    laments the passing of [Sāriputta]{lang="sa"} and
    [Moggallāna]{lang="sa"}. He would have heard of
    [Sāriputta]{lang="sa"}'s passing before then, while on the road from
    [Nāḷandā]{lang="sa"} to [Pāṭaligāma]{lang="sa"}, even though that is
    implausibly said to have happened at [Sāvatthī]{lang="sa"} ([SN
    47.13](https://suttacentral.net/sn47.13/en/sujato)).

[^299]: Also at [SN 56.21](https://suttacentral.net/sn56.21/en/sujato).

[^300]: [Ñātika]{lang="sa"} (also spelled *[nātika]{lang="sa"}*, or
    *[nādika]{lang="sa"}*; Sanskrit *[jñātṛka]{lang="sa"}*;
    [Prākrit]{lang="sa"} *[nāyika]{lang="sa"}*) was the clan to which
    the Jain leader [Mahāvīra]{lang="sa"} (called
    [Nāṭaputta]{lang="sa"}, i.e. [Ñātiputta]{lang="sa"}) belonged. Here
    it is unusually spelled in plural, which means "the land of the
    [Ñātika]{lang="sa"} clan".

[^301]: Here [Ñātika]{lang="sa"} is in singular and appears to be the
    name of a town. Thus [Ñātika]{lang="sa"} is first of all a name of a
    clan, then the land they live in, then their chief town. \| Over a
    millennium previously, the Indus Valley Civilization had built
    cities of fired brick with standardized size and construction
    methods, but in the Buddha's day most buildings were wood. This is
    the only brick building mentioned in the suttas; brick construction
    is also discussed in the Vinaya.

[^302]: It is unprecedented to discuss the spiritual destinies of an
    entire town like this. Normally this question is only asked when
    specific individuals known to the Buddha have passed away. Likely
    the passage was created in the wake of the passing of
    [Mahāvīra]{lang="sa"} to show that even his own people were
    enthusiastic Buddhists. This portion of the text is also found at
    [SN 55.8](https://suttacentral.net/sn55.8/en/sujato).

[^303]: [SN 55.9](https://suttacentral.net/sn55.9/en/sujato) inserts
    another series on a monk, nun, layman, and laywoman all called
    Asoka.

[^304]: This portion of the text is at [SN
    55.10](https://suttacentral.net/sn55.10/en/sujato). I spell names
    consistently with there.

[^305]: The suttas consistently depict lay folk as attaining the third
    stage of awakening, non-return, but not the highest stage of
    arahantship, except in rare cases on the deathbed. The tradition
    extended this by saying that if a lay person becomes an arahant,
    they must ordain or die that day, but this is not supported in the
    early texts. The point is not whether you receive an ordination, but
    whether you let go of attachments.

[^306]: The commentary to the [Saṁyutta]{lang="sa"} attributes the large
    number of deaths to a recent plague (*[ahivātaroga]{lang="sa"}*,
    "viper's breath disease"). This deadly contagion was known to kill
    off entire families ([Kd
    1:50.1.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#50.1.1)).
    According to the commentaries, the same disease once afflicted
    [Vesālī]{lang="sa"}, prompting the teaching of the Ratanasutta ([Snp
    2.1](https://suttacentral.net/snp2.1/en/sujato), [Kp
    6](https://suttacentral.net/kp6/en/sujato)).

[^307]: Mirrors are for doing makeup ([DN
    1:1.16.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn1/en/sujato#1.16.2)) or
    admiring oneself ([MN
    77:33.18](https://suttacentral.net/mn77/en/sujato#33.18)), which a
    mendicant should not do ([Kd
    15:2.4.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd15/en/sujato#2.4.1)). In
    the Dhamma a mirror is for self-reflection ([MN
    61:8.2](https://suttacentral.net/mn61/en/sujato#8.2)), while the
    mind is purified like polishing a mirror ([AN
    3.70:13.4](https://suttacentral.net/an3.70/en/sujato#13.4)).

[^308]: The four principles that follow are elsewhere identified as four
    factors of stream-entry ([AN
    9.27](https://suttacentral.net/an9.27/en/sujato)). At [SN
    12.41](https://suttacentral.net/sn12.41/en/sujato), understanding of
    dependent origination is included.

[^309]: "Experiential" is *avecca*, literally "having undergone".
    "Experiential confidence" is the faith of a stream-enterer, who has
    seen for themselves.

[^310]: They have experiential confidence in the Buddha as a teacher
    because they have followed his path and realized the results that he
    speaks of.

[^311]: A stream-enterer has direct experience of the four noble truths,
    so they have confirmed that the teaching is indeed realizable in
    this very life.

[^312]: The suttas distinguish between two senses of
    [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"}. The "mendicant [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"}"
    (*[bhikkhusaṅgha]{lang="sa"}*) is the conventional community of
    monks and nuns. The "[Saṅgha]{lang="sa"} of disciples"
    (*[sāvakasaṅgha]{lang="sa"}*) is classified as fourfold according to
    the stages of awakening: stream-entry, once-return, non-return, and
    perfection. Each of these stages is further subdivided into those of
    the path who are practicing for realization and those of the fruit
    who have realized. These are referred to as "noble disciples", four
    of the path and four of the fruit, making eight individuals in
    total.

[^313]: This entails keeping the five precepts at a minimum.

[^314]: [Ambapālī]{lang="sa"} means "protector of mangoes". Here the
    text just says she had a "grove", but it is identified as a mango
    grove below ([DN
    16:2.14.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#2.14.1)).

[^315]: In some versions, the Buddha is said to have taught the monks
    mindfulness in anticipation of the arrival of the beautiful
    courtesan.

[^316]: In the Gradual Training the mendicant "establishes mindfulness"
    to begin meditation. Here this practice is expanded, and in [DN
    22](https://suttacentral.net/dn22/en/sujato) it is expanded even
    further.

[^317]: The locative idiom *[kāye]{lang="sa"} [kāyānupassī]{lang="sa"}*
    indicates that the meditator focuses on a particular aspect of body
    contemplation, such as observing the breath, awareness of the body
    parts, or reflection on the four elements. \| "Rid of desire and
    aversion for the world" refers to clearing the mind through the
    previous practice of sense restraint.

[^318]: *[Dhammā]{lang="sa"}* here refers to the "principles" of cause
    and effect and the four noble truths which are understood by
    reflecting on the psychology of meditation itself. In this context
    *[dhammā]{lang="sa"}* does not mean "mental objects" or "phenomena"
    or "mental qualities".

[^319]: A *[gaṇikā]{lang="sa"}* was a trained entertainer and escort
    whose company commanded a high fee ([Kd
    8:1.1.7](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd8/en/sujato#1.1.7)). It
    was a respected position, as we see the city guild of
    [Rājagaha]{lang="sa"}, with King [Bimbisāra]{lang="sa"}'s blessing,
    appoint the young woman [Sālavatī]{lang="sa"} in a similar position
    ([Kd
    8:1.2.13](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd8/en/sujato#1.2.13)).

[^320]: The text spends more time on the Vajjis' appearance than on
    [Ambapālī]{lang="sa"}'s. At [Thig
    15.1:12.1](https://suttacentral.net/thig15.1/en/sujato#12.1) the nun
    [Isidāsī]{lang="sa"} recalls how she used to do the makeup for her
    husband every day, like his own personal beautician.

[^321]: There are various reading for *[paṭivaṭṭesi]{lang="sa"}*. The
    commentary glosses with *pahari* ("struck, collided").

[^322]: The derogatory indeclinable vocative *je* is otherwise used of
    the "naughty maid" [Kāḷī]{lang="sa"} at [MN
    21:9.13](https://suttacentral.net/mn21/en/sujato#9.13). "Wench" is a
    term meant to insult on the basis of servitude or promiscuity, so it
    seems appropriate. The Licchavis are not distinguishing themselves
    here; given their youth, wealth, licentiousness, and evident
    familiarity with [Ambapālī]{lang="sa"}, it seems safe to conclude
    that they had been her clients.

[^323]: *Dassatha* is second future plural of *[dadāti]{lang="sa"}*. The
    Vinaya parallel at [Kd
    6:30.4.5](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd6/en/sujato#30.4.5) uses
    optative forms instead. \| "With provinces" is
    *[sāhāraṁ]{lang="sa"}* (literally "with food", commentary:
    *[sajanapadaṁ]{lang="sa"}*) refers to the holdings around the city
    that supplied food and other necessities.

[^324]: Despite the context, *[ambakā]{lang="sa"}* has no etymological
    relation to *amba* ("mango") but is a term for "woman, mother", used
    here as a play on words.

[^325]: This demonstrates that a woman of [Ambapālī]{lang="sa"}'s
    profession could own land and control significant resources.

[^326]: An *[ārāma]{lang="sa"}* is a tended and pleasant ground, a
    "park". Here it has previously been referred to as "grove" (*vana*)
    and "mango grove". Usually in the suttas, however, it is a name for
    a place where monastics live. In English, monastics don't live in
    parks, they live in monasteries. So once a place has been dedicated
    for the [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"}, I translate *[ārāma]{lang="sa"}* as
    "monastery".

[^327]: Pali texts waver between *beluva* ("wood-apple") and
    *[veḷuva]{lang="sa"}* (from *[veḷuvant]{lang="sa"}*, "full of
    bamboo").

[^328]: The Buddha was travelling with a large retinue, which could
    become burdensome on a small village if they were to stay the three
    months of the rains residence. The texts note several monasteries
    and places to stay around [Vesālī]{lang="sa"}.

[^329]: Here *[upaṭṭhāka]{lang="sa"}* refers to the lay devotees. \|
    *Apaloketi* ("take leave") also has a literal sense of "glance
    back"; both senses are found in the sutta (see [DN
    16:4.1.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#4.1.2)).

[^330]: "Life force" is *[jīvitasaṅkhāra]{lang="sa"}* whereas below we
    find *[āyusaṅkhāra]{lang="sa"}* ([DN
    16:3.10.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#3.10.1)). They
    are evidently synonyms; [Ps
    1.6:4.3](https://suttacentral.net/ps1.6/en/sujato#4.3) refers to the
    three *[jīvitasaṅkhāras]{lang="sa"}* from [MN
    43:24.2](https://suttacentral.net/mn43/en/sujato#24.2), but there
    the text has *[āyusaṅkhāra]{lang="sa"}*. *[Saṅkhāra]{lang="sa"}*
    could be interpreted here either as "volition" ("will to live") or
    as "life force" (i.e. the vital energy that sustains life).
    Elsewhere, however, *[āyusaṅkhāra]{lang="sa"}* clearly means "life
    force" ([SN
    20.6:3.2](https://suttacentral.net/sn20.6/en/sujato#3.2), [MN
    43:23.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn43/en/sujato#23.1)).

[^331]: This is the first indication of Ānanda's fragile emotional state
    in the days to come. \| Read *[diṭṭhā]{lang="sa"}* (= Sanskrit
    *[diṣṭyā]{lang="sa"}*), "fantastic, how fortunate", per [DN
    26:21.9](https://suttacentral.net/dn26/en/sujato#21.9) and [Ja
    81:1.4](https://suttacentral.net/ja81/en/sujato#1.4).

[^332]: Previously the Buddha had spoken of "taking leave" of the
    [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"}, but here something more specific is meant.
    *[Udāharati]{lang="sa"}* is typically used when a
    previously-mentioned matter (often unpleasant) is "brought up", as
    at the monastic procedures for confession or invitation to admonish
    ([Kd
    1:16.2.5](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#16.2.5), [Kd
    1:2.1.9](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#2.1.9)).
    Ānanda is implying that there is some unfinished disciplinary
    business that needs attention. Despite the Buddha's response, he did
    in fact go on to make a number of statements and rulings.

[^333]: For forty-five years he had been teaching and supporting the
    community.

[^334]: A principle not followed by some contemporary Buddhist schools
    that harbor "secret teachings".

[^335]: "Closed fist of a teacher" is *[ācariyamuṭṭhi]{lang="sa"}*.

[^336]: *[Pariharissāmi]{lang="sa"}* needs careful parsing with regard
    to tenses. The general meaning is to "carry about" or "maintain,
    nurture". The Buddha elsewhere said that he "leads" the
    [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"} ([DN
    26:25.6](https://suttacentral.net/dn26/en/sujato#25.6):
    *[pariharāmi]{lang="sa"}*). When the Buddha was about to go on
    retreat, [Moggallāna]{lang="sa"} said that he and
    [Sāriputta]{lang="sa"} "shall lead" (*[pariharissāmi]{lang="sa"}*),
    to which the Buddha said they "should lead" ([MN
    67:13.7](https://suttacentral.net/mn67/en/sujato#13.7):
    *[parihareyyaṁ]{lang="sa"}*). Devadatta, on the other hand, ensured
    his downfall when he determined that he "shall lead" the
    [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"}, having taken over from the Buddha ([AN
    5.100:2.3](https://suttacentral.net/an5.100/en/sujato#2.3), [Kd
    1:2.1.23](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#2.1.23)).
    Thus the future tense indicates the determination to lead. But at
    this point, the Buddha's mind is already set on letting go. He is
    not saying that no-one should lead the [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"}, but that
    it is up to whoever leads it to address the issues.

[^337]: The reading and derivation of *[veṭhamissakena]{lang="sa"}* are
    unclear. As a form of violence we find *[veṭhamissena]{lang="sa"}*
    at [Thag 2.12:1.1](https://suttacentral.net/thag2.12/en/sujato#1.1),
    notably also in instrumental. *[Veṭha]{lang="sa"}* means "twist,
    strap, turban". *Missa* means "mixed" or "plaited" ([Bu Ss
    2:2.1.21](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-ss2/en/sujato#2.1.21)).
    Thus it probably refers to a kind of strong twisted material used to
    tie or bind, i.e. rope.

[^338]: Compare Rig Veda 8.48.5: "As leather binds a chariot, soma knits
    my joints together." [Bṛhadāraṇyaka]{lang="sa"}
    [Upaniṣad]{lang="sa"} 4.3.35 compares the labored breathing of one
    near death with the creaking of a heavily-laden cart.

[^339]: The suttas say little about this signless (*animitta*)
    meditation. Its defining characteristic is that consciousness does
    not "follow after signs" (*[nimittānusāri]{lang="sa"}
    [viññāṇaṁ]{lang="sa"}*, eg. [AN
    6.13:5.3](https://suttacentral.net/an6.13/en/sujato#5.3)). This is
    explained in [MN
    138:10.2](https://suttacentral.net/mn138/en/sujato#10.2) as not
    being distracted or affected by the features of sense impressions,
    as the "signs" are created by greed, hate, and delusion ([MN
    43:37.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn43/en/sujato#37.1)). The mental
    unification (*[ekattaṁ]{lang="sa"}*) based on this practice is
    listed after the form (and formless) *[jhānas]{lang="sa"}*, so it is
    very advanced. It is nonetheless a conditioned state ([MN
    121:11.4](https://suttacentral.net/mn121/en/sujato#11.4)), so it is
    possible that a mendicant might fall from it and disrobe ([AN
    6.60:8.10](https://suttacentral.net/an6.60/en/sujato#8.10)). However
    it may also be used to describe the meditation of an arahant ([SN
    41.7:6.12](https://suttacentral.net/sn41.7/en/sujato#6.12)). It is
    clear from [MN
    121:10.5](https://suttacentral.net/mn121/en/sujato#10.5) that the
    six senses are still functioning, unlike in deep serenity
    meditations. It seems that in such a state, the Buddha was able to
    function normally while seeing through the pain in his body.

[^340]: This central theme of the Buddha's teaching---that each of us is
    responsible for our own salvation---becomes even more important as
    the Buddha's days grow short.

[^341]: Read *tama(t)agge* with *tama* as superlative, literally "at the
    peak of the best".

[^342]: This detail is often mentioned in texts of the
    [Sarvāstivāda]{lang="sa"} school, but rarely in Pali.

[^343]: Normally *kappa* as a period of time means "eon", but the late
    canonical texts [Kathāvatthu]{lang="sa"} ([Kv
    11.5](https://suttacentral.net/kv11.5)) and
    [Milindapañha]{lang="sa"} ([Mil
    5.1.10](https://suttacentral.net/mil5.1.10)) argue that it means the
    "lifespan", an interpretation followed by the commentaries. Support
    for this comes from [DN
    26:28.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn26/en/sujato#28.3), which says
    that "long life" for a mendicant is the four bases of psychic power
    which enable you to remain for the *kappa*. Here it is surely
    talking about a full lifespan. Underlying this is the idea that in
    different epochs the "proper lifespan" varies; in the Buddha's day
    it was one hundred years ([DN
    14:1.7.7](https://suttacentral.net/dn14/en/sujato#1.7.7)). We can
    resolve the problem if we read *kappa* here, not as a period of
    time, but as "proper, fitting", i.e. the "proper" lifespan of a
    hundred years.

[^344]: Here Ānanda's mind is "as if" possessed (*[yathā]{lang="sa"}*),
    but in the Vinaya *[yathā]{lang="sa"}* is missing: he is possessed
    ([Kd
    1:1.10.17](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#1.10.17)).
    Remember, Ānanda is telling this story about himself.

[^345]: [Māra]{lang="sa"} is the Buddhist deity of death, sex, and
    delusion; his aim is to trap beings in transmigration. He appears in
    many guises, both real and metaphorical, throughout the canon, but
    this sequence is his only direct appearance in the
    [Dīghanikāya]{lang="sa"}. Here he feigns compassion, urging the
    Buddha to find his final peace; but he has an ulterior motive, for
    with the Buddha's passing [Māra]{lang="sa"}'s job will be much
    easier.

[^346]: *[Parinibbāna]{lang="sa"}* means "extinguishment", as of a
    flame. Here it appears as both noun ("extinguishment") and verb
    ("become extinguished"). It is not a particularly difficult term to
    translate. Linguistically it has nothing to do with "attachment", so
    renderings such as "unbinding" are untenable. It should be
    translated rather than just keeping "[Nibbāna]{lang="sa"}", not
    least because English resists verbifying adopted words, resulting in
    such constructions as "enter [Nibbāna]{lang="sa"}", which reifies it
    in a way that the Pali does not.

[^347]: [Māra]{lang="sa"} is citing the Buddha's words for his own
    purpose. The Pali tradition does not say when this encounter took
    place, but the Sanskrit [Sarvāstivāda]{lang="sa"}
    [Catuṣparisatsūtra]{lang="sa"} places it shortly after the Buddha's
    awakening.

[^348]: This passage makes it clear that it was the Buddha's intention
    from the beginning to establish an order of nuns
    (*[bhikkhunī]{lang="sa"}*).

[^349]: This passage is the narrative inverse of the occasion when
    [Brahmā]{lang="sa"} begged the Buddha to teach ([SN
    6.1:5.5](https://suttacentral.net/sn6.1/en/sujato#5.5), etc.).
    Throughout, the sutta artfully preserves a degree of narrative
    ambiguity. Here it almost appears as if the Buddha assents to
    passing away because of [Māra]{lang="sa"}'s request, although his
    intent was clear earlier. [Māra]{lang="sa"}'s reasoning, moreover,
    is based on the Buddha's own words.

[^350]: It seems that at this point, the Buddha is still spending the
    rains in Beluva village, from where he would sometimes go to nearby
    [Vesālī]{lang="sa"} for alms, or to a local shrine for meditation.
    He left [Vesālī]{lang="sa"} only after holding a meeting for all the
    [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"}, which probably signified the completion of the
    rains ([DN
    16:4.1.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#4.1.2)). If this
    reasoning is correct---and the text is not explicit---then he made
    this statement during the rains retreat. If, however, this reasoning
    is incorrect and we are already after the rains, it could not have
    been long after. Thus it is probably September/October, meaning that
    the final extinguishment was to take place in December/January. This
    conflicts with the tradition of ascribing his final extinguishment
    to the full moon of Vesak, in May.

[^351]: The [Cāpāla]{lang="sa"} shrine is unknown outside of this
    context. The [Udāna]{lang="sa"} commentary says that it was named
    after the *yakkha* who formerly lived there. Now,
    *[cāpalla]{lang="sa"}* means "fickleness, propensity to tremble" and
    is from the same root as earth-"quake" (*[bhumicāla]{lang="sa"}*;
    *capala* = *pacala* via metathesis). It is no great leap to
    *[cāpāla]{lang="sa"}*. If this is correct, it suggests that the
    shrine was dedicated to an earth spirit who commanded earthquakes; a
    fitting setting for the events to follow.

[^352]: Northern India lies in the shadow of the seismically active
    Alpide Belt, so the occurrence of earthquakes is realistic even if
    the causes are not scientific.

[^353]: For this difficult verse, I generally follow Bhikkhu Bodhi's
    long discussion in *Connected Discourses of the Buddha*, note 255 on
    the [Mahāvagga]{lang="sa"}. \| *Sambhava* means "production,
    creation", and here I think it refers to the prolonging of life
    which the Buddha had just rejected.

[^354]: *[Bhavasaṅkhāra]{lang="sa"}* ("life force") here is equivalent
    to *[āyusaṅkhāra]{lang="sa"}* and *[jīvitasaṅkhāra]{lang="sa"}*.

[^355]: *Attasambhava* ("self-creation") refers back to *sambhava* in
    the first line.

[^356]: So far as it goes, this is a naturalistic explanation. The
    "water element" has the quality of softening, while the "air
    element" is traditionally understood as "movement". Thus in modern
    terms this means, "When underground forces disturb a region of
    instability."

[^357]: The might of ascetics was legendary in ancient India (eg. [MN
    56:14.2](https://suttacentral.net/mn56/en/sujato#14.2)). The
    meditation described here might be compared with the "dimensions of
    mastery" below ([DN
    16:3.25.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#3.25.1)).

[^358]: At [DN
    14:1.17.7](https://suttacentral.net/dn14/en/sujato#1.17.7) and [MN
    123:7.6](https://suttacentral.net/mn123/en/sujato#7.6) it is,
    rather, the entire galaxy that trembles, perhaps indicating that the
    enhanced miracle is a later development.

[^359]: There are many accounts of the Buddha's awakening in the suttas,
    but none, so far as I know, that mention an earthquake.

[^360]: See [SN
    56.11:13.2](https://suttacentral.net/sn56.11/en/sujato#13.2).

[^361]: This refers to the Buddha's death. The Pali here uses both
    *[nibbāna]{lang="sa"}* and *[parinibbāna]{lang="sa"}*. Sometimes it
    is said that *[nibbāna]{lang="sa"}* is the attainment of
    arahantship, while *[parinibbāna]{lang="sa"}* is the death of an
    arahant, but this distinction is not consistently applied in the
    suttas. \| Regarding *[nibbānadhātuyā]{lang="sa"}* ("the element of
    extinguishment"), in Pali the case is ambiguous. It is sometimes
    translated "by means of" or "through", which assumes the
    instrumental; but the Sanskrit is *[nirvāṇadhātau]{lang="sa"}*,
    which must be locative.

[^362]: As at [AN 8.69](https://suttacentral.net/an8.69/en/sujato). The
    following series of "eight things" seems arbitrarily inserted here
    and breaks the flow of the narrative. They are absent from the
    Sanskrit [Sarvāstivāda]{lang="sa"} text edited by Waldschmidt, which
    goes directly to the conversation where Ānanda realizes that the
    Buddha is going to die.

[^363]: These "assemblies" were formal deliberative meetings, not just
    gatherings. Thus "householders" here does not mean "lay folk" in
    apposition to "ascetics"; rather it means "home owners". Some of
    these assemblies are depicted or alluded to in the
    [Dīghanikāya]{lang="sa"}. At [DN
    6:1.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn6/en/sujato#1.3), brahmins
    assemble in [Vesālī]{lang="sa"}; while at [DN
    3:1.13.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn3/en/sujato#1.13.3),
    [Ambaṭṭha]{lang="sa"} speaks of an assembly of Sakyans, who were
    aristocrats. At [DN
    18:12.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn18/en/sujato#12.1), we get a
    glimpse of the proceedings at a meeting of the gods of the
    thirty-three.

[^364]: There do not appear to be any records of this happening in the
    early texts, although that is perhaps to be expected. It does feel
    out of character for the Buddha, as normally he is very up front.
    The commentary says that this ruse was adopted so the Buddha could
    plant subtle seeds (*[vāsanā]{lang="sa"}*) for the future.

[^365]: These are another way of describing the different experiences of
    *[jhāna]{lang="sa"}*. Also at [AN
    8.65](https://suttacentral.net/an8.65/en/sujato), [AN
    10.29:6.1](https://suttacentral.net/an10.29/en/sujato#6.1), [DN
    33:3.1.142](https://suttacentral.net/dn33/en/sujato#3.1.142), [DN
    34:2.1.160](https://suttacentral.net/dn34/en/sujato#2.1.160), and
    [MN 77:23.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn77/en/sujato#23.1).

[^366]: "Perceiving form (*[rūpa]{lang="sa"}*) internally" refers to
    someone developing meditation based on an aspect of their own body,
    such as the breath or the parts of the body. The "forms"
    (*[rūpā]{lang="sa"}*) seen externally are the lights or other
    meditation phenomena that today are usually called *nimitta*. An
    "ugly" form is the mental image that arises in such contemplations
    as the parts of the body. A "beautiful" image arises from practices
    such as mindfulness of breathing.

[^367]: "Not perceiving form internally" refers to meditations such as
    the external elements, or the decaying of another's body.

[^368]: This is the meditation where one contemplates an external color,
    either a prepared disk or a natural phenomena such as the sky or a
    flower, which eventually gives rise to a "counterpart" image. Today
    such meditations are called *[kasiṇa]{lang="sa"}* following the
    Visuddhimagga, but in the early texts *[kasiṇa]{lang="sa"}* means
    "totality" and refers rather to the state of *[samādhi]{lang="sa"}*
    that results.

[^369]: Already encountered at [DN
    15:35.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn15/en/sujato#35.1).

[^370]: Note that this is the idiom that Ānanda uses when directly
    quoting the Buddha.

[^371]: "Misdeed" is *[dukkaṭaṁ]{lang="sa"}*. This term is familiar as
    the most minor class of offences in the Vinaya. Here we see an
    informal use of the term as something that has been wrongly done,
    rather than a legal violation. It is used in a similar way by
    [Mākassapa]{lang="sa"} at the First Council, who accuses Ānanda of
    several misdeeds. As a legal term, *[dukkaṭa]{lang="sa"}* is late;
    the category of offences is not found in the Vinayas of the
    [Mahāsaṅghika]{lang="sa"} group of schools, which use
    *vinayatikkrama* for a similar idea.

[^372]: The Buddha goes on to list multiple places where he hinted to
    Ānanda, but the only record of such a conversation is at the
    [Cāpāla]{lang="sa"} Shrine.

[^373]: The following group of places are all near
    [Rājagaha]{lang="sa"}, and collectively illustrate the variety of
    dwellings and environs enjoyed by the mendicants practicing there.
    \| The Gotama banyan tree shrine is mentioned only here. Gotama is a
    common name, and this shrine does not appear to have any connection
    with the Buddha.

[^374]: A cliff from which bandits were tossed as a means of execution.

[^375]: The Pali tradition says the First Council was held near the
    entrance to this cave ([Dīpavaṁsa]{lang="sa"} 4.19,
    [Mahāvaṁsa]{lang="sa"} 3.19, [Samantapāsādikā]{lang="sa"}
    [Paṭhamamahāsaṅgītikathā]{lang="sa"}). The Vinayas all agree that it
    was at [Rājagaha]{lang="sa"}, but traditions vary as to the exact
    location.

[^376]: A large open area where Jains did their penances ([MN
    14:15.2](https://suttacentral.net/mn14/en/sujato#15.2)) and the
    Buddha taught occasionally ([SN
    8.10](https://suttacentral.net/sn8.10/en/sujato)), but it is most
    famous as the place the monks Godhika ([SN
    4.23](https://suttacentral.net/sn4.23/en/sujato)) and Vakkali took
    their lives ([SN
    22.87](https://suttacentral.net/sn22.87/en/sujato)).

[^377]: Mentioned several times in the suttas as a pleasant place to
    meditate, it is notable as the site of the conversion of the
    Buddha's chief layman disciple [Anāthapiṇḍika]{lang="sa"} ([SN
    10.8](https://suttacentral.net/sn10.8/en/sujato)). But the most
    famous event there was when the Buddha moderated the monk
    [Soṇa]{lang="sa"}'s excessive striving ([AN
    6.55](https://suttacentral.net/an6.55/en/sujato)).

[^378]: The hot springs near [Rājagaha]{lang="sa"} were a popular place
    for monks to bathe, so much so that they prompted a rule ensuring
    that the monks did not monopolize the springs ([Bu Pc
    57](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-pc57/en/sujato)). They are
    still in use and just as popular as ever.

[^379]: A personal gift of King [Bimbisāra]{lang="sa"}, this was the
    first permanent monastery offered to the Buddha and his
    [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"} ([Kd
    1:22.17.3](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#22.17.3)).

[^380]: [Mahākappina]{lang="sa"} stayed here ([Kd
    1:5.3.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#5.3.1)), and
    the Buddha rested there when his foot was first injured, apparently
    by Devadatta, before [Jīvaka]{lang="sa"} offered his mango grove
    ([SN 1.38](https://suttacentral.net/sn1.38/en/sujato), [SN
    4.13](https://suttacentral.net/sn4.13/en/sujato)).

[^381]: The following group of places are all in the Vajjian country
    near [Vesālī]{lang="sa"}. Whereas the varied sites around
    [Rājagaha]{lang="sa"} reflect the rugged terrain there, at
    [Vesālī]{lang="sa"} all the sites are tree-shrines, as the
    surrounding land is flat. [DN
    24:1.11.5](https://suttacentral.net/dn24/en/sujato#1.11.5) indicates
    where they lay relative to [Vesālī]{lang="sa"}; the Udena shrine was
    to the east, and the next three to the south, west, and north
    respectively. \| *Udena* is an epithet of the "arising" sun (Rig
    Veda 1.48.7), although here it may have the sense "prosperity".

[^382]: Named after the local *yakkha*, to whom offerings would have
    been made. It could get cold enough to snow, prompting the Buddha to
    lay down a rule permitting three robes ([Kd
    1:13.2.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#13.2.1)). A
    short but galaxy-shaking discourse was once taught there ([AN
    3.125:0.3](https://suttacentral.net/an3.125/en/sujato#0.3)).

[^383]: The Pali has *amba* ("mango") and *ambaka* ("maiden") as
    variants. The commentary supports the latter, saying it was named
    for seven legendary princesses of Varanasi in the time of Buddha
    Kassapa, all of whom went on to become great disciples of our
    Buddha. I made this translation on the small island of Qimei off the
    south coast of Taiwan, where there is a shrine to the "seven
    maidens" who gave the island its name. Stories of "seven maidens"
    are widespread in myth and folklore all over the world, including
    Aboriginal Dreamtime stories. The archetype descends from the
    constellation known in English as Pleiades and in Sanskrit as
    [Kṛttikā]{lang="sa"}. Since there are six main visible stars in the
    cluster today, the stories often tell of how the youngest of the
    seven sisters was lost. In the Buddhist telling this is
    [Visākhā]{lang="sa"}, the only sister not to become an arahant in
    this life.

[^384]: This was, obviously, a fertility shrine.

[^385]: Previously mentioned as the place where the seven principles of
    non-decline were taught to the Licchavis. The meaning of
    [Sārandada]{lang="sa"} is obscure and variants profligate, but it
    might be derived from the Munda word *sara*, "funeral pyre". The
    commentary says it was taken over from an old shrine to a *yakkha*
    of that name.

[^386]: "Take back" is *[paccāvamissati]{lang="sa"}*, which has several
    variants. It occurs in [Ja
    69](https://suttacentral.net/ja69/en/sujato) and [Ja
    509](https://suttacentral.net/ja509/en/sujato), where it is the
    opposite of *vamati*, to "expel, vomit".

[^387]: This was the major [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"} residence near
    [Vesālī]{lang="sa"}. The Great Wood, according to the commentary,
    stretched as far as the Himalayas. Later tradition says that a town
    should have three woods: a "great wood" for wilderness
    (*[mahāvana]{lang="sa"}*); a "prosperity wood" for resources
    (*sirivana*); and an "ascetic wood" for spiritual practice
    (*tapovana*).

[^388]: Compare the earlier request to gather all the mendicants around
    [Rājagaha]{lang="sa"} ([DN
    16:1.6.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#1.6.2)).

[^389]: Memorization was considered an essential basis for learning. In
    similar passages, we also find the injunction to "recite" the
    teachings, thus authorizing the recitation of the Buddha's teachings
    after his death, which was later formalized in the Councils
    (*[saṅgīti]{lang="sa"}*).

[^390]: These are the sets of practices later called the 37
    *[bodhiyapakkhiyā]{lang="sa"} [dhammā]{lang="sa"}*, the "wings to
    awakening". Here they are presented as an essential summary of the
    Buddha's teachings. They form the backbone of the final book of the
    [Saṁyuttanikāya]{lang="sa"}, the Maggavagga (or
    [Mahāvagga]{lang="sa"}). It is likely that the Buddha was referring
    to the earliest recension of this text. They are found as a summary
    of the Buddha's teachings at [DN
    29:17.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn29/en/sujato#17.3), [MN
    103:3.2](https://suttacentral.net/mn103/en/sujato#3.2), [MN
    104:5.3](https://suttacentral.net/mn104/en/sujato#5.3), and [AN
    8.19:17.2](https://suttacentral.net/an8.19/en/sujato#17.2) = [Ud
    5.5:25.2](https://suttacentral.net/ud5.5/en/sujato#25.2).

[^391]: This is perhaps the briefest summary possible of the Buddha's
    teaching. The world as it is, and all the beings in it, are created
    and sustained through conditions. Since the conditions that sustain
    them are finite, the conditioned phenomena are also finite and must
    come to an end. Reflecting on this gives rise to gratitude for the
    life we have and the opportunities it grants us, and determination
    to live and practice dedicated to the realization of the
    unconditioned, Nibbana.

[^392]: "Ripe old age" is *paripakko vayo*.

[^393]: "Well-settled thoughts" is *[susamāhitasaṅkappā]{lang="sa"}*.
    Compare *[asamāhitasaṅkappo]{lang="sa"}* at [AN
    4.22:5.3](https://suttacentral.net/an4.22/en/sujato#5.3).

[^394]: *[Nāgāpalokitaṁ]{lang="sa"}* is the "elephant look". There is a
    similar Sanskrit term *[siṁhāvalokana]{lang="sa"}*, the "lion look",
    said to be the slow glance back that a lion makes as he leaves his
    kill. There is a nuance in meaning here, because while *apalokana*
    literally means to "look back", it is used in the sense to "take
    leave" before setting out on a journey.

[^395]: *[Bhaṇḍagāma]{lang="sa"}*, where *[bhaṇḍa]{lang="sa"}* means
    "wares"; it must have been a trading post. It seems the villages in
    this passage were named after their chief economic activity, so I
    translate them to highlight this pragmatic system.

[^396]: This four also found at [AN
    4.1:2.3](https://suttacentral.net/an4.1/en/sujato#2.3) and [AN
    7.66:14.4](https://suttacentral.net/an7.66/en/sujato#14.4).

[^397]: Here the text refers to the Buddha in the third person; compare
    the previous set of verses in first person. Although the text states
    that these verses were spoken by the Buddha, it is possible that, as
    with many similar cases especially in the
    [Aṅguttaranikāya]{lang="sa"}, the verses were added by redactors.

[^398]: Note the use of "quenched" (*parinibbuto*) here while the Buddha
    is still alive.

[^399]: *[Hatthigāma]{lang="sa"}*, probably a village which specialized
    in training elephants.

[^400]: *[Ambagāma]{lang="sa"}*.

[^401]: *[Jambugāma]{lang="sa"}*.

[^402]: Departing from the economic naming scheme, here *bhoga* does not
    mean "wealth". Rather, the Bhogas were one of the clans of the Vajji
    Federation.

[^403]: "Great references" is *[mahāpadesa]{lang="sa"}*; also at [AN
    4.180](https://suttacentral.net/an4.180/en/sujato). A different set
    of four *[mahāpadesas]{lang="sa"}* are [Kd
    1:40.1.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#40.1.1),
    which provide criteria by which mendicants may judge what is and is
    not allowable.

[^404]: The phrase used in the suttas when reporting a teaching heard
    directly from the Buddha, eg. [SN
    55.52:5.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn55.52/en/sujato#5.1), [SN
    22.90:9.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn22.90/en/sujato#9.1), [MN
    47:10.7](https://suttacentral.net/mn47/en/sujato#10.7), etc.

[^405]: *Dhamma*, *vinaya*, and *[satthusāsana]{lang="sa"}* are used in
    the suttas in the general sense of the entirety of the Buddha's
    teachings. In his advice to [Mahāpajāpatī]{lang="sa"} ([AN
    8.53](https://suttacentral.net/an8.53/en/sujato)) and
    [Upāli]{lang="sa"} ([AN
    7.83](https://suttacentral.net/an7.83/en/sujato)), they are said to
    be whatever conforms with letting go. A stream-enterer who is a lay
    person is grounded in them ([AN
    6.16:7.2](https://suttacentral.net/an6.16/en/sujato#7.2)). They are
    more frequent in the Vinaya, being used as a general qualifier of a
    legitimate legal procedure ([Bu Pc
    63:2.1.8](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-pc63/en/sujato#2.1.8)).
    Due to this, the fact that there are three items, and the fact that
    *dhamma* and *sutta* are different words, we should not take
    *dhamma* and *vinaya* here as equivalent to *sutta* and *vinaya*
    below. Rather, they are a general claim to authenticity and do not
    specify particular texts.

[^406]: As at [MN
    112:3.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn112/en/sujato#3.1), [DN
    29:18.4](https://suttacentral.net/dn29/en/sujato#18.4), and [AN
    4.180:2.5](https://suttacentral.net/an4.180/en/sujato#2.5).

[^407]: It is tempting to assume that *sutta* here means the
    [Suttapiṭaka]{lang="sa"} and *vinaya* means the
    [Vinayapiṭaka]{lang="sa"}, or at least an earlier version of these
    texts; but this is not supported by tradition. The late canonical
    Netti explains *sutta* as "the four noble truths" and *vinaya* as
    "the removal of greed, hate, and delusion" ([Ne
    6](https://suttacentral.net/ne6/en/sujato)). The commentaries
    discuss many interpretations; ultimately they agree with the Netti
    for *vinaya*, but say *sutta* means the entire
    [Tipiṭaka]{lang="sa"}. I think the Netti is correct: teachings
    should "fit in" with the four noble truths (like other footprints
    fit in to an elephant's footprint, [MN
    28](https://suttacentral.net/mn28/en/sujato)), and they should "be
    exhibited" in that the results of practice should be apparent
    (*[sandiṭṭhika]{lang="sa"}*). \| "Memorize"
    (*[uggahetvā]{lang="sa"}*), because in an oral tradition a text is
    not learned until it is known by heart; such claims must be taken
    seriously. \| "Fit into" is *[osāreti]{lang="sa"}* or
    *[otāreti]{lang="sa"}* per [AN
    4.180](https://suttacentral.net/an4.180/en/sujato); the two words
    have a similar meaning, "to flow down into".

[^408]: The text has *ca* ("and") rather than *[vā]{lang="sa"}* ("or"),
    which would be expected if they were to be found in one or other
    textual collection. This is urging that any teaching fits in with
    the fundamental principles of the four noble truths, and is
    effective in removing defilements.

[^409]: The Pali text appears to say that one should remember the
    *[mahāpadesa]{lang="sa"}*, whereas the Sanskrit clearly says one
    should remember the passage (*[ayaṁ]{lang="sa"}
    dharmo'[yaṁ]{lang="sa"} vinaya [idaṁ]{lang="sa"} [śāstuḥ]{lang="sa"}
    [śāsanam]{lang="sa"} iti [viditvā]{lang="sa"}
    [dhārayitavyāḥ]{lang="sa"}*). It seems as if this pattern should
    underlie the Pali too, for if the passage is false, one "should
    reject it" (*[chaḍḍeyyātha]{lang="sa"}*). In the case of a correct
    passage we should expect a parallel injunction that one "should
    remember it" (*[dhāreyyātha]{lang="sa"}*). But if this applies to
    the *[mahāpadesa]{lang="sa"}* there is no such injunction. Thus it
    seems as if the Sanskrit is correct here and the Pali corrupt. I
    restore it by reversing the order of the phrases.

[^410]: Each of the four references is a little less solid than the
    previous. Here one hasn't heard from the Buddha, but from the
    [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"}. The fact that one hears it from the
    [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"} implies a kind of formal group recitation such
    as a *[saṅgīti]{lang="sa"}* or "council".

[^411]: Now one learns not from a unified [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"}, but from
    several learned elders. This is a stock description of learned
    mendicants (eg. [AN
    3.20:6.2](https://suttacentral.net/an3.20/en/sujato#6.2)). \|
    "Inheritors of the heritage" is *[āgatāgamā]{lang="sa"}*, where
    *[āgama]{lang="sa"}* means "what has come down", namely the
    scriptural heritage. *Āgama* is a synonym for *[nikāya]{lang="sa"}*
    in the sense of "collection of scripture". \| The "outlines"
    (*[mātikā]{lang="sa"}*, literally "matrix") are the summary outlines
    of topics that served as seeds for the development of Abhidhamma.
    [DN 16:3.50.5](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#3.50.5)
    features one of the earliest of such lists, the 37 path factors that
    the Buddha "taught from his own direct knowledge". These serve as
    outline for the section on the path in the [Saṁyutta]{lang="sa"},
    from where they were adopted in various Abhidhamma texts such as the
    [Vibhaṅga]{lang="sa"}.

[^412]: Finally the testimony of a single mendicant, which is the
    weakest of all. Nonetheless, the procedure is the same.

[^413]: The parallel at [AN
    4.180:9.11](https://suttacentral.net/an4.180/en/sujato#9.11) omits
    *[dhāreyyāthā]{lang="sa"}*, which I take to be the correct reading.
    See my note on *[dhāreyyāthā]{lang="sa"}* at [DN
    16:4.8.13](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#4.8.13).

[^414]: [Pāvā]{lang="sa"}, a town of the Mallas, has more significance
    for this narrative than appears at first sight. It was, according to
    Buddhist texts, the place where [Mahāvīra]{lang="sa"} had recently
    died, plunging the Jains into chaos. (The Jains, however, say this
    was another [Pāvā]{lang="sa"}, east of [Nāḷandā]{lang="sa"}.)
    [Pāvā]{lang="sa"} became associated with especially ascetic monks:
    thirty [Pāvā]{lang="sa"} monks became awakened on hearing a
    particularly strong discourse ([SN
    15.13](https://suttacentral.net/sn15.13/en/sujato));
    [Mahākassapa]{lang="sa"} heard the news of the Buddha's passing at
    [Pāvā]{lang="sa"}; and monks from [Pāvā]{lang="sa"} allied with
    monks of "Avanti and the south" arguing for strict Vinaya in the
    Second Council ([Kd
    1:1.7.11](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#1.7.11)).

[^415]: This passage also at [Ud
    8.5](https://suttacentral.net/ud8.5/en/sujato).

[^416]: Cunda was apparently a metal-worker, which was an advanced form
    of technological craftsmanship.

[^417]: This is, according to the commentary, preserved as the other
    discourse taught to Cunda, which deals with proper asceticism ([Snp
    1.5](https://suttacentral.net/snp1.5/en/sujato)).

[^418]: The exact meaning of *[sūkaramaddava]{lang="sa"}* is unclear.
    *[Sūkara]{lang="sa"}* is "pig", but some Chinese translations
    suggest the sense "mushroom"; and it is true that there are several
    names of plants or plant dishes that begin with
    *[sūkara]{lang="sa"}* or other animals. At [Dhp
    377](https://suttacentral.net/dhp377/en/sujato), flowers are
    described as *maddava*, meaning "overripe, withering on the vine".
    It is a common practice to allow meat to sit for a while to become
    tender and "high" for extra flavor when cooked. But this can lead to
    a proliferation of dangerous bacteria unless properly cooked, and
    such seems to have been the case here. In any case, this disputed
    term is marginal in a consideration of meat-eating in early
    Buddhism, which is discussed more fully elsewhere.

[^419]: If the Buddha knew the meal was dangerous, why ask to be served
