    it? This is even more striking in light of the fact that it is a
    Vinaya offence to request fine food, including meat, and nowhere
    else is such a request recorded ([Bu Pc
    39:2.10.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-pc39/en/sujato#2.10.1)).
    Such narrative ambiguities serve to escalate a sense of wonder.

[^420]: The Buddha's illness is sometimes diagnosed as mesenteric
    infarction. The Buddha's body rejects the world of conditioned
    existence entirely.

[^421]: The commentary says that these verses were added by the elders
    at the Council.

[^422]: The *[saṅghāṭi]{lang="sa"}* ("outer robe" or "cloak") is
    double-layered, and served both for warmth and as bedding. The
    Buddha has not recovered fully from his illness.

[^423]: The Buddha was an exemplary patient. He did not complain, but
    made his needs known clearly to his carer.

[^424]: It would seem that, since the Buddha was enduring his illness
    without complaint and with dignity, Ānanda did not fully realize how
    weak he has become.

[^425]: The bowl (*patta*) was used both for eating and drinking.

[^426]: Pukkusa is not elsewhere mentioned. The reference to
    [Āḷāra]{lang="sa"} [Kālāma]{lang="sa"} recalls the narrative of the
    bodhisatta's practices before awakening. There are several details
    that indicate the stories of the first part and the last part of the
    Buddha's life were unified by such callbacks. We meet the Malla clan
    later on.

[^427]: *[Niśraya]{lang="sa"}* in the sense of "near" occurs at
    [Śatapathabrāhmaṇa]{lang="sa"} 3.1.2.17.

[^428]: In deep meditation the senses cease to function.

[^429]: As were [Mahāpajāpatī]{lang="sa"}'s limbs ([AN
    8.51](https://suttacentral.net/an8.51/en/sujato)).

[^430]: A nearby town where the Buddha had stayed previously ([Kd
    1:37.4.2](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#37.4.2)).

[^431]: *[Siṅgīvaṇṇa]{lang="sa"}*, or else *[siṅgīsuvaṇṇa]{lang="sa"}*
    ([AN 3.70:38.3](https://suttacentral.net/an3.70/en/sujato#38.3)), is
    gold from a mountain peak (*[siṅgī]{lang="sa"}*).
    [Kauṭilya]{lang="sa"} mentions a form of gold that is
    *[śṛṅgaśūktija]{lang="sa"}*, "occurring on mount [Śūkti]{lang="sa"}"
    ([Arthaśāstra]{lang="sa"} 2.13.3). The commentary to that passage
    says it had the color of red arsenic, so it would have been a rose
    gold color.

[^432]: Closing the narrative circle with the story of awakening.

[^433]: The commentary says this verse was added by the senior monks at
    the Council.

[^434]: The monk Cundaka makes an abrupt appearance here; this is the
    only mention of this name. The commentary to this passage calls him
    *cunda*, while the gloss to the parallel passage at [Ud
    8.5:14.3](https://suttacentral.net/ud8.5/en/sujato#14.3) has
    *cundaka* throughout. The diminutive ending *-ka* is perhaps meant
    to differentiate him from the smith Cunda. There are various Cundas
    whose connection is unclear. [Mahācunda]{lang="sa"} was one of the
    great disciples ([MN
    118:](https://suttacentral.net/mn118/en/sujato), [AN
    6.17](https://suttacentral.net/an6.17/en/sujato), [MN
    8](https://suttacentral.net/mn8/en/sujato)), who later brought the
    Dhamma to the land of the [Cetīs]{lang="sa"} ([AN
    6.46](https://suttacentral.net/an6.46/en/sujato), [AN
    10.24](https://suttacentral.net/an10.24/en/sujato)). He once stayed
    with Channa and [Sāriputta]{lang="sa"} ([MN
    144:4.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn144/en/sujato#4.1), [SN
    35.87:1.2](https://suttacentral.net/sn35.87/en/sujato#1.2)); the
    commentaries say he was in fact [Sāriputta]{lang="sa"}'s younger
    brother. They also identify him with the "novice Cunda" who reported
    the deaths of [Sāriputta]{lang="sa"} ([SN
    47.13:1.3](https://suttacentral.net/sn47.13/en/sujato#1.3)) and
    [Mahāvīra]{lang="sa"} ([DN
    29:2.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn29/en/sujato#2.1), [MN
    104:3.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn104/en/sujato#3.1)), explaining
    that the title "novice" was a nickname that persisted from the time
    he ordained as a young novice. It is unclear whether the
    commentaries take the Cundaka of this passage to be the same person,
    but he is here performing a similar role as carer adjacent to the
    Buddha's death. Later there appears a [Cūḷacunda]{lang="sa"} ([Tha
    Ap 52:13.3](https://suttacentral.net/tha-ap52/en/sujato#13.3)). Thus
    there may have been one person known by different names, or several
    people whose stories have become conflated.

[^435]: These verses were also added at the Council according to the
    commentary.

[^436]: Adopt PTS and BJT reading *[satthā]{lang="sa"}
    [pavattā]{lang="sa"}*.

[^437]: The text addresses Cunda with the respectful
    *[āvuso]{lang="sa"}*.

[^438]: Again, the narrative circle.

[^439]: The text uses *[āyasmā]{lang="sa"}* here for Cunda, as does the
    Sanskrit, whereas it is normally reserved for mendicants (see below,
    [DN 16:6.2.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#6.2.1)).

[^440]: This became a point of discussion in later Buddhism: can it be
    that the merit of a gift grows after it is given?

[^441]: Known today as Kushinagar, it is a popular site for pilgrims,
    with many ancient stupas, Buddha images, and a peaceful park for
    meditation.

[^442]: Normally when the Buddha lies down, his mind is focused on
    getting up. But now he knows that he will not rise again.

[^443]: Sal trees blossom in April/May. This detail agrees with the
    Buddha's final extinguishment in December/January, rather than in
    May (Vesak) as is currently celebrated (see note to [DN
    16:3.9.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#3.9.3)).

[^444]: This calls back to the description of the fourfold assembly in
    the [Māra]{lang="sa"} section above ([DN
    16:3.7.4](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#3.7.4)).

[^445]: This kind of narrative elevation is characteristic of the
    Buddha's teaching. He did not try to deny or eliminate any belief in
    the miraculous, or in the power of devotion, but rather to show that
    such things were of limited worth compared with practice.

[^446]: Like Cundaka above, [Upavāna]{lang="sa"} shares the duties of an
    attendant with Ānanda; at [DN
    29:41.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn29/en/sujato#41.1) he is also
    fanning the Buddha. At [AN
    5.166](https://suttacentral.net/an5.166/en/sujato), Ānanda goes to
    [Upavāna]{lang="sa"} for support when he feels he has disappointed
    the Buddha.

[^447]: In [SN 7.13](https://suttacentral.net/sn7.13/en/sujato)
    [Upavāna]{lang="sa"} fetched hot water and molasses for the Buddha,
    an event he remembered in his own verses at [Thag
    2.33](https://suttacentral.net/thag2.33/en/sujato).

[^448]: For this usage of *[phuṭo]{lang="sa"}*, see [AN
    3.56:1.3](https://suttacentral.net/an3.56/en/sujato#1.3).

[^449]: Elsewhere, *[pathavīsaññī]{lang="sa"}* refers to those who
    develop a form of meditation where they are "percipient of earth"
    ([AN 10.6:1.2](https://suttacentral.net/an10.6/en/sujato#1.2)).
    Here, however, it simply means those deities who are aware of events
    on the ground. \| Read *[chinnaṁpādaṁ]{lang="sa"} viya papatanti*.

[^450]: The Buddha as "eye" evokes the common (eg. Rig Veda 1.164.14,
    5.40.8, 5.59.5, 10.10.9) Vedic image of the Sun as the "eye of all"
    (*[viśvacakṣāḥ]{lang="sa"}*, 7.63.1), the "eye" for "eyes to see"
    (10.158.4), moving as an unaging wheel through the sky (1.164.14).
    See [Snp 3.9:11.1](https://suttacentral.net/snp3.9/en/sujato#11.1) =
    [MN 98:7.23](https://suttacentral.net/mn98/en/sujato#7.23). \| Pali
    has *[cakkhuṁ]{lang="sa"} loke* ("eye in the world"), Sanskrit has
    *[cakṣur]{lang="sa"} lokasya* ("eye of the world").

[^451]: These two reactions---grief and equanimity---are depicted often
    in Buddhist art and narrative. They stimulated the two poles of
    development of the Buddhist community. The devotional tradition,
    feeling the need for an emotional connection with the Teacher,
    developed art, story, and doctrines to, as it were, keep him alive,
    resulting in the [Jātakas]{lang="sa"} and the Bodhisattva doctrine.
    A cooler, rational tradition developed a comprehensive system
    analyzing the impermanence of "conditions", leading to the
    Abhidhamma.

[^452]: As for example at [MN
    24:2.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn24/en/sujato#2.1).

[^453]: *[Manobhāvanīya]{lang="sa"}* is explained in the commentaries as
    "those who, when seen, cause the mind to grow in what is skillful."

[^454]: These four sites are the primary destinations of modern Buddhist
    pilgrims in India. \| *[Saṁvejanīya]{lang="sa"}* is "stirring,
    provoking inspiration or urgency" such as when seeing an
    astonishing, disturbing, or amazing sight.

[^455]: [Lumbinī]{lang="sa"} in modern Nepal, which today is a
    well-maintained and quiet place for devotion and meditation. The
    site is marked with an Ashokan pillar dated perhaps 150 years after
    this time. On the pillar is inscribed in [Brahmī]{lang="sa"}
    characters *hida [bhagavaṁ]{lang="sa"} [jāte]{lang="sa"} ti*, which
    is a direct quote in [Māgadhī]{lang="sa"} of the Pali phrase here,
    *idha [tathāgato]{lang="sa"} [jāto]{lang="sa"} ti*.
    (*[Tathāgata]{lang="sa"}* "realized one" is how the Buddha referred
    to himself, while *[bhagavā]{lang="sa"}* "blessed one" is how his
    followers referred to him.) This is the oldest direct quote from the
    early canon preserved in the archaeological record.

[^456]: Bodhgaya in modern Bihar, which is called [Uruvelā]{lang="sa"}
    in the Pali texts. It is a bustling center for pilgrims from all
    over the Buddhist world, at the center of which is the great stupa
    next to the Bodhi tree.

[^457]: Isipatana is modern Sarnath, in the northern part of Varanasi on
    the Ganges. In the temple next to the park, the first sermon is
    recited every night. \| Note the passive voice, whereas the parallel
    at [AN 4.118](https://suttacentral.net/an4.118/en/sujato) is active.

[^458]: The phrase *[nibbānadhātuyā]{lang="sa"} parinibbuto*, "fully
    quenched in the element of extinguishment" shows how the Pali uses
    two roots simultaneously for Nibbana. The better-known term,
    *[nibbāna]{lang="sa"}* (Sanskrit *[nirvāṇa]{lang="sa"}*), is from
    the root *[vā]{lang="sa"}* ("to blow"), with the basic sense of
    going out through being deprived of air. The past participle form
    *nibbuta* (Sanskrit *[nirvṛta]{lang="sa"}*), from the root *var*
    ("to check or restrain"), has the basic sense of freedom from
    obstacles and constrictions. Generally, the Pali treats *nibbuta* as
    the past participle form of *[nibbāna]{lang="sa"}*, and for the most
    part the difference is purely verbal. Yet I believe the ambiguity is
    deliberate, as each captures a slightly different nuance of Nibbana.
    "Quenched" means both to go out like a flame and to be satisfied
    like a thirst. This positive feel appears in Sanskrit passages using
    *[nirvṛta]{lang="sa"}*, such as [Manusmṛti]{lang="sa"} 1.54, where
    it describes the blissful absorption of all beings into the great
    divinity. By using "extinguishment" for *[nibbāna]{lang="sa"}* and
    "quenched" for *nibbuta* I hope to express this subtle distinction,
    or at least to alert the reader that it exists.

[^459]: This passage is absent from the Sanskrit parallel and it is
    probably a late interpolation. Ānanda was handsome, and many stories
    of his encounters with women are preserved, for example at [Bu Pc
    41:1.1.5](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-pc41/en/sujato#1.1.5).

[^460]: As it stands, this appears to contradict [MN
    152:2.10](https://suttacentral.net/mn152/en/sujato#2.10), where the
    Buddha ridicules the idea that sense restraint implies not seeing.
    The commentary, however, explains it as not looking at a woman who
    is standing in the doorway of a monk's hut, so as not to give rise
    to lust. Thus it restricts this apparently general rule to an
    unusually intimate encounter.

[^461]: The verb changes from *[paṭipajjāma]{lang="sa"}* (first person
    plural) to *[paṭipajjitabbaṁ]{lang="sa"}* (future passive
    participle), a shift that mirrors the following passage regarding
    the funeral proceedings. There, the shift to future passive
    participle indicates that the subject is the lay folk who carry out
    the funeral, whereas here that does not apply. This suggests that
    this passage has been derived from that later passage.

[^462]: The commentary refers to [AN
    5.55:6.5](https://suttacentral.net/an5.55/en/sujato#6.5), which
    speaks of chatting alone in private with a woman, a circumstance
    also dealt with in [Bu Pc
    45](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-pc45/en/sujato).

[^463]: Ānanda does not hesitate to let the Buddha know he has no
    intention of following his advice.

[^464]: The commentary says, quoting [SN
    35.127:1.6](https://suttacentral.net/sn35.127/en/sujato#1.6), that
    when a woman has sincere motivations, one should speak while
    thinking of them as a mother, a sister, or a daughter.

[^465]: "Corpse" is *[sarīra]{lang="sa"}*.

[^466]: For *[abyāvaṭā]{lang="sa"}* ("don't get involved"), compare
    *[samaṇena]{lang="sa"} [bhavitabbaṁ]{lang="sa"}
    [abyāvaṭena]{lang="sa"}* ("a monastic shouldn't get involved" (in
    domestic matters)) at [Bu Ss
    5:1.3.34](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-ss5/en/sujato#1.3.34).

[^467]: Read *sadatthe* ("own goal") rather than *[sāratthe]{lang="sa"}*
    ("essential goal").

[^468]: An iron case was also used for Queen [Bhaddā]{lang="sa"}'s body
    at [AN 5.50:1.6](https://suttacentral.net/an5.50/en/sujato#1.6). The
    second iron case does not "enclose" the first; rather it "crooks"
    (*[paṭikujjati]{lang="sa"}*) like a lid to "close it up".

[^469]: This is a rare mention of the *[thūpa]{lang="sa"}* ("sepulchral
    momunent") that became a major feature of the Buddhist landscape.
    Buddhist monuments were round in shape, which was apparently a
    characteristic of the lands around Magadha.
    [Śatapathabrāhmaṇa]{lang="sa"} 13.8.1.5 says that the correct form
    of such a monument was square, and that round monuments were built
    by godless "easterners".

[^470]: Here the Buddha is said to be explicitly instructing his
    followers to adopt the building of monuments from the local customs.

[^471]: "Independent Buddhas" are sages who discover the Dhamma and are
    awakened independently, but who do not themselves go on to found a
    dispensation or establish a monastic order. Text has
    *paccekasambuddho* rather than the usual *paccekabuddho*.

[^472]: This refers to the "eight individuals" who make up the
    "[Saṅgha]{lang="sa"} of disciples", namely those who have achieved
    the four stages of awakening and those on the path.

[^473]: In contrast with [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"}.

[^474]: Like Queen [Subhaddā]{lang="sa"}, wife of
    [Mahāsudassana]{lang="sa"}, at [DN
    17:2.8.7](https://suttacentral.net/dn17/en/sujato#2.8.7) and [DN
    17:2.12.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn17/en/sujato#2.12.1). \|
    *[Kapisīsa]{lang="sa"}* is door-jamb, not lintel. \| For
    *[vihāra]{lang="sa"}* ("building") the commentary has "pavilion"
    (*[maṇḍalamāla]{lang="sa"}*), which may have been a temporary
    construction for the occasion (compare [MN
    92:4.6](https://suttacentral.net/mn92/en/sujato#4.6)).

[^475]: At this point Ānanda was a stream-enterer.

[^476]: When admonishing Ānanda, the Buddha first gently but firmly
    restrains him, then gives words of support and encouragement.

[^477]: The phrase "undivided and limitless" (*advayena
    [appamāṇena]{lang="sa"}*) normally describes *[kasiṇa]{lang="sa"}*
    meditation ([AN
    10.25:1.3](https://suttacentral.net/an10.25/en/sujato#1.3)); here it
    is also found in the Sanskrit: *[ānanda]{lang="sa"}
    [maitreṇa]{lang="sa"} [kāyakarmaṇā]{lang="sa"} hitena
    [sukhenādvayenāpramāṇena]{lang="sa"}*.

[^478]: On the eve of the First Council---in about six months
    time---Ānanda devoted himself to meditation and achieved
    arahantship.

[^479]: The same is said regarding the chief disciples
    [Sāriputta]{lang="sa"} and [Moggallāna]{lang="sa"} at [SN
    47.14:2.4](https://suttacentral.net/sn47.14/en/sujato#2.4) and
    regarding assemblies of deities at [DN
    20:4.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn20/en/sujato#4.3).

[^480]: *Tittha*, literally "ford", is a path to salvation, used as a
    term for a non-Buddhist "religion". *Titthakara* is a "religious
    founder" (literally "ford-maker"); *titthiya* is a "monastic of
    (another) religion" (for example at [Bu NP
    22:1.2.5](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-np22/en/sujato#1.2.5));
    *[titthiyasāvaka]{lang="sa"}* is a "disciple of a monastic of
    (another) religion".

[^481]: As at [AN
    4.130:6.4](https://suttacentral.net/an4.130/en/sujato#6.4). Ānanda
    was in awe of the "incredible and amazing" qualities of the Buddha,
    and spoke of them often. Here, when Ānanda is at his most
    vulnerable, the Buddha turns the teaching around, pointing out that
    Ānanda is incredible and amazing too.

[^482]: This anticipates the story to follow.

[^483]: [Campā]{lang="sa"}, [Rājagaha]{lang="sa"},
    [Sāvatthī]{lang="sa"}, and [Kosambī]{lang="sa"} were the capitals of
    [Aṅga]{lang="sa"}, Magadha, Kosala, and Vaccha respectively.
    [Sāketa]{lang="sa"} was the former capital of Kosala. Varanasi was
    formerly the capital of [Kāsi]{lang="sa"}, but at this time was
    contested by Kosala and Magadha, and had recently been won from
    [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"} ([SN
    3.14](https://suttacentral.net/sn3.14/en/sujato), [SN
    3.15](https://suttacentral.net/sn3.15/en/sujato)).

[^484]: This story is also found in [DN
    17](https://suttacentral.net/dn17/en/sujato). It seems that the Pali
    tradition extracted the story and greatly expanded it in an
    independent long discourse, whereas the Sanskrit tradition kept it
    at a more moderate length within the
    [Mahāparinirvāṇasūtra]{lang="sa"} itself.

[^485]: [Kusāvatī]{lang="sa"} features in the [Kusajātaka]{lang="sa"}
    ([Ja 531](https://suttacentral.net/ja531/en/sujato)), where the ugly
    but wise prince Kusa, son of the legendary [Okkāka]{lang="sa"}, wins
    the hand of the radiant [Pabhāvatī]{lang="sa"}. The
    [Rāmāyaṇa]{lang="sa"} also tells the story of a Kusa, son of
    [Rāma]{lang="sa"}, who ruled the city of [Kusāvatī]{lang="sa"},
    although this city was located far to the south in the Vindhya
    ranges. Both stories are united by the detail that *kusa* grass, a
    prominent feature of Vedic ritual, ensured the kingly lineage.

[^486]: A "league" (*yojana*) is usually estimated at between seven and
    twelve kilometers. By way of comparison, even at its greatest extent
    under Ashoka, [Pāṭaliputta]{lang="sa"} was less than a league per
    side, so the dimensions of [Kusāvatī]{lang="sa"} here are strictly
    legendary.

[^487]: According to [DN 32](https://suttacentral.net/dn32/en/sujato),
    [Āḷakamandā]{lang="sa"} was one of the many cities of Kuvera in
    Uttarakuru.

[^488]: [Vāseṭṭha]{lang="sa"} is a Vedic priestly clan. The Mallas
    adopted the name of their priest's lineage, which was the normal
    custom for initiated *khattiyas*. Other examples in the Pali are
    Saccaka who is called Aggivessana ([MN
    35:4.2](https://suttacentral.net/mn35/en/sujato#4.2)), and the
    Buddha and his family who are called Gotama.

[^489]: *Attadutiyo* "with self as second" is also at [MN
    146:4.6](https://suttacentral.net/mn146/en/sujato#4.6) where Nandaka
    visits the nuns. Sanskrit has *[bhikṣuṇā]{lang="sa"}
    [paścācchramaṇena]{lang="sa"}* ("with a mendicant as accompanying
    ascetic"). It was apparently evening (the "wrong time",
    *[vikāla]{lang="sa"}*), when it is inappropriate for a monk to be
    wandering the town alone.

[^490]: Evidently the Mallas, like the Vajjis, met frequently.

[^491]: This is the second Subhadda we have met in this discourse, the
    first being a deceased devotee of [Ñātika]{lang="sa"} ([DN
    16:2.6.13](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#2.6.13)). The
    corrupt monk who, after the Buddha's death, urges the rejection of
    the Vinaya rules is another person of the same name ([DN
    16:6.20.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#6.20.1)). The
    [Mahāsudassanasutta]{lang="sa"} also features a Queen
    [Subhaddā]{lang="sa"} ([DN
    17:2.5.9](https://suttacentral.net/dn17/en/sujato#2.5.9)).

[^492]: While some such as [Mahāvīra]{lang="sa"} the [Ñātika]{lang="sa"}
    ([MN 14:17.2](https://suttacentral.net/mn14/en/sujato#17.2)) and
    [Pūraṇa]{lang="sa"} Kassapa ([AN
    9.38:2.1](https://suttacentral.net/an9.38/en/sujato#2.1)) claimed to
    have direct knowledge, others such as Ajita Kesakambala denied that
    such knowledge was possible ([DN
    2:23.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn2/en/sujato#23.2)).

[^493]: The Buddha responded the same way when asked this question by
    the brahmin [Piṅgalakoccha]{lang="sa"} ([MN
    30:2.6](https://suttacentral.net/mn30/en/sujato#2.6)), and to a
    similar question at [AN
    9.38:3.2](https://suttacentral.net/an9.38/en/sujato#3.2).

[^494]: The four ascetics are defined at [AN
    4.241:1.1](https://suttacentral.net/an4.241/en/sujato#1.1) as those
    on the four paths.

[^495]: While many aspects of the eightfold path are shared with others,
    some details may be missing (such as not-self or
    [Nibbāna]{lang="sa"}), while others are added (such as the belief in
    the efficacy of rituals, an eternal soul, or a creator god).

[^496]: The Buddha points to the mendicants who have gathered there.

[^497]: This is the only place in the early texts where the Buddha
    identifies his age when going forth.

[^498]: Detailed in such suttas as [MN
    36](https://suttacentral.net/mn36/en/sujato).

[^499]: This verse appears to be corrupt. The sense can be restored
    through two extra lines in the Sanskrit: "Ethics, immersion,
    conduct, and knowledge, and unification of mind have been developed
    by me, teacher of the references for the noble teaching." \| For
    *[padesavattī]{lang="sa"}*, the Sanskrit has
    *[pradeśavaktā]{lang="sa"}*, where *[vaktā]{lang="sa"}* means
    "speaker" and *[pradeśa]{lang="sa"}* has the sense "pointing out". I
    think it means, "I am the one who taught the four great references
    (*[mahāpadesa]{lang="sa"}*)".

[^500]: The commentary says this line was added at the Council.

[^501]: This is similar to the idea of the Four Great references, and
    sets the scene for the First Council at which the teachings were
    recited. \| *[Paññatto]{lang="sa"}* here means "pointed out" rather
    than "laid down", as can be seen from [DN
    9:33.21](https://suttacentral.net/dn9/en/sujato#33.21), where the
    same phrase refers to the four noble truths.

[^502]: *Āvuso* is from the root *[āyu]{lang="sa"}* ("age") and thus has
    a respectful sense and does not mean "friend" as it is often
    translated. Like *bhante*, it is an indeclinable vocative of address
    that may be used with or without the name (see eg. [MN
    5:31.2](https://suttacentral.net/mn5/en/sujato#31.2)).

[^503]: I render *bhante* as "sir" when it stands alone and "honorable"
    when it prefixes a name. \| Unlike *bhante*, *[āyasmā]{lang="sa"}*
    ("venerable") is declinable, so it is used in parts of speech other
    than direct address. It is from the same root as
    *[āvuso]{lang="sa"}* but with a slightly more respectful tone,
    perhaps because it sounds more Sanskritic.

[^504]: These are not defined here, and the senior monks at the First
    Council were unable to agree on them ([Kd
    21:1.9.3](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd21/en/sujato#1.9.3)).
    Nonetheless, the Pali Vinaya consistently labels the
    [Pācittiya]{lang="sa"} rules as "lesser" (*khuddaka*; [Bu Pc
    92:2.2.22](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-pc92/en/sujato#2.2.22),
    [Bi Pc
    96:2.2.22](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bi-vb-pc96/en/sujato#2.2.22),
    [Pvr
    1.1:219.3](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-pvr1.1/en/sujato#219.3)),
    which would make the [Pātidesanīyas]{lang="sa"} "minor"
    (*anukhuddaka*). The Sekhiya rules are also "minor", but they were
    not at this point reckoned among the training rules for recitation.

[^505]: "Divine punishment" is *[brahmadaṇḍa]{lang="sa"}*. Channa
    features often in the Vinaya as a monk who refuses correction and
    acts disrespectfully. The Sangha had already imposed an act of
    "ejection" (*[ukkhepanīyakamma]{lang="sa"}*) on him due to his
    persistent bad behavior, but that was still not enough ([Kd
    1:25.1.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#25.1.1)).
    The *[brahmadaṇḍa]{lang="sa"}* was imposed at the First Council ([Kd
    1:1.12.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#1.12.1)),
    upon which Channa finally saw the error of his ways.
    *[Brahmadaṇḍa]{lang="sa"}* is encountered in a different sense at
    [DN 3:1.23.21](https://suttacentral.net/dn3/en/sujato#1.23.21).

[^506]: *Vattabba* in such contexts means "advise, correct" rather than
    more generally "speak to". Thus the *[brahmadaṇḍa]{lang="sa"}* is
    not the "silent treatment".

[^507]: The commentary says this line was added at the Council.

[^508]: Even on his deathbed, the Buddha retains mastery over his mind.

[^509]: Following the commentary, which reads this as a question.
    Anuruddha was renowned for his psychic powers. Note that Ānanda and
    Anuruddha have immediately adopted the forms of address recommended
    by the Buddha above.

[^510]: Each of these characters reveal something of themselves in their
    verses. Sahampati, as a royal deity, emphasizes the universal nature
    of the teaching and the grandeur of the Buddha.

[^511]: "Bag of bones" is a loose rendering of *samussaya*.

[^512]: Less creative than [Brahmā]{lang="sa"}, Sakka repeats a famous
    verse spoken by the Buddha at [SN
    15.20:8.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn15.20/en/sujato#8.1) and [DN
    17:2.17.5](https://suttacentral.net/dn17/en/sujato#2.17.5).

[^513]: Anuruddha was a reclusive meditator who specialized in
    mindfulness of breathing.

[^514]: Ānanda has the most emotional reaction. While Anuruddha speaks
    only of peace, Ānanda empathizes with those who were distraught.

[^515]: Text omits "with hair disheveled" (*kese pakiriya*) when
    describing the shaven-headed monks.

[^516]: "Still on the same business" (*teneva [karaṇīyena]{lang="sa"}*)
    calls back to [DN
    16:5.20.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#5.20.1), where
    they were said to be "on some business" (*kenacideva
    [karaṇīyena]{lang="sa"}*). They had been discussing all night.

[^517]: An uplifting response to tragedy, full of beauty and
    celebration.

[^518]: It seem unlikely that everyone simply forgot. It was probably
    the custom to wait seven days before the cremation.

[^519]: "Unworn" is *ahata*, as at [DN
    14:1.33.9](https://suttacentral.net/dn14/en/sujato#1.33.9) and [Kd
    1:1.6.2](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#1.6.2).

[^520]: "Coronation" is *[makuṭabandhana]{lang="sa"}*, "the binding of
    the crown". The commentary says there was, fittingly, an auspicious
    decorative royal hall there; perhaps too the name was felt to pun
    with *muktabandhana*, "freedom from ties".

[^521]: I think *sandhi* here means "covered without gaps".

[^522]: With the passing of [Sāriputta]{lang="sa"} and
    [Moggallāna]{lang="sa"}, [Mahākassapa]{lang="sa"} was the most
    senior of the remaining leading mendicants. As a solitary recluse,
    it was unusual for him to be travelling with such a large group, or
    with anyone at all really. He was to cite the events depicted here
    at the start of the First Council ([Kd
    1:1.1.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#1.1.1)).

[^523]: A follower of the Bamboo-staffed Ascetic [Gosāla]{lang="sa"}
    ([DN 2:19.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn2/en/sujato#19.1)). \| This
    may be an inspiration for the so-called "Flower Sermon", a medieval
    Zen story that depicts the Buddha holding up a flower and
    Mahakassapa smiling.

[^524]: Compare [Bu Pc
    72](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-pc72/en/sujato). The
    syntax is clumsy here, perhaps deliberately so.

[^525]: Here *[sarīrāneva]{lang="sa"}* is plural and so must mean
    "relics", whereas previously it was singular, "corpse".

[^526]: As when he was born ([DN
    14:1.28.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn14/en/sujato#1.28.1)).

[^527]: [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"} would have learned of the news from
    spies. It is a 600 km round trip to [Rājagaha]{lang="sa"} and back,
    which a mounted messenger could make in fourteen days.

[^528]: A bold move, considering that his designs on the Vajjis were no
    secret. Perhaps he was seeking a pretext for war. The justification
    for taking a share of relics is caste, rather than practice of the
    Dhamma.

[^529]: Both the tribe and the town are exceedingly obscure, mentioned
    nowhere else in early texts. They must have been a small clan
    nearby.

[^530]: The Koliyans were south-eastern neighbors of the Sakyans, and
    several of their towns and people feature in the early texts.

[^531]: This brahmin is mentioned nowhere else.

[^532]: *[Dassāma]{lang="sa"}* ("we shall give") is the future second
    plural of *dadati*.

[^533]: The brahmin [Doṇa]{lang="sa"} appears suddenly in the narrative,
    a reminder that there were many more people than the ones who are
    mentioned. The suttas record two encounters with a brahmin of this
    name: one is the wondrous story of seeing the Buddhas footprints
    ([AN 4.36](https://suttacentral.net/an4.36/en/sujato)), while the
    other discusses the five kinds of brahmin ([AN
    5.192](https://suttacentral.net/an5.192/en/sujato)).

[^534]: The fear of war was justified and the resolution achieved by
    [Doṇa]{lang="sa"} probably marks the last time these parties
    achieved a diplomatic outcome. The relative peace that had lasted
    most of the Buddha's life was crumbling. We hear of war or threats
    of war between the Kosalans and the Magadhans, the Magadhans and the
    Vajjis, the Sakyans and the Koliyans, the Kosalans and the Mallas,
    and the Kosalans and the Sakyans. It is probably because of the
    latter two conflicts that [Viḍūḍabha]{lang="sa"}---Pasenadi's son
    and the newly crowned king of Kosala---did not send an emissary to
    the funeral. By sparking conflicts with former allies the Sakyans
    and Mallas, [Viḍūḍabha]{lang="sa"} undid the successes of his father
    and fatally weakened the Kosalan Empire. When the dust cleared a few
    decades later, all these lands had been consumed by Magadha.

[^535]: Thus begins the practice of establishing Buddhism by interring
    relics in a stupa.

[^536]: The Moriyas were a minor clan of the region, unmentioned outside
    of this passage, but their obscurity was not to last long. About a
    century after these events, Chandragupta the Moriyan, having won the
    Magadhan crown from the Nandas, proceeded to route the Greeks in the
    west. His empire, which covered most of northern India, was further
    expanded to the south by his son Bindusara and grandson Ashoka,
    under whom the Mauryan Empire became the greatest of all Indian
    empires. Thus [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"}'s expansionist dreams were
    ultimately fulfilled beyond his imagining.

[^537]: The commentary says this summary was added at the Council.

[^538]: The commentary says this line was added at the Third Council,
    which was held about 150 years after the Buddha under Ashoka. The
    Buddha's life was already fading into legend.

[^539]: According to the commentary, the remainder of the text was added
    by the monks of Sri Lanka. Note, however, that this verse and the
    next are fairly similar to those in the Sanskrit text. Since that is
    a northern text, it seems unlikely these verses were composed in Sri
    Lanka.

[^540]: "Offering" is *[āyāga]{lang="sa"}*.

[^541]: Picking up the story of the Buddha's past life as King
    [Mahāsudassana]{lang="sa"} from [DN
    16:5.17.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#5.17.1).

[^542]: From here we leave [DN
    16](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato) behind and with it any
    semblance of realism. Some of these details are shared with [SN
    22.96](https://suttacentral.net/sn22.96/en/sujato), which however
    does not mention the name [Mahāsudassana]{lang="sa"}.

[^543]: Readings vary. The Sanskrit text has *[saptapauruṣā]{lang="sa"}
    [ardhacaturthapauruṣāś]{lang="sa"} ca [nikhātā]{lang="sa"}*.

[^544]: I think the point is that they listened to the music rather than
    doing bad things.

[^545]: This is specific instance, so I use past tense. Where it is an
    abstract description of a legendary past, I use the present tense to
    denote an eternal or mythic present.

[^546]: The wheel is firstly the sun and secondly the wheel of the
    chariots that drove the Indo-Europeans in their conquests. It is the
    manifestation of unstoppable power. The solar imagery is reflected
    in the name [Mahāsudassana]{lang="sa"} ("Great Splendor"). The whole
    story reflects the Indo-European dream of universal domination.

[^547]: Many of the details in this myth echo the Brahmanical horse
    sacrifice. Since the horse was the primary source of Indo-European
    dominion, its sacrifice served to authorize the power of a king. It
    was a costly and dangerous rite that was attempted only by the
    greatest of sovereigns. \| *[Bhiṅkāra]{lang="sa"}* ("ceremonial
    vase") and *abbhukkirati* ("besprinkled") are elevated terms.

[^548]: In the horse sacrifice, the horse is released for a year, while
    the king follows it with his army, claiming any land that it wanders
    on as his.

[^549]: Read *[bhuñjati]{lang="sa"}* at [MN
    98:10.30](https://suttacentral.net/mn98/en/sujato#10.30) with
    *[yathābhuttañca]{lang="sa"} [bhuñjatha]{lang="sa"}* at [DN
    17:1.9.4](https://suttacentral.net/dn17/en/sujato#1.9.4), [DN
    26:6.7](https://suttacentral.net/dn26/en/sujato#6.7), and [MN
    129:35.7](https://suttacentral.net/mn129/en/sujato#35.7). These have
    sometimes been rendered "eat", "enjoy", or "govern". But compare the
    archaic English "use" meaning "the benefit or profit of lands". Thus
    *[yathābhuttañca]{lang="sa"} [bhuñjatha]{lang="sa"}* means "use as
    has been used", i.e. "maintain the current level of taxation".

[^550]: The Wheel plunges into the seas, while the sacrificial horse is
    born in the western and eastern seas.

[^551]: Historically, India has usually been divided into squabbling
    realms, but from an early age there was a dream of a unified and
    peaceful continent.

[^552]: "At the High Court" (*[atthakaraṇapamukhe]{lang="sa"}*) is
    uncertain. *Attha-* has many variants, including
    *[aḍḍa]{lang="sa"}-* and *[aṭṭa]{lang="sa"}-*.
    *[Atthakaraṇa]{lang="sa"}* normally refers to a king "sitting in
    judgment" as at [SN
    3.7:1.5](https://suttacentral.net/sn3.7/en/sujato#1.5) or [MN
    89:13.3](https://suttacentral.net/mn89/en/sujato#13.3). Here,
    however it is a place. *-Pamukha* is a standard descriptor of the
    attributes of a wheel-turning monarch in the sense of "finest" (eg.
    [DN 17:2.5.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn17/en/sujato#2.5.1)) rather
    than "entrance". Thus I take it as meaning "the supreme place of
    judgment" i.e. "High Court".

[^553]: The white elephant is a symbol of royalty to this day. The
    description recalls Indra's elephant [Airāvata]{lang="sa"}. \| For
    *[sattappatiṭṭho]{lang="sa"}*, the commentary has
    *[susaṇṭhitaaṅgapaccaṅga]{lang="sa"}* ("well-grounded on each and
    every limb"), a sense confirmed by the [Mūlasarvāstivāda]{lang="sa"}
    [Bhaiṣajyavastu]{lang="sa"} which has *[saptāṅgaḥ]{lang="sa"}
    [supratiṣṭhito]{lang="sa"}* ("well-established on seven limbs"). The
    subcommentary lists the four feet, trunk, tail, and penis
    (*[varaṅga]{lang="sa"}*).

[^554]: The sacrificial horse is likewise white with black head or
    forequarters. It is identified with the sun, thus being a
    "sky-walker". "Thundercloud" (*[valāhaka]{lang="sa"}*; Sanskrit
    *[balāhaka]{lang="sa"}*) is the name of one of the four horses of
    [Kṛṣṇa]{lang="sa"}'s chariot in the [Mahābharata]{lang="sa"}. The
    description here also recalls the Vedic sacred horse
    *[uccaiḥśravas]{lang="sa"}*.

[^555]: The "commander" (*[pariṇāyaka]{lang="sa"}*) is mostly found in
    Buddhist texts of this context, and is not a regular term of
    governance. This passage shows that he was responsible for
    management of the realm, while below he appears as commander of the
    army ([DN 17:2.8.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn17/en/sujato#2.8.2)).
    Elsewhere he is said to excel in strategy ([AN
    5.134:2.5](https://suttacentral.net/an5.134/en/sujato#2.5)).

[^556]: The jarring inclusion of "women" (*[itthī]{lang="sa"}*) in this
    list probably refers to state-sponsored prostitution.

[^557]: As at [DN 5:19.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn5/en/sujato#19.1).

[^558]: The palace is named "Principle" (*dhamma*) in recognition of the
    king being subject to a higher law. Normally I translate
    *[pāsāda]{lang="sa"}* as "stilt longhouse" but here something
    grander is meant.

[^559]: For *[kūṭāgāra]{lang="sa"}* as "chamber" see [MN
    37:8.10](https://suttacentral.net/mn37/en/sujato#8.10).

[^560]: Thus denying the doctrine of [Pūraṇa]{lang="sa"} Kassapa at [DN
    2:17.5](https://suttacentral.net/dn2/en/sujato#17.5). This is the
    recognition, at least partially, of right view.

[^561]: The *[mahāviyūha]{lang="sa"}* must have been some kind of
    structure at the entrance to the palace, a "foyer".

[^562]: These are the three factors of right thought. The king
    skillfully uses a transition in physical space to set up his
    intention to meditate.

[^563]: Here as in [DN 1](https://suttacentral.net/dn1/en/sujato),
    *[jhāna]{lang="sa"}* is not a uniquely Buddhist practice.

[^564]: This segment breaks the expected pattern of "foremost" things.

[^565]: Not the British car of the same name. The chariot is the source
    of victory in battle.

[^566]: Read *[dukūlasandanāni]{lang="sa"}*, where *[dukūla]{lang="sa"}*
    is "fine cloth, silk" and *sandana* is "cord, tether".

[^567]: An odd detail. Maybe the sound of the elephants disturbed his
    meditation?

[^568]: Like Ānanda at [DN
    16:5.13.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#5.13.1).

[^569]: He makes it clear that this will not be an intimate visit.

[^570]: The aorist is not past tense, as it is governed by
    *[mā]{lang="sa"}*.

[^571]: "Desire" is *chanda*, the first of the four bases of psychic
    power, which in [DN
    16:3.3.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#3.3.1) are said to
    lead to long life. By urging him to live long, she inverts the
    [Mahāparinibbānasutta]{lang="sa"} where Ānanda fails to do the same.

[^572]: Implying that Ānanda was right to not beg the Buddha to live
    long.

[^573]: At [AN 6.16:2.2](https://suttacentral.net/an6.16/en/sujato#2.2)
    this advice is given by Nakula's mother.

[^574]: Like Ānanda at [DN
    16:5.13.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#5.13.1).

[^575]: This contrasts with the Buddha's last meal at [DN
    16:4.20.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#4.20.1), which
    caused sickness and distress. The Buddha was rejecting existence
    entirely, whereas [Mahāsudassana]{lang="sa"} was continuing in a
    pleasant form of conditioned existence.

[^576]: After [DN
    5:21.16](https://suttacentral.net/dn5/en/sujato#21.16), this is the
    second [Jātaka]{lang="sa"} in the [Dīghanikāya]{lang="sa"}.

[^577]: Accepting the [Mahāsaṅgīti]{lang="sa"}'s reading of
    *[vessinī]{lang="sa"}*. I believe the variant
    *[velāmikā]{lang="sa"}* is a ghost word contaminated from [AN
    9.20:4.1](https://suttacentral.net/an9.20/en/sujato#4.1).

[^578]: This picks up from the events of [DN
    16:2.5.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#2.5.1), which
    however discusses only the [Ñātikans]{lang="sa"} (who are reckoned
    among the Vajjis). Like [DN
    17](https://suttacentral.net/dn17/en/sujato), it bears many signs of
    a late sutta. The overall theme is the presence of a cosmic order
    where the liberating teaching of the Buddha is reconciled with the
    requirements of worldly power.

[^579]: This list of ten nations is unique. The omission of the
    [Aṅgas]{lang="sa"} and Magadhans is noted below. The more famous
    list of "sixteen nations" adds Avanti and Assaka in the south, and
    [Gandhāra]{lang="sa"} and Kamboja in the north-west (eg. [AN
    3.70:28.3](https://suttacentral.net/an3.70/en/sujato#28.3)). The
    Buddha did not visit these lands, which explains why they are
    omitted.

[^580]: "Devotees" is *[paricāraka]{lang="sa"}*, which normally means
    "servant". It is not in the relevant passages of DN 16. This sense
    is found in only a couple of other places, both late ([Snp
    5.18:1.2](https://suttacentral.net/snp5.18/en/sujato#1.2), [Kd
    1:22.14.8](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#22.14.8)).

[^581]: Bearing in mind that none of the nations were actually mentioned
    in the relevant [Mahāparinibbānasutta]{lang="sa"} passage, this
    whole introduction is framed to emphasize the importance of Magadha
    to Buddhism, especially given the problematic character of
    [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"}. In the decades following the Buddha's
    death, the Buddhist community adapted to a new political landscape
    which for a time saw the entirety of Buddhism contained within the
    sprawling Magadhan empire.

[^582]: As we known from [DN 2](https://suttacentral.net/dn2/en/sujato),
    [Bimbisāra]{lang="sa"} had recently been murdered by
    [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"}.

[^583]: A not-so-subtle hint of the changes under
    [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"}. The passing of [Bimbisāra]{lang="sa"}
    signifies the crumbling of the social order that prevailed in the
    Buddha's lifetime, bringing with it the immediate threat of war and
    chaos. This sutta establishes a timeless order that persists while
    worldly conditions fluctuate.

[^584]: Elsewhere the suttas say that [Bimbisāra]{lang="sa"} went for
    refuge ([DN 4:6.33](https://suttacentral.net/dn4/en/sujato#6.33)),
    while the Vinaya says he was in fact a stream-enterer ([Kd
    1:22.9.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#22.9.1)),
    which is confirmed below. The Jains, however, claim him as one of
    their own but say he went to hell for committing suicide. Like his
    son [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"}, it is likely that he frequented several
    teachers in his realm.

[^585]: Near the town of [Uruvelā]{lang="sa"} on the bank of the
    [Nerañjarā]{lang="sa"} River at the place known today as Bodhgaya.

[^586]: "Suggestion" is *[parikathaṁ]{lang="sa"}*, which elsewhere
    occurs in the Vinaya ([Kd
    7:1.5.16](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd7/en/sujato#1.5.16)) and
    the Abhidhamma [Vibhaṅga]{lang="sa"} ([Vb
    17:43.2](https://suttacentral.net/vb17/en/sujato#43.2)) in the sense
    of "hint".

[^587]: Normally *[aṭṭhiṁ]{lang="sa"} [katvā]{lang="sa"}
    [manasikatvā]{lang="sa"} [sabbaṁ]{lang="sa"} [cetasā]{lang="sa"}
    [samannāharitvā]{lang="sa"}* describes listening to Dhamma. This
    whole process seems unusually laborious.

[^588]: *[Nāmadheyyaṁ]{lang="sa"}* means "name (borne by someone or
    something)", not "one who bears the name" ([MN
    50:22.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn50/en/sujato#22.1)).

[^589]: *Janavasabha* is "chief of men", spelled *janesabha* at [DN
    20:10.10](https://suttacentral.net/dn20/en/sujato#10.10) and [DN
    32:10.9](https://suttacentral.net/dn32/en/sujato#10.9). The synonym
    *[narāsabha]{lang="sa"}* is an occasional poetic epithet of the
    Buddha ([Snp
    3.11:6.2](https://suttacentral.net/snp3.11/en/sujato#6.2), [Snp
    5.1:21.3](https://suttacentral.net/snp5.1/en/sujato#21.3), [SN
    11.3:14.2](https://suttacentral.net/sn11.3/en/sujato#14.2)). In
    Sanskrit we find *[puruṣaṛṣabha]{lang="sa"}* in the same sense.

[^590]: *[Uḷāravaṇṇa]{lang="sa"}* describes beautiful people at [MN
    96:7.8](https://suttacentral.net/mn96/en/sujato#7.8).

[^591]: Read *ito*. I think this implies what is stated more explicitly
    in Anuruddha's partly parallel verses at [Thag
    16.9:23.1](https://suttacentral.net/thag16.9/en/sujato#23.1): after
    each of seven rebirths under [Vessavaṇa]{lang="sa"} he can become a
    king of men. This explains the name Janavasabha.

[^592]: It is rare to see *[saṁsāra]{lang="sa"}* used as a countable
    noun, but see [Thag
    2.48:2.2](https://suttacentral.net/thag2.48/en/sujato#2.2).

[^593]: As a stream-enterer he is freed from any lower rebirths, yet he
    still aspires to a higher realization.

[^594]: In this idiom, the verb for "aware" varies between
    *[sañjānāti]{lang="sa"}*, *[jānāti]{lang="sa"}*,
    *[pajānāti]{lang="sa"}*, and *[sampajānāti]{lang="sa"}*.

[^595]: Compare [SN
    1.50:5.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn1.50/en/sujato#5.1) and [SN
    2.24:6.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn2.24/en/sujato#6.1).

[^596]: It is not just the Vajjis and the Buddhist [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"}
    who meet frequently in a hall to discuss business, but the gods as
    well. Here we get a rare glimpse into how the heavens work, or more
    to the point, how depictions of heavenly proceedings act as a
    template for how things should be on earth.

[^597]: The thirty-three and the Four Great Kings are both present; the
    heavenly realms are not shut off from one another.

[^598]: For *vipakkamati* compare [MN
    127:11.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn127/en/sujato#11.1).

[^599]: Compare [DN
    11:80.12](https://suttacentral.net/dn11/en/sujato#80.12).

[^600]: For *[vipāko]{lang="sa"} bhavissati* in discerning the results
    of signs, compare [DN
    1:1.24.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn1/en/sujato#1.24.2).

[^601]: See [AN
    3.127:2.3](https://suttacentral.net/an3.127/en/sujato#2.3).

[^602]: This phrase is also at [DN
    19:16.8](https://suttacentral.net/dn19/en/sujato#16.8), but apart
    from that the words *[pakativaṇṇa]{lang="sa"}* ("normal
    appearance"), *[anabhisambhavanīya]{lang="sa"}* ("imperceptible"),
    and *cakkhupatha* ("visual range") are all unique in the early
    texts.

[^603]: For the unique term "human form" (*[mānusaṁ]{lang="sa"}
    [viggahaṁ]{lang="sa"}*) compare the Vinaya phrase
    *[manussaviggahaṁ]{lang="sa"}* ([Bu Pj
    3:2.49.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-pj3/en/sujato#2.49.1)).

[^604]: Also appearing in [DN
    21:1.8.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn21/en/sujato#1.8.3), [DN
    20:10.11](https://suttacentral.net/dn20/en/sujato#10.11), and [SN
    35.119](https://suttacentral.net/sn35.119/en/sujato),
    [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"} ("Five-Crest") was a handsome and virile
    deity of the *gandhabbas*.

[^605]: *[Bhāsittha]{lang="sa"}* is 3rd singular aorist middle voice.

[^606]: Compare [MN
    91:21.4](https://suttacentral.net/mn91/en/sujato#21.4).

[^607]: Everything [Sanaṅkumāra]{lang="sa"} says is just a bit off. Here
    he adopts an idiom commonly used by the Buddha, but in third person
    rather than the Buddha's second person; and he asks only
    rhetorically, where the Buddha engages with his audience. If it were
    only this one example it would mean nothing, but similar changes
    happen throughout. I believe this is a subtle literary device that
    tells two narratives to two audiences. To potential converts it
    sounds like [Sanaṅkumāra]{lang="sa"} is giving a ringing and learned
    endorsement of Buddhism, while to knowledgeable Buddhists he appears
    as less than well versed in the teachings. I note these eccentric
    wordings as we proceed.

[^608]: The sutta began by recounting those who have achieved various
    stages of awakening. But escape from rebirth threatens the gods;
    this potentially fraught relationship is played out in [MN
    49](https://suttacentral.net/mn49/en/sujato).
    [Sanaṅkumāra]{lang="sa"} is preempting such arguments by pointing
    out that many of the Buddha's followers are reborn among the gods
    and do not escape transmigration, at least not for now. This is no
    trivial metaphysical argument. In order for Buddhism to prevail, it
    must show that its radical soteriology is compatible with worldly
    prosperity, lest it face opposition from kings and other temporal
    powers. The gods act as proxies to demonstrate the appropriate
    behavior for terrestrial kings. This is why the leading character is
    [Bimbisāra]{lang="sa"}/Janavasabha, who straddles the two realms.

[^609]: [Sanaṅkumāra]{lang="sa"} begins sharing the Buddha's teaching
    with the gods, starting with the bases of psychic power that
    featured prominently in DN 16. Here, however, he focuses on the
    worldly dimensions of psychic powers, ignoring the liberating
    dimension that was central to the Buddha. This sets the pattern for
    the teachings to follow, except for the very last.

[^610]: *[Yāva]{lang="sa"} [supaññattā]{lang="sa"}*, which is also at
    [MN 51:4.3](https://suttacentral.net/mn51/en/sujato#4.3), is a
    variation of the common exclamation *[yāva]{lang="sa"}
    [subhāsita]{lang="sa"}*. The phrase *[iddhipahutāya]{lang="sa"}
    [iddhivisavitāya]{lang="sa"} [iddhivikubbanatāya]{lang="sa"}* is
    unique. The [Paṭisambhidāmagga]{lang="sa"} draws on this passage to
    explain *vikubbana* both as a general term for development of
    psychic powers ([Ps
    3.2:4.3](https://suttacentral.net/ps3.2/en/sujato#4.3)) and as
    specific kind of psychic power, namely the transformation of one's
    apparent form as demonstrated by [Sanaṅkumāra]{lang="sa"} ([Ps
    3.2:15.1](https://suttacentral.net/ps3.2/en/sujato#15.1)).

[^611]: These "three opportunities" are not found elsewhere.

[^612]: This unique term is the reverse of the phrase that begins the
    first *[jhāna]{lang="sa"}*. The passage, however, speaks only of
    lifestyle rather than deep meditation.

[^613]: "Aloof" (*[asaṁsaṭṭha]{lang="sa"}*) is a synonym of "secluded"
    (*vivicca*). The *[jhāna]{lang="sa"}* formulas are extremely stable
    and it is rare to find them played with like this.

[^614]: Since *[jhāna]{lang="sa"}* arises from pleasure, it cannot be
    meant here.

[^615]: "Coarse physical, verbal, and mental processes"
    (*[oḷārikā]{lang="sa"} [kāyasaṅkhārā]{lang="sa"}
    ...[vacīsaṅkhārā]{lang="sa"} ... [cittasaṅkhārā]{lang="sa"}*) is
    another unique term. They are probably to be identified with the
    three "processes" of [MN
    44:14.2](https://suttacentral.net/mn44/en/sujato#14.2).

[^616]: This builds off [DN
    1:2.24.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn1/en/sujato#2.24.1), but the
    full phrase is only here and [DN
    19:7.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn19/en/sujato#7.2).

[^617]: Normally *[satipaṭṭhāna]{lang="sa"}* is taught "in order to
    purify sentient beings, to get past sorrow and crying, to make an
    end of pain and sadness, to end the cycle of suffering, and to
    realize extinguishment" (eg. [DN
    22:1.7](https://suttacentral.net/dn22/en/sujato#1.7)). Here, in yet
    another unique phrasing, a more humble goal is sought.

[^618]: Again the liberating dimension is ignored in favor of worldly
    psychic abilities. This is another unique formulation.

[^619]: Also found at [AN
    7.45:1.1](https://suttacentral.net/an7.45/en/sujato#1.1). At [MN
    44:12.4](https://suttacentral.net/mn44/en/sujato#12.4) the four
    right efforts are said to be the "prerequisites of immersion". Here
    at last [Sanaṅkumāra]{lang="sa"} introduces the liberating dimension
    of the path as a whole.

[^620]: Also at [SN 45.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn45.1/en/sujato),
    etc.

[^621]: To the standard passage on the qualities of the Dhamma,
    [Sanaṅkumāra]{lang="sa"} clumsily tacks on the line spoken by the
    Buddha when he was persuaded by [Brahmā]{lang="sa"} to teach ([SN
    6.1:10.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn6.1/en/sujato#10.1), [DN
    14:3.7.13](https://suttacentral.net/dn14/en/sujato#3.7.13), [MN
    26:21.6](https://suttacentral.net/mn26/en/sujato#21.6), [MN
    85:45.6](https://suttacentral.net/mn85/en/sujato#45.6)).

[^622]: Finally the question is answered, although the number of deaths
    is obviously exaggerated. We cannot estimate the population size at
    the time with any confidence. But we know that
    [Pāṭaliputta]{lang="sa"} under Ashoka covered about 25 km², which
    suggests a population somewhere around 250,000. Probably there were
    a few million people in the whole of Magadha.

[^623]: The commentary identifies these as non-returners, but this is
    unlikely as *[puññābhāga]{lang="sa"}* ("share of merit") indicates
    those who do good for the sake of a good rebirth rather than
    liberation ([AN
    6.63:30.2](https://suttacentral.net/an6.63/en/sujato#30.2)). There
    would have been countless more of such good folk than
    stream-enterers, whereas the number of non-returners, as indicated
    by the count of the [Ñātikans]{lang="sa"}, would have been small,
    not "countless". This verse is also at [SN
    6.13:7.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn6.13/en/sujato#7.1), where the
    commentary says nothing of non-returners.

[^624]: [Sanaṅkumāra]{lang="sa"} lives longer than the lesser gods so he
    has a broader perspective. He emphasizes the long-term stability of
    cosmic order, which prevails through the crises that afflict the
    short-lived kingdoms of men.

[^625]: Astonishingly, this sutta traces its textual lineage directly to
    [Brahmā]{lang="sa"}, exactly like the Brahmanical texts (eg.
    [Bṛhadāraṇyaka]{lang="sa"} [Upaniṣad]{lang="sa"} 6.5.4). Of course,
    [Brahmā]{lang="sa"} gets his teaching from the Buddha, but this is
    clearly copying the Brahmanical model.

[^626]: Here Ānanda plays a key role as the linchpin of the oral
    tradition. It is likely that not just the
    [Mahāparinibbānasutta]{lang="sa"} itself, but all these suttas of
    this cycle were composed by Ānanda or his students.

[^627]: With this unique ending, there is no claim that this sutta was
    heard by the mendicants in the usual way. Rather it makes its
    purpose explicit, to act as a means to widely propagate the Dhamma.
    The [Mahāparinibbānasutta]{lang="sa"} also speaks of the spread of
    the Dhamma, and the Janavasabhasutta indicates some of the means by
    which that was achieved in the decades following the Buddha's death;
    in particular, by allying itself with the ascendant power of
    Magadha.

[^628]: We were introduced to [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"} in [DN
    18](https://suttacentral.net/dn18/en/sujato), which is similar to
    the current sutta in many other details as well. Thus this sutta can
    be considered as a distant cousin to the
    [Mahāparinibbāna]{lang="sa"} cycle.

[^629]: The council of gods described by Janavasabha at [DN
    18:12.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn18/en/sujato#12.1), while
    otherwise similar, took place at the entry to a rainy season,
    whereas this one is at the end of a rainy season. This cannot have
    been the Buddha's last rains, for he was already in Vajji by then.

[^630]: For "genuine praise" (*[yathābhucce]{lang="sa"}
    [vaṇṇe]{lang="sa"}*), see [DN
    1:1.28.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn1/en/sujato#1.28.1).

[^631]: This set of eight is not found elsewhere. \| "Proffered" is
    *[payirudāhāsi]{lang="sa"}*, a unique term in early texts.

[^632]: Sakka adopts the same rhetorical style as
    [Sanaṅkumāra]{lang="sa"} ([DN
    18:20.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn18/en/sujato#20.2)).

[^633]: Compare [DN
    18:25.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn18/en/sujato#25.1).

[^634]: *[Paṭipadā]{lang="sa"}* is instrumental.

[^635]: I don't think this idea is found elsewhere in quite this way.
    Normally it is said that the path leads to [Nibbāna]{lang="sa"} like
    the rivers lead to the ocean (eg. [SN
    45.114:1.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn45.114/en/sujato#1.1)).

[^636]: This item is unique.

[^637]: Another unique item.

[^638]: This is normally true, but in some cases the Buddha left a
    badly-behaved community ([MN
    48](https://suttacentral.net/mn48/en/sujato)), dismissed misbehaving
    monks (eg. [AN
    8.10:2.2](https://suttacentral.net/an8.10/en/sujato#2.2)), or even
    dismissed a large community ([MN
    67](https://suttacentral.net/mn67/en/sujato)). \| The "joy of
    solitude" is *[ekārāmataṁ]{lang="sa"}* .

[^639]: Also at [DN
    29:29.5](https://suttacentral.net/dn29/en/sujato#29.5), [AN
    4.23:4.1](https://suttacentral.net/an4.23/en/sujato#4.1), and [Iti
    112:5.1](https://suttacentral.net/iti112/en/sujato#5.1).

[^640]: *[Pariyositasaṅkappo]{lang="sa"}* ("achieved all he wished for")
    is a synonym of the more common *[paripuṇṇasaṅkappo]{lang="sa"}*
    (eg. [MN 29:2.5](https://suttacentral.net/mn29/en/sujato#2.5)).

[^641]: This impossibility is also at [AN
    1.277:1.1](https://suttacentral.net/an1.277/en/sujato#1.1) and [MN
    115:14.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn115/en/sujato#14.1). \| "In one
    solar system" (*[ekissā]{lang="sa"} [lokadhātuyā]{lang="sa"}*): a
    single *[lokadhātu]{lang="sa"}* encompasses a single terrestrial
    world with a single moon and sun.

[^642]: It is not common to wish for the Buddha's long life, but we do
    find this sentiment expressed in a conversation between
    [Sāriputta]{lang="sa"} and Ānanda ([SN
    21.2:3.5](https://suttacentral.net/sn21.2/en/sujato#3.5)).

[^643]: "Having appraised" (*[saṅkhāya]{lang="sa"}*) is glossed by the
    commentary with "having known" (*[jānitvā]{lang="sa"}*). Compare the
    discussion on "judgmentalism" at [DN
    1:1.3.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn1/en/sujato#1.3.1).

[^644]: Disampati means "head of the directions", i.e. king of all the
    land. He and his story are found nowhere else in the early texts.

[^645]: "Steward" is Govinda, literally "lord of cows". The "high
    priest" (*purohita*) was a hereditary office, a learned ritualist
    and adviser attached to a specific family. For royal families the
    post could be highly contested. The closeness of the role is shown
    in that aristocratic families could be referred to by the lineage of
    the high priest.

[^646]: While the current sutta does not explicitly identify their
    realm, [Dīpavaṁsa]{lang="sa"} iii 40 records Disampati and
    [Reṇu]{lang="sa"} as kings of [Kāsi]{lang="sa"}. This fits with the
    geographical layout depicted in this sutta, with [Kāsi]{lang="sa"}
    at the center. The story, then, depicts the establishment of
    Brahmanical kings across India from [Kāsi]{lang="sa"}.

[^647]: [Jotipāla]{lang="sa"} means "guardian of the sacred flame", i.e.
    someone who maintains the Vedic fire ritual (see below at [DN
    19:47.26](https://suttacentral.net/dn19/en/sujato#47.26)). There was
    another [Jotipāla]{lang="sa"} in the time of Buddha Kassapa at [MN
    81:6.2](https://suttacentral.net/mn81/en/sujato#6.2), and another
    who was a religious founder of the past ([AN
    6.54:18.1](https://suttacentral.net/an6.54/en/sujato#18.1), [AN
    7.73:2.5](https://suttacentral.net/an7.73/en/sujato#2.5)). Despite
    the popularity of the name in Pali, it does not seem to appear in
    Sanskrit sources. Given that it appears in Pali only in legendary
    contexts, it is probably a vocational epithet.

[^648]: The king's only care, it seems, is not for the passing of his
    friend, but that he no longer gets to indulge in whatever he wants.

[^649]: This shows that Govinda is an office rather than a personal
    name.

[^650]: Following the [Mahāsaṅgīti]{lang="sa"} reading rather than the
    PTS (*[nānusāsi]{lang="sa"} ... [nānusāsati]{lang="sa"}*). It seems
    required by the context that he does more than his father.

[^651]: Following PTS reading *[sukhaṁ]{lang="sa"}
    [edheyyātha]{lang="sa"}*.

[^652]: The meaning of *[sakaṭamukhaṁ]{lang="sa"}* ("front of a cart")
    is clarified by comparison with the parallels. T 8 is identical (),
    while DA 3 expresses a similar idea more briefly (). The
    [Mahāvastu]{lang="sa"} (Mvu 85.17) confirms this sense with
    *[dakṣiṇena]{lang="sa"} [saṁkṣiptā]{lang="sa"}
    [śakaṭamukhasaṁsthitaṁ]{lang="sa"}*. These appear to demonstrate a
    knowledge of the shape of the Indian subcontinent. Below it says
    that each of the kingdoms is shaped like the front of a cart, just
    as India is as a whole.

[^653]: Neither Rhys Davids nor Walshe translate this line, but it
    receives an extensive discussion in the commentary. It says that six
    kingdoms were arranged around [Reṇu]{lang="sa"}'s kingdom in the
    middle, like an umbrella (*[vitānasadisaṁ]{lang="sa"}*).

[^654]: The verses tell the realms starting with the rising sun at
    [Kaliṅga]{lang="sa"} in the east and proceeding clockwise
    (*[padakkhiṇā]{lang="sa"}*) until the circle is complete with
    [Aṅga]{lang="sa"} on the [Kaliṅga]{lang="sa"} border. Thus the
    original center was probably the last place on the list,
    [Kāsi]{lang="sa"}, which is indeed geographically central.

[^655]: [Kaliṅga]{lang="sa"} was a coastal realm in modern Odisha and
    Andhra Pradesh. There is no consensus on the exact location of
    Dantapura.

[^656]: Assaka stretched from [Kaliṅga]{lang="sa"}'s western border
    across the interior. Potana is modern Bodhan in Telangana state.

[^657]: *Mahesaya* of the Pali editions is a variant spelling for
    *[māhissatī]{lang="sa"}*; [Mahāvastu]{lang="sa"} has
    *[māhiṣmatī]{lang="sa"}*. It is in present-day Madhya Pradesh, on
    the banks of Narmada River, and is perhaps to be identified with
    modern Maheshwar.

[^658]: [Sovīra]{lang="sa"} was on the lower Indus, and Roruka is
    identified with modern Rohri in Sindh province, Pakistan. It is
    implausible that they received Aryan culture from [Kāsi]{lang="sa"}.

[^659]: [Mithilā]{lang="sa"} was the capital of Videha, to the north
    east of the Vajjian federation, nestled against the Himalayas.
    [Mithilā]{lang="sa"} was a dominant kingdom before the Buddha, its
    king Janaka featuring prominently in early [Upaniṣads]{lang="sa"}.
    It features rarely in the suttas ([MN
    91](https://suttacentral.net/mn91/en/sujato), [Thig
    6.2](https://suttacentral.net/thig6.2/en/sujato), [Thig
    13.4](https://suttacentral.net/thig13.4/en/sujato)) and had
    apparently declined in importance. Śatapatha [Brāhmaṇa]{lang="sa"}
    1.4.1.10--19 depicts its origins in terms of the spread of
    fire-worship from the west by its founding king [Māthava]{lang="sa"}
    Videgha with his priest Gotama [Rāhūgaṇa]{lang="sa"}.

[^660]: [Campā]{lang="sa"} is modern Champapuri near Bhagalpur in Bihar
    state, not far from West Bengal. [Aṅga]{lang="sa"} lay between the
    Chandan river to the west and the Rajmahal hills to the east. In the
    centuries before the Buddha it expanded its dominion to the sea on
    the south-east and Magadha in the west. But with the
    [Kaliṅgas]{lang="sa"} pushing back from the coast and the rise of
    [Bimbisāra]{lang="sa"} in Magadha, they were pushed back to their
    ancestral lands and fell under Magadhan dominion.

[^661]: Varanasi, one of the oldest cities in the world, was the capital
    of the [Kāsī]{lang="sa"} kingdom. It lost its status as an
    independent kingdom shortly before the Buddha, when it was taken
    over by Kosala. It appears in countless Buddhist stories of the past
    as the dominant city of the region in what appears to be a timeless
    and ageless past. However, despite its great antiquity, it is a
    historical settlement, of which the discovered remains date back to
    perhaps 1200 BCE; it was a capital city from perhaps 800 BCE.

[^662]: The names of the aristocrat kings---most of which are difficult
    to identify from other sources---are listed in an abruptly-inserted
    verse. The names are missing from the parallels in
    [Mahāvastu]{lang="sa"} and DA 3, but T 8 appears to have the same
    list of names in the same order, except with [Reṇu]{lang="sa"} at
    the start. Rhys Davids proposes to assign each one to a kingdom as
    listed in the same order. This seems to work for the first four
    names, as [Ja 424](https://suttacentral.net/ja424/en/sujato),
    invoking this sutta, identifies Bharata as king of
    [Sovīra]{lang="sa"}. But it falls apart with [Reṇu]{lang="sa"}, who
    as we have seen, belongs in [Kāsi]{lang="sa"} rather than Videha.

[^663]: "Bharata" is the name of an individual king, while they are
    collectively known as "[Bhāratas]{lang="sa"}" (with a long initial
    *[ā]{lang="sa"}* due to secondary derivation). [Ja
    424](https://suttacentral.net/ja424/en/sujato), invoking this sutta,
    identifies Bharata as king of [Sovīra]{lang="sa"}.

[^664]: Here *[bhāratā]{lang="sa"}* means "kings of India" or perhaps,
    if read together with the preceding verses, "kingdoms of India".
    India was called [Bhārata]{lang="sa"} after the legendary King
    Bharata of [Hastināpura]{lang="sa"} (Delhi), founder of the Lunar
    dynasty, conqueror of India, and sire of the warring tribes of the
    [Mahābharata]{lang="sa"}. His story is told in the
    [Saṁbhavapara]{lang="sa"} of the [Mahābhārata]{lang="sa"}. The name
    represents the success of Vedic culture across the subcontinent.

[^665]: In spiritual circles, such rumors spread like wildfire in an
    Australian summer.

[^666]: The ancient Brahmanical teachers are invoked to justify the
    Buddhist rains retreat. \| Compassion is one of the "meditations of
    [Brahmā]{lang="sa"}" (*[brahmavihāra]{lang="sa"}*). Normally the
    suttas speak of the absorptions (*[jhāna]{lang="sa"}*) and of the
    meditation on compassion (*[karuṇā]{lang="sa"}*), but the idea of an
    "absorption on compassion" is unique to this sutta.

[^667]: Compare [MN
    51:10.3](https://suttacentral.net/mn51/en/sujato#10.3), where the
    same construction is a site for the sacrifice.

[^668]: "Deathless" from a Brahmanical perspective, but very much within
    the realm of *[saṁsāra]{lang="sa"}* from a Buddhist perspective.

[^669]: *Brahme* is the normal vocative for *[brahmā]{lang="sa"}*.
    However in verse it is sometimes used as vocative for
    *[brāhmaṇa]{lang="sa"}* (eg. [Snp
    5.1:7.3](https://suttacentral.net/snp5.1/en/sujato#7.3), [Snp
    5.19:3.4](https://suttacentral.net/snp5.19/en/sujato#3.4)).

[^670]: These factors are explained as a summary of the Gradual
    Training.

[^671]: "At one" (*[ekodibhūta]{lang="sa"}* or more commonly
    *[ekodibhāva]{lang="sa"}*) normally describes deep meditation, and
    is part of the formula for the second *[jhāna]{lang="sa"}*. Here it
    is explained as equivalent to "seclusion" (*viveka*).

[^672]: "Putrefaction" (*[āmagandha]{lang="sa"}*) is the smell of
    (moral) decay or corruption ([AN
    3.128](https://suttacentral.net/an3.128/en/sujato), [Snp
    2.2](https://suttacentral.net/snp2.2/en/sujato)).

[^673]: "Desire" is (*[icchā]{lang="sa"}*). PTS reads
    *[vicikicchā]{lang="sa"}* ("doubt"), but this must be incorrect as
    the commentary explains it as "craving" (*[taṇhā]{lang="sa"}*).

[^674]: "Easy to quell" (*sunimmadaya*) is unique to this passage.

[^675]: As noted in the comment to [DN
    16:5.19.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn16/en/sujato#5.19.2), kingly
    clans adopted the lineage name of the high priest (*purohita*)
    during initiation.

[^676]: "True teaching" (*saddhamma*) normally describes the Buddha's
    teaching.

[^677]: "Leads to rebirth in the [Brahmā]{lang="sa"} realm"
    (*[brahmalokūpapattiyā]{lang="sa"}*), just as the teaching of
    [Āḷāra]{lang="sa"} [Kālāma]{lang="sa"}, based on the even more
    refined formless meditations, leads to rebirth in the dimension of
    nothingness ([MN
    36:14.14](https://suttacentral.net/mn36/en/sujato#14.14)).

[^678]: Echoing the ancient hermit Araka at [AN
    7.74:2.2](https://suttacentral.net/an7.74/en/sujato#2.2), who said
    life is evanescent like a dewdrop.

[^679]: It is regarded as normal that woman should renounce.

[^680]: Compare with the mass renunciation under [Vipassī]{lang="sa"}
    ([DN 14:2.16.6](https://suttacentral.net/dn14/en/sujato#2.16.6)).

[^681]: His meditation expands from compassion to include all four of
    the *[brahmavihāras]{lang="sa"}*.

[^682]: This is the third and final [Jātaka]{lang="sa"} in the
    [Dīghanikāya]{lang="sa"}, after [DN
    5](https://suttacentral.net/dn5/en/sujato) and [DN
    17](https://suttacentral.net/dn17/en/sujato).

[^683]: According to the doctrine of the "perfections"
    (*[pāramī]{lang="sa"}*), which emerged around two to four centuries
    after the Buddha's passing, the practices he undertook in past lives
    laid the foundation for awakening in this life. Here, however, the
    Buddha states that his former practices did not lead to awakening.
    Rather, since they were based on the wrong view of eternal bliss in
    the [Brahmā]{lang="sa"} realm, they only led to a good rebirth so
    long as that kamma lasted. It is the eightfold path, which the
    Buddha discovered in his final life, which leads to awakening. The
    same saying in a similar context is found at [MN
    83:21.7](https://suttacentral.net/mn83/en/sujato#21.7).

[^684]: This discourse gives an extensive account of divine beings. Some
    are familiar from elsewhere in the Pali texts, others found in
    various Brahmanical sources, while still others occur only here. For
    the names we can compare the edition of the Sanskrit text by
    Waldschmidt and Sander and available on SuttaCentral as [SF
    140](https://suttacentral.net/sf140/san/waldschmidt-sander).

[^685]: The Pure Abodes are inhabited entirely by non-returners.

[^686]: "Great congregation" is *[mahāsamaya]{lang="sa"}*.

[^687]: A village ([Bu Pj
    2:3.8](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-pj2/en/sujato#3.8)) or
    royal compound ([Bu Pc
    83:1.3.56.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-pc83/en/brahmali#1.3.56.1))
    was marked with a "boundary post" (*[indakhīla]{lang="sa"}*). It
    symbolized a fixed and immovable point, either in a good sense ([SN
    56.39:4.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn56.39/en/sujato#4.1)) or, as
    here, an obstacle. \| For [Mahāsaṅgīti]{lang="sa"}'s
    *[ūhacca]{lang="sa"} [manejā]{lang="sa"}* read
    *[ūhacca]{lang="sa"}-m-[anejā]{lang="sa"}*.

[^688]: *[Nāga]{lang="sa"}* can refer to a class of semi-divine beings
    in a powerful serpentine form ("dragon"); a large and powerful
    snake, especially a king cobra; a bull elephant; or any powerful and
    mighty being ("giant").

[^689]: The discourse up to here is also found at [SN
    1.37](https://suttacentral.net/sn1.37/en/sujato).

[^690]: This recalls [DN
    14:1.10.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn14/en/sujato#1.10.1), where
    the relative sizes of the mendicant congregations of different
    Buddhas are tallied. That sutta, in another point of similarity,
    also featured a conversation with deities of the Pure Abodes.

[^691]: "Paean of praise" is *siloka*, a rare case where this means
    "verse (of praise)" rather than "fame, renown". \|
    *[Anukassāmi]{lang="sa"}* is present tense from the root *kass*, "to
    draw (up), to drag". Compare Sanskrit *[anukarṣa]{lang="sa"}* in the
    sense "invoking, summoning by incantation".

[^692]: This gives a hint as to one of the purposes of this sutta.
    Living alone in remote forests can be terrifying. Such places were
    widely believed to be haunted by all manner of supernatural
    creatures, not all of them friendly. While for an arahant this posed
    no threat, there would have been then, as there are today, many
    young or aspiring meditators who faced such challenges with
    trepidation. These verses offer succor, surrounding them with
    powerful, albeit invisible, allies.

[^693]: "Spirit" is *yakkha*. In later legend they appear as monstrous
    figures, but in the suttas they are ambiguous and may often be
    friendly to the Dhamma. Early statues of *yakkhas* at
    [Madhurā]{lang="sa"} (circa 100 BCE) depict noble and powerful
    kings, not ogres. *Yakkha* is also sometimes used more generally in
    the sense of an individual or deity.

[^694]: Colorful like the nymphs of [MN
    50:25.6](https://suttacentral.net/mn50/en/sujato#25.6) or
    [Susīma]{lang="sa"}'s gods at [SN
    2.29:7.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn2.29/en/sujato#7.1).

[^695]: Mount [Sātā]{lang="sa"} is unidentified, but according to the
    commentary it was in the middle region.

[^696]: [Vessāmitta]{lang="sa"} ("friend of all") is the name of a Vedic
    hermit. Legend has it that he was a *khattiya* who earned
    brahminhood due to his intense austerities in the remote Himalayas.
    Perhaps this is a mountain named for him, although I can find no
    trace of it in Sanskrit.

[^697]: *[Kumbhīra]{lang="sa"}* means "crocodile".

[^698]: We met two human King [Dhataraṭṭhas]{lang="sa"} ("Strongrealm")
    in the [Mahāgovindasutta]{lang="sa"}.

[^699]: "Centaur" is *gandhabba*. *Gandhabbas* are wild, sexual beings
    who, being first to yoke the horse (Śatapatha [Brāhmaṇa]{lang="sa"}
    5.1.4.8) and riding the steed called "Racer" (*[vājin]{lang="sa"}*,
    10.6.4.1), "take the reins" from Indra (Rig Veda 1.163.2) as his
    charioteer [Mātali]{lang="sa"}. It is likely the Sanskrit
    *gandharva* stems from the same root as the Greek *kentauro*, and
    the ultimate origin of the idea comes from the proto-Indo-Europeans,
    who rode the horse, creating a potent entity that was invincible in
    battle.

[^700]: By implication, they are renowned as being powerful as Indra,
    the king of gods who in Pali is more commonly called Sakka. It is
    also a nod to the multiplicity of divinities in the Vedic system,
    where one becomes many and many become one.

[^701]: *[Virūḷhaka]{lang="sa"}* is "growth"; he was probably a god of
    the fertile crops. In the Pali it is spelled [Virūḷha]{lang="sa"}
    here due to the meter.

[^702]: "Goblins" is *[kumbhaṇḍa]{lang="sa"}*, a race of lesser deities
    often depicted as ugly and misshapen. On the face of it,
    *[kumbhaṇḍa]{lang="sa"}* means "potballs", i.e. deities whose
    testicles are as big as pots. Perhaps more likely they are related
    to the beings known as *[kuṣmāṇḍa]{lang="sa"}* in Sanskrit, whose
    bellies are round as "pumpkins".

[^703]: One of the "royal snake families" at [AN
    4.67:3.2](https://suttacentral.net/an4.67/en/sujato#3.2). His name
    means "multiple eyes" (*[virūpa]{lang="sa"}-akkha*) for the false
    eyes on a cobra's hood.

[^704]: Also known as [Vessavaṇa]{lang="sa"} ([DN
    32:7.43](https://suttacentral.net/dn32/en/sujato#7.43), [Snp
    2.14:6.1](https://suttacentral.net/snp2.14/en/sujato#6.1)), which
    means "Son of the Renowned" from his father [Viśrava]{lang="sa"}.
    These two names appear together elsewhere in contemporary literature
    (Atharvaveda 8,10.28c, Śatapatha [Brāhmaṇa]{lang="sa"} 13.4.3.10),
    where, although the passages are obscure, he appears to be
    associated with wickedness, concealment, and theft. Although a god
    of wealth (*dhanada*), the name Kuvera is explained by
    lexicographers as "deformed". Probably he was originally a god of
    the underground, blessed with the earth's riches, yet deformed by
    its great pressure.

[^705]: The following verses are a treasure-trove of ancient Indian
    mythology, recording the names of deities otherwise lost to history.
    Many of the names are obscure and variant readings are recorded in
    manuscripts and noted in the commentary. My spellings follow
    Ānandajoti's translation, *[The Discourse on the Great
    Convention](https://suttacentral.nethttp://www.ancient-buddhist-texts.net/Texts-and-Translations/Safeguard/02x-Atirekani-Sattasuttani-23.htm)*,
    unless there is a reason to change. I try to identify the deities as
    best as I can, but many of them remain speculative.

[^706]: These deities are called *[dāsa]{lang="sa"}*, which normally
    means "slave" in Pali. But here, it seems, we have a singular
    instance of the old Vedic meaning, an uncivilized foe. The
    *[dāsas]{lang="sa"}* [Vṛtra]{lang="sa"}, Namuci, and
    [Vṛṣaśipra]{lang="sa"} are described as "deceivers"
    (*[māyin]{lang="sa"}*, Rig Veda 1.53.7; 2.11.10; 7.99.4; 10.73.7).
    This epithet is also used of the closely related *dasyu* (Rig Veda
    1.33.10; 4.16.9; 8.14.14; 10.73.5; see note on [DN
    5:11.5](https://suttacentral.net/dn5/en/sujato#11.5)). Such beings
    are said to be "godless" since they oppose the Vedic deities (Rig
    Veda 2.19.7; 3.31.19; 7.1.10; 10.11.6; 10.138.4, etc.), so we can
    translate as "heathen".

[^707]: I am not able to identify any of these names with confidence,
    but perhaps *[kuṭeṇḍu]{lang="sa"}* and *[viṭeṇḍu]{lang="sa"}* could
    be traced to *indu* ("moon"), thus "crooked moon" and "defective
    moon". Compare *[khaṇḍendu]{lang="sa"}*, a later term for Śiva as
    the crescent moon.

[^708]: The Sanskrit names are *[kiṭi]{lang="sa"}*,
    *[vikiṭi]{lang="sa"}*, *[bhṛgu]{lang="sa"}*, and
    *[bhṛkuṭi]{lang="sa"}*. Only *[bhṛgu]{lang="sa"}* is attested in the
    Vedas, but he was a sage, not a "crafty heathen".

[^709]: Candana appears in [MN
    134:6.4](https://suttacentral.net/mn134/en/sujato#6.4) and [SN
    40.11:1.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn40.11/en/sujato#1.1), and
    together with [Kāmaseṭṭha]{lang="sa"} in [DN
    32:10.4](https://suttacentral.net/dn32/en/sujato#10.4). *Candana*
    means "sandalwood", although the name of the god might also relate
    to its root "shining". \| *[Kāmaseṭṭha]{lang="sa"}* means "Chief of
    Sex", i.e. "Eros"; he does not seem to appear in a Brahmanical
    context.

[^710]: [Panāda]{lang="sa"} ("roarer") was the name of an ancient king
    who performed the horse sacrifice ([Thag
    2.22:1.1](https://suttacentral.net/thag2.22/en/sujato#1.1), [DN
    26:26.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn26/en/sujato#26.1)). In [Ja
    265](https://suttacentral.net/ja265/en/sujato) he is said to have
    been the son of [Vessavaṇa]{lang="sa"} (Kuvera). \|
    [Opamañña]{lang="sa"} is a descendant of the ascended sage Upamanyu
    ("zealous one"; see note on [MN
    99:10.3](https://suttacentral.net/mn99/en/sujato#10.3)).

[^711]: [Mātali]{lang="sa"} is the charioteer of Sakka (i.e. Indra; [SN
    11.6:1.5](https://suttacentral.net/sn11.6/en/sujato#1.5), [MN
    83:14.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn83/en/sujato#14.1), etc.), a
    role he plays throughout Brahmanical literature as well. He appears
    here as one of the centaur lords, and is father to the centaur
    [Sikhaṇḍī]{lang="sa"}, [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"}'s rival in love [DN
    21:1.6.8](https://suttacentral.net/dn21/en/sujato#1.6.8).

[^712]: The *gandharva* Citrasena ("Brightspear") appears in the
    Sanskrit [Purāṇas]{lang="sa"} as a friend of Arjuna in various
    adventures.

[^713]: *Nala* means "reed". There was a King Nala of Vidarbha whose
    love for his Queen [Damayantī]{lang="sa"} is celebrated in the
    [Mahābharata]{lang="sa"}. \| Janesabha is an alternate spelling of
    Janavasabha ([DN 18](https://suttacentral.net/dn18/en/sujato)).

[^714]: In [DN 21](https://suttacentral.net/dn21/en/sujato) we shall
