    learn of how the centaur [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"} ("Fivecrest") wooed
    Timbaru's daughter [Suriyavaccasā]{lang="sa"} ("Sunshine").

[^715]: Read *[nābhasa]{lang="sa"}*, which means "celestial, heavenly".
    The commentary says this was the name of a lake, which agrees with
    the legend that a [Nābhasa]{lang="sa"} was a son of Nala ("reed")
    and father of [Puṇḍarīka]{lang="sa"} ("lotus"). The Sanskrit here,
    however, has *[sahabhuṁ]{lang="sa"} [nāgo]{lang="sa"}*.

[^716]: *[Vesālā]{lang="sa"}* is from the adjectival form
    *[vesāla]{lang="sa"}* ("of [Vesālī]{lang="sa"}"). \| Pali *taccha*
    can represent Sanskrit *[takṣa]{lang="sa"}*, and the Sanskrit does
    indeed have *[takṣakaḥ]{lang="sa"}* here, so Tacchaka is probably
    "of [Takṣasilā]{lang="sa"}". This famous city, normally spelled
    [Takkasilā]{lang="sa"} in Pali, is Taxila in West Pakistan, an
    ancient center of learning.

[^717]: These *[nāgas]{lang="sa"}* are frequently mentioned together in
    Sanskrit literature such as the [Mahābhārata]{lang="sa"} (1.31.1a,
    2.9.9a, 5.101.9c), where they are also said to come from
    [Pāyāga]{lang="sa"} (3.83.72a: *[prayāgaṁ]{lang="sa"}
    [sapratiṣṭhānaṁ]{lang="sa"} [kambalāśvatarau]{lang="sa"}*).

[^718]: [Payāga]{lang="sa"} (modern Prayagraj, formerly Allahabad) is
    the sacred ford at the confluence of the Ganges and the
    [Yamunā]{lang="sa"} beside [Kosambī]{lang="sa"} (see [Bu Pj
    1:4.18](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-pj1/en/sujato#4.18)).

[^719]: The [Dhataraṭṭha]{lang="sa"} and [Erāvaṇa]{lang="sa"}
    *[nāgas]{lang="sa"}* also appear frequently in Sanskrit literature,
    and often in association with other deities in our text as part of a
    loose cluster of divinities (eg. [Harivamśa]{lang="sa"} 3.112--117).
    [Erāvaṇa]{lang="sa"} also appears at [Snp
    2.14:5.1](https://suttacentral.net/snp2.14/en/sujato#5.1). Here he
    is a *[nāga]{lang="sa"}* as in "dragon", but later tradition saw him
    as a *[nāga]{lang="sa"}* as in "elephant", in which role he became
    the mighty mount of Indra/Sakka.

[^720]: Here we see the eternal mythic struggle between birds and
    snakes, the creatures of the sky and the underworld.

[^721]: Birds are called "twice-born", once from the mother, once from
    the egg. "Twice-born" is also an epithet of brahmins ([Thig
    15.1:31.3](https://suttacentral.net/thig15.1/en/sujato#31.3)).

[^722]: The *[supaṇṇā]{lang="sa"}* (Sanskrit *[suparṇa]{lang="sa"}*,
    "brightwing") or *[garuḷā]{lang="sa"}* (Sanskrit
    *[garuḍā]{lang="sa"}*, "devourer") is the great eagle, king of
    birds, and later the vehicle of [Viṣṇu]{lang="sa"}. He has been
    falsely compared with the loathsome harpy; but his golden wings,
    closeness to the sun, and role as bearer of the nectar of
    immortality show that he is the Indian phoenix, the golden eagle of
    the sun. Unlike the western phoenix, he is not said to combust
    himself and be reborn anew. However this motif is merely the surface
    expression of the sun's fiery immortality. As the brother of the
    Dawn ([Aruṇa]{lang="sa"}), his golden wings at sunset "devour" the
    sun, and he disappears only to be reborn the next day.

[^723]: The Buddha reconciles even such inveterate enemies. In the
    Buddhist view, all of these beings have their place in the great
    pattern of nature.

[^724]: *Vajirahattha* ("thunderbolt-in-hand") is a synonym of
    *[vajirapāṇi]{lang="sa"}* ([DN
    3:1.21.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn3/en/sujato#1.21.3), [MN
    35:14.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn35/en/sujato#14.1)). It is a
    frequent epithet of Sakka in the Vedas (eg. Rig Veda 1.173.10a
    *indro vajrahastaḥ*), an identification confirmed by the commentary
    here.

[^725]: [Vāsava]{lang="sa"} is another epithet of Sakka (see below, [DN
    20:14.9](https://suttacentral.net/dn20/en/sujato#14.9)), meaning
    "endowed with wealth" (*vasu*; see for example Rig Veda 1.9.9a
    *[indraṁ]{lang="sa"} [vasupatiṁ]{lang="sa"}* "Indra, lord of
    wealth"). The Buddhist explanation of his name [SN
    11.13:9.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn11.13/en/sujato#9.1), rather,
    plays on the word *[āvasatha]{lang="sa"}* and calls him the "giver
    of a guesthouse". Later Brahmanical texts enumerated eight Vasus,
    with Indra as their lord, who are reckoned among the thirty-three.
    \| Sakka's wife is the demon (*asura*) princess [Sujā]{lang="sa"},
    daughter of Vepacitti, hence they are his "brothers", technically
    in-laws ([SN
    11.12:6.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn11.12/en/sujato#6.1)).

[^726]: The [Kālakañja]{lang="sa"} ("Blue-lotus") is said to be the
    lowest class of demons ([DN
    24:1.7.19](https://suttacentral.net/dn24/en/sujato#1.7.19)).

[^727]: The [Dānavas]{lang="sa"} are a prominent group of *asuras*
    otherwise unattested in early Pali. The name stems from "rivers,
    waters" after their mother [Danū]{lang="sa"}. \| Ghasa ("devourer")
    is occasionally mentioned as the name of a *[rakṣasa]{lang="sa"}*
    ([Rāmāyaṇa]{lang="sa"} 5.22.36a *[praghasā]{lang="sa"}
    [nāma]{lang="sa"} [rākṣasī]{lang="sa"}*). The commentary treats them
    as a single class of arrow-wielding *asuras*.

[^728]: Vepacitti means "wise thinker", Sucitti means "good thinker".
    Vepacitti was the lord of the *asuras* and Sakka's counterpart.
    Their relationship was complicated ([SN
    11.23](https://suttacentral.net/sn11.23/en/sujato)). Brahmanical
    literature regards him as the eldest son of [Danū]{lang="sa"} and
    hence chief of the [Dānavas]{lang="sa"}. The Sanskrit form is
    *vipracitti*, but in Buddhist texts it is incorrectly Sanskritized
    to *vemacitra*. Vepacitti's fame matches Sucitti's obscurity, for he
    does not seem to appear elsewhere.

[^729]: [Pahārāda]{lang="sa"} the ocean-loving *asura* lord appears in
    [AN 8.19](https://suttacentral.net/an8.19/en/sujato). His name
    (Sanskrit *[prahlāda]{lang="sa"}*) means "mirth". Later Brahmanical
    legends say he was a righteous son of the wicked
    [Hiraṇyakaśipu]{lang="sa"}, who hated him for his devotion to
    [Viṣṇu]{lang="sa"}. \| The story goes that when Namuci stole the
    soma from Indra, Indra's vengeance was frustrated by his vow not to
    harm him with anything wet or dry, in the dark or the light
    (Śatapatha [Brāhmaṇa]{lang="sa"} 12.7.3.1). Indra's ingenious
    solution was to dismember him with foam (Rig Veda 8.14.13) at dawn.
    Namuci's name is explained as *na-muci*, "not letting go" (the
    waters). In Buddhism, Namuci is a name of [Māra]{lang="sa"},
    although here [Māra]{lang="sa"} appears separately below..

[^730]: According to legend, Bali was the grandson of
    [Pahārāda]{lang="sa"} and son of Virocana. His hundred sons were
    dread warriors ([Bhāgavata]{lang="sa"} [Purāṇa]{lang="sa"} 8.10.30).
    The word *bali* also refers to a kind of sacrificial offering, but
    the roots of the term are obscure.

[^731]: *Virocana* was their grandfather. He was another adversary of
    Indra, despite which they were both said to have sought knowledge
    from [Prajāpatī]{lang="sa"}, but Virocana misunderstood since he saw
    only the surface meaning ([Chāndogya]{lang="sa"}
    [Upaniṣad]{lang="sa"} 8.7.2--8.5). Here he is associated with
    [Rāhu]{lang="sa"}, while [Gaṇeśa]{lang="sa"} [Purāṇa]{lang="sa"}
    2.29 says he was granted a crown by the sun which he then lost.
    These details hint at a connection with the eclipse, in which case
    *virocana* would be the rays that emanate during a solar eclipse.

[^732]: [Rāhu]{lang="sa"} is the mighty *asura* who "seizes" the moon
    ([SN 2.9](https://suttacentral.net/sn2.9/en/sujato)) or the sun ([SN
    2.10](https://suttacentral.net/sn2.10/en/sujato)) to create an
    eclipse. He is the son of Vepacitti.

[^733]: These four are normally treated in the suttas as physical
    properties. Each of them is worshiped in the Rig Veda as a deity,
    and the Upanishads list them together along with other elements (eg.
    [Bṛhadāraṇyaka]{lang="sa"} [Upaniṣad]{lang="sa"} 4.4.5). I am not
    aware of any earlier contexts that group the four together
    systematically as deities in this way.

[^734]: [Varuṇa]{lang="sa"} is invoked frequently in the Vedas, and in
    the suttas he is associated with other leading deities such as Indra
    and Soma ([DN
    13:25.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn13/en/sujato#25.2), [DN
    32:10.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn32/en/sujato#10.2), [SN
    11.3:5.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn11.3/en/sujato#5.1)). As one of
    the twelve children of Aditi he stood for a calendar month, and grew
    a wide and sometimes baffling array of associations---the oceans,
    water, the sky (at night), and justice. Vedic [Varuṇa]{lang="sa"}
    was the god of command, the king of tough rule. He was identified
    with the aristocrats, while his partner Mitra was the brahmins
    (Śatapatha [Brāhmaṇa]{lang="sa"} 4.1.4).

[^735]: *Soma* is the divine nectar that filled the ancient
    proto-Indo-Europeans with vitality. Its biological identity is
    disputed, but may have been ephedra. It is associated with the moon,
    hence the connection with [Varuṇa]{lang="sa"} as the night sky. \|
    *Yasa* means "fame, glory"; so far as I know it is not personified
    in early Brahmanical texts, and rarely later. The connection between
    these two is illuminated by such passages as Rig Veda 7.85.3. To
    uphold Indra and [Varuṇa]{lang="sa"} in battle, there is an offering
    of Soma, which is described as *[svayaśasaḥ]{lang="sa"}*,
    "Self-Glorious", i.e. the state of being high on speed, if speed
    were a god.

[^736]: In the Vedas, Mitra ("friend") is almost always paired with
    [Varuṇa]{lang="sa"}, and the appearance of [Mettā]{lang="sa"}
    ("friendliness") here echoes that closeness. He was the god of
    alliances. \| *[Karuṇā]{lang="sa"}* in the sense of "compassion"
    does not occur at all in pre-Buddhist Sanskrit. It is probably
    introduced here as companion to [Mettā]{lang="sa"} by association
    with Mitra. The Sanskrit here, however, is *[maitrī]{lang="sa"}
    [varuṇikā]{lang="sa"}*. From a Buddhist point of view, these are
    deities reborn due to the development of *[jhāna]{lang="sa"}* based
    on love and compassion.

[^737]: [Veṇhu]{lang="sa"} (variant *[veṇḍu]{lang="sa"}*) is the Pali
    spelling of Sanskrit *[viṣṇu]{lang="sa"}*, who appears only here and
    at [SN 2.12](https://suttacentral.net/sn2.12/en/sujato). In the Rig
    Veda he was a solar god who made three great strides (said to
    encompass the earth, the sky, and the heavens). \| A deity named
    [Sahalī]{lang="sa"} ("with plow") appears at [SN
    2.30:3.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn2.30/en/sujato#3.1) where he
    praises Makkhali [Gosāla]{lang="sa"}, but I cannot trace him
    anywhere else.

[^738]: *Yama* often refers to a god who guards the paths to the land of
    the dead ([SN
    1.33:10.3](https://suttacentral.net/sn1.33/en/sujato#10.3), [MN
    130:5.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn130/en/sujato#5.1)), lord of the
    [Yāma]{lang="sa"} gods. Here, however, it refers to "twins" who in
    the Vedas are often identified with the [Aśvins]{lang="sa"} (the
    twin horses yoked to the chariot). In the Vedas, it seems *asama*
    ("unequalled") is used as an epithet only, although the commentary
    says it is a name. \| In Pali these lines lack a verb, while the
    Sanskrit supplies *[āgataś]{lang="sa"}* ("come") instead of
    *[asamā]{lang="sa"}*, so perhaps we should read "the pair of Twins
    came".

[^739]: [Vāsava]{lang="sa"} is above at [DN
    20:12.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn20/en/sujato#12.3).

[^740]: *Purandara* ("Fortbreaker") is another epithet of Sakka or Indra
    (eg. Rig Veda 1.102.7). The Buddha reforms it to *Purindada*
    ("Firstgiver") at [SN
    11.12:2.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn11.12/en/sujato#2.1). But
    Indra's generosity is long renowned, eg. Rig Veda 1.10.6c: "He is
    the able one, and he will be able for us---Indra who distributes the
    goods" (*sa [śakra]{lang="sa"} uta naḥ [śakad]{lang="sa"} indro vasu
    [dayamānaḥ]{lang="sa"}*).

[^741]: Sanskrit has *[sabhikā]{lang="sa"}*. Most of the deities in the
    following verses do not seem to be met with elsewhere. The Sanskrit
    forms are often quite different but equally untraceable.

[^742]: Their names might mean "Unhurt" and "Hurter". The Sanskrit
    implausibly has *[romā]{lang="sa"}* ("Romans").

[^743]: For [Varuṇa]{lang="sa"} see above ([DN
    20:13.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn20/en/sujato#13.3)). \|
    Sahadhamma means "who share the same duty".

[^744]: The "Unfallen" and the "Unshakeable".

[^745]: The [Sūleyyas]{lang="sa"} might be "spear (or trident) bearers";
    *[śūli]{lang="sa"}* is a name of Shiva. \| Rucira means "brilliant,
    beautiful".

[^746]: *[Vāsavanesi]{lang="sa"}* means "seekers of
    [Vāsava]{lang="sa"}".

[^747]: "Equals" and "Great Equals".

[^748]: "Humans" and "Superhumans", but here perhaps "Descendants of
    Manu".

[^749]: As per [DN
    1:2.7.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn1/en/sujato#2.7.2).

[^750]: [DN 12.10.2](https://suttacentral.net/dn12.10.2/en/sujato).

[^751]: *Hari* means "yellow, green" and is the astrological color of
    the planet *budha* ("Mercury"). It came to have the general meaning
    of "sacred, holy" and was an epithet for various deities, which in
    the Rig Veda included Indra and Vishnu. However the astrological
    connection is suggested by the references to Mars and Venus below.

[^752]: "Dwellers in the Red Place (= planet)", i.e. "Martians".

[^753]: *[Pāraga]{lang="sa"}* means "one who has crossed over".

[^754]: The deities seem to represent the three main lights that
    announce a new day: the morning star, the newborn sun, and the aura
    of the dawn. \| *Sukka* ("Bright") is the name of the planet Venus,
    the morning star. \| *[Aruṇa]{lang="sa"}* is the dawn, a major Vedic
    deity signifying the arising of consciousness and order. \|
    *Karambha* means "porridge", which was offered to the sun under the
    name [Pūṣan]{lang="sa"} (Rig Veda 3.52.7, 6.56.1; Śatapatha
    [Brāhmaṇa]{lang="sa"} 4.2.5.22; [Kauṣītaki]{lang="sa"}
    [Brāhmaṇa]{lang="sa"} 6.8.18; [Maitrāyaṇī]{lang="sa"}
    [Saṁhitā]{lang="sa"} 3.10.6). Hence he became known as
    *[karambhād]{lang="sa"}*, "porridge-eater". Apparently the sun lost
    its teeth and could only eat porridge (Śatapatha
    [Brāhmaṇa]{lang="sa"} 1.7.4.8). Being toothless like a baby, and
    lacking the risen sun's "teeth" (i.e. "rays"), he probably
    represents the "newborn sun". Compare for example Rig Veda 10.35,
    where [Pūṣan]{lang="sa"} is invoked, along with many other deities,
    in a hymn to the dawn.

[^755]: [Mahāsaṅgīti]{lang="sa"} has *[veghanasā]{lang="sa"}*, for which
    the Buddha Jayanthi variant *vekhanasa* is preferable. We meet a
    wanderer of that name in [MN
    80](https://suttacentral.net/mn80/en/sujato), and in Sanskrit
    *[vaikhānasa]{lang="sa"}* is a general term for ascetics; but this
    meaning seems unlikely in context. The astrologer
    [Varāhamitra]{lang="sa"} names it a constellation
    ([Bṛhat]{lang="sa"}-[saṁhitā]{lang="sa"} 47.62), but that is much
    later. The Sanskrit here is *[nīlakavāsinī]{lang="sa"}* ("Dwellers
    in the Blue"), which pairs well with *[lohitavāsino]{lang="sa"}*
    above. *[Nīlavāsa]{lang="sa"}* is a name for Saturn, whose color is
    blue.

[^756]: *[Odātagayha]{lang="sa"}* is probably "white planet", i.e. the
    sun. Sanskrit has *[avadātakeśā]{lang="sa"}* ("white-haired").
    Compare the description of solar gods [Sūrya]{lang="sa"} at Rig Veda
    1.50.8 as *[śociṣkeśaṁ]{lang="sa"} [vicakṣaṇa]{lang="sa"}*
    ("flame-haired, brilliant") and [Varuṇa]{lang="sa"} at Rig Veda
    8.41.9 as *[śvetā]{lang="sa"} [vicakṣaṇā]{lang="sa"}* ("white,
    brilliant").

[^757]: Sanskrit has *[pītakavāsinī]{lang="sa"}*, "dwellers in the
    yellow", either Mercury or Jupiter.

[^758]: The *[sadāmatta]{lang="sa"}* ("everdrunk") gods appear in some
    later Buddhist texts, alongside the *[māyādharas]{lang="sa"}* (or
    *[mālādharas]{lang="sa"}*), but they don't seem to appear in a Vedic
    context. \| I cannot find any reference to the
    *[hāragaja]{lang="sa"}* gods. Sanskrit has *[hāritakā]{lang="sa"}*,
    perhaps "sons of [Harītī]{lang="sa"}" (the *[yakkhinī]{lang="sa"}*;
    but see below, [DN
    20:20.12](https://suttacentral.net/dn20/en/sujato#20.12)).

[^759]: "Assorted" is *missaka*, not elsewhere attested as a name of
    gods.

[^760]: Pajjuna (Sanskrit *parjanya*) is a Vedic god of thunderstorms
    closely associated with Indra/Sakka. His daughters appear in [SN
    1.39](https://suttacentral.net/sn1.39/en/sujato) and [SN
    1.40](https://suttacentral.net/sn1.40/en/sujato). Sanskrit has
    instead *[śuddhakā]{lang="sa"} [rucikā]{lang="sa"}* ("pure,
    bright").

[^761]: The gods of Tusita and Yama are normal parts of the Buddhist
    cosmology. Neither *khemiya* nor the Sanskrit *[kṣemaka]{lang="sa"}*
    appear to be the names of gods elsewhere, and the commentary
    explains that they dwell in both the Tusita and Yama realms.

[^762]: The commentary acknowledges the variant readings
    *[kaṭṭhakā]{lang="sa"}* (from "stick" or "plowed") and
    *[kathakā]{lang="sa"}* ("reciters"), while Sanskrit has
    *[kṛṣṇuktāś]{lang="sa"}* ("reciters of the black"). Perhaps related
    to Sanskrit *[kāṭhaka]{lang="sa"}*, a recension of the Black Yajur
    Veda taught by the sage [Kaṭha]{lang="sa"}.

[^763]: Perhaps from *lamba* ("hang, droop") in reference to deities of
    the sunset (cp. [Mahābhārata]{lang="sa"} 4.31.4c *[sūrye]{lang="sa"}
    vilambati*). Sanskrit has *[lumbinī]{lang="sa"}
    [lumbinīśreṣṭhā]{lang="sa"}*, which is perhaps normalized to mean
    "of [Lumbinī]{lang="sa"}".

[^764]: "Shining" (*joti*) is used widely of many gods, including the
    Sun and Indra. \| The commentary derives *[āsavā]{lang="sa"}* from
    *[āsa]{lang="sa"}* ("wish") not *[āsava]{lang="sa"}* ("defilement"),
    explaining that they achieved their status due to desire
    (*chandavasena*). This agrees with the Sanskrit
    *[svāśiṣā]{lang="sa"}* ("well-wish, prayer"), i.e. words of wishing
    or blessing uttered over the sacrifice (see Rig Veda 10.44.5; cp.
    8.44.23). This is a case where using the Pali in a translation would
    be actively misleading.

[^765]: *[Pavuṭṭha]{lang="sa"}* ("shed"), from *pavasati* ("stay away"),
    seems to be unique in the Pali in this sense. Commentary glosses
    *vigata*, "disappeared". \| The first part of this verse, according
    to the commentary, addresses the [Saṅgha]{lang="sa"} as a group in
    the singular, but I translate in plural for clarity.

[^766]: [Subrahmā]{lang="sa"} appears at [SN
    2.17](https://suttacentral.net/sn2.17/en/sujato) and [SN
    6.6](https://suttacentral.net/sn6.6/en/sujato). \| Paramatta appears
    only here in Pali. In Brahmanical texts it is a common term for the
    highest conception of [Brahmā]{lang="sa"} as the "supreme soul"
    (*[paramātman]{lang="sa"}*) of the cosmos. This term came into
    common usage later, but is found occasionally in earlier texts such
    as [Maitrāyaṇī]{lang="sa"} [Saṁhitā]{lang="sa"} 2.9.1.

[^767]: [Sanaṅkumāra]{lang="sa"} makes regular guest appearances in the
    suttas ([DN 3](https://suttacentral.net/dn3/en/sujato), [DN
    18](https://suttacentral.net/dn18/en/sujato), [DN
    19](https://suttacentral.net/dn19/en/sujato), [DN
    27](https://suttacentral.net/dn27/en/sujato), [SN
    6.11](https://suttacentral.net/sn6.11/en/sujato), [AN
    11.10](https://suttacentral.net/an11.10/en/sujato)). \| Tissa is a
    common name, meaning "born under the star Sirius". Perhaps this is
    the former monk of that name who was reborn in the
    [Brahmā]{lang="sa"} realm ([AN
    6.34](https://suttacentral.net/an6.34/en/sujato)).

[^768]: The moniker "Great [Brahmā]{lang="sa"}"
    (*[mahābrahmā]{lang="sa"}*) is here used of an individual, but
    sometimes it is a class of leading [Brahmās]{lang="sa"}.

[^769]: "God Almighty" (*issara*) is a [Brahmā]{lang="sa"} god to whom
    the creation of the world is falsely attributed ([DN
    24:2.14.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn24/en/sujato#2.14.3), [MN
    101:22.5](https://suttacentral.net/mn101/en/sujato#22.5), [AN
    3.61:1.6](https://suttacentral.net/an3.61/en/sujato#1.6)).

[^770]: Sanskrit has *[hāritī]{lang="sa"}*, the name of the fabled
    *[yakkhiṇī]{lang="sa"}* of [Madhurā]{lang="sa"} converted by the
    Buddha.

[^771]: This late sutta exhibits considerable literary sophistication.
    The A-plot concerns Sakka's rejection of his violent past and
    embrace of the Dhamma, while the B-plot concerns
    [Pañcasikkha]{lang="sa"}'s more modest growth from a libertine to a
    married man. \| This discourse is quoted by name and a passage
    discussed at [SN 22.4](https://suttacentral.net/sn22.4/en/sujato).
    In addition, the dialogue at [MN
    37](https://suttacentral.net/mn37/en/sujato) picks up directly where
    this leaves off and may be considered a sequel. A Sanskrit parallel
    published by Ernst Waldschmidt in 1979 is available on SuttaCentral
    as [SF 241](https://suttacentral.net/sf241/san/waldschmidt). A
    different discourse of the same name is found at [SN
    35.118](https://suttacentral.net/sn35.118/en/sujato).

[^772]: Rhys Davids follows the commentary in translating this "the cave
    of Indra's [Sāl]{lang="sa"} Tree". However the Sanskrit
    *[yāvacchailaguhāyām]{lang="sa"}* supports "hill cave".

[^773]: Introduced in a minor role in [DN
    18](https://suttacentral.net/dn18/en/sujato), [DN
    19](https://suttacentral.net/dn19/en/sujato), and [DN
    20](https://suttacentral.net/dn20/en/sujato),
    [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"} gets his moment to shine in this sutta.
    Outside of the [Dīghanikāya]{lang="sa"}, he appears only in [SN
    35.119](https://suttacentral.net/sn35.119/en/sujato). His name means
    "Fivecrest", evidently in reference to his impressive hairdo; a
    nymph or group of nymphs bore the synonymous name
    [Pañcacūḍā]{lang="sa"}, "Five Topknots". The centaur (*gandhabba*)
    was a wild rogue of music, dancing, and sex. He must be tamed, and
    here this is achieved through the power of love and filial respect.
    A less diplomatic approach is taken in Atharvaveda 4.37.7, where the
    *gandharva* with his "egg-crest" (*[śikhaṇḍino]{lang="sa"}*) is
    threatened with castration.

[^774]: The "arched harp" (*[vīṇa]{lang="sa"}*) was a multi-stringed
    instrument held in the lap, with the strings attached across a
    curved open arch or bow. It is not the modern Indian instrument
    called veena, which is a lute or stick zither. *Gandhabbas* are
    closely associated with music. [Māra]{lang="sa"}'s harp is described
    in the same way ([SN
    4.23:10.7](https://suttacentral.net/sn4.23/en/sujato#10.7)), and
    tradition says they were in fact the same instrument.

[^775]: In the Buddhist hierarchy of divinity, *gandhabbas* occupy a
    more humble place than the gods of the thirty-three, yet here
    [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"} appears with them in a position of honor. In
    [DN 19:28.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn19/en/sujato#28.3) we saw
    that the even more exalted [Brahmā]{lang="sa"} takes the form of
    [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"}. This fluidity is characteristic of old
    Vedic cosmology, where there is no clear hierarchy of divinity. The
    formalized hierarchies in Buddhism and Hinduism are a later
    conception.

[^776]: We will learn later of how Sakka's appearance could be too
    disturbing for the Buddha while on retreat ([DN
    21:1.10.13](https://suttacentral.net/dn21/en/sujato#1.10.13)).
    [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"}, on the other hand, would appear alone,
    without a retinue. Note, however, that on subsequent occasions Sakka
    showed no such reticence, either because he was already a
    stream-enterer or because the Buddha was not in solitary retreat.

[^777]: The idea that the Buddha would be wooed by a love song seems
    like an absurd conceit, until you take into account the centaurs'
    dangerous reputation as inveterate lechers. [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"}
    wants to show that he is maturing and finally seeking a commitment
    in a respectable marriage.

[^778]: [Suriyavaccasā]{lang="sa"} ("Sunshine") accords with the Vedic
    conception of the wife as the Sun ([Sūryā]{lang="sa"}, Rig Veda
    10.85) who takes as husband Soma, the moon. She appears in
    Atharvaveda 8.10.27, which invokes the *gandharvas* with their
    female counterpart the "nymphs" (Pali *[accharā]{lang="sa"}*,
    Sanskrit *[āpsarásā]{lang="sa"}*).

[^779]: In Sanskrit literature, Timbaru (Sanskrit *tumburu*) was an
    elder *gandharva* musician in the courts of Indra and Kuvera.
    According to [Rāmāyaṇa]{lang="sa"} 3.4, he was cursed with a
    monstrous form by Kuvera due to an inappropriate lust for the nymph
    [Rambhā]{lang="sa"}. Upon his defeat at the hands of
    [Rāma]{lang="sa"} he was restored to his former status.
    [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"} would have wanted to avoid a similar fate.
    Rather than [Suriyavaccasā]{lang="sa"}, the Sanskrit literature
    mentions his daughters [Manuvantī]{lang="sa"} and
    [Sukeśī]{lang="sa"} ([Vāyupurāṇa]{lang="sa"} 69.49 =
    [Brahmāṇḍapurāṇa]{lang="sa"} 2.7.13).

[^780]: *[Aṅgīrasi]{lang="sa"}* means "shining one" and is
    etymologically linked with *agni* ("fire").
    [Suriyavaccasā]{lang="sa"} earned the name due to both her radiant
    nature and her family connections: her father Timbaru was a son of
    [Kaśyapa]{lang="sa"}, who was (according to certain lineages)
    brother of [Aṅgīras]{lang="sa"}, from whom the
    [Aṅgīrasas]{lang="sa"} were descended. In the Arthavaveda---the only
    early non-Buddhist source for a nymph named
    [Suriyavaccasā]{lang="sa"}---*[aṅgīrasi]{lang="sa"}* is associated
    with witchcraft (Atharvaveda 12.5.52). *[Aṅgīrasa]{lang="sa"}* is
    also a patronymic of the Buddha ([DN
    32:3.14](https://suttacentral.net/dn32/en/sujato#3.14)).

[^781]: "Saints" is *[arahantā]{lang="sa"}*, which I normally translate
    as "perfected ones".

[^782]: *[Parinibbāpaya]{lang="sa"}*, "please quench me".
    [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"} subverts imagery employed by the
    Buddha---quenching thirst, cooling breezes, extinguished flames---to
    erotic effect.

[^783]: [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"} is showing off his literary skill. The
    same image, the elephant, illustrates opposing qualities, peaceful
    and violent. In these two sides of sensual desire, he reveals,
    unconsciously perhaps, the rapacious side of his own nature. For
    [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"}, the elephant bursting its bonds illustrates
    his crazed devotion, whereas later it illustrates breaking free of
    desire ([DN
    21:1.12.45](https://suttacentral.net/dn21/en/sujato#1.12.45)).

[^784]: For how a married couple may stay together in this life and the
    next, see [AN 4.55](https://suttacentral.net/an4.55/en/sujato).

[^785]: [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"} is using the present participle
    *[jigīsāno]{lang="sa"}* in the historical present.

[^786]: In Indian culture, a *gandharva* wedding is a pure love-match,
    without the blessing of parents or priests. Here, however,
    [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"} wants to do the right thing and get the
    father's blessing.

[^787]: If [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"} was seeking the Buddha's approval for
    his love-match, he was doomed to disappointment, as the Buddha and
    his mendicants do not involve themselves in arranging marriages. The
    Buddha sidesteps the issue by politely complementing
    [Pañcasikha]{lang="sa"} on his musicianship.

[^788]: "When did you compose" is also at [MN
    56:30.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn56/en/sujato#30.1).

[^789]: "Sister" (*[bhaginī]{lang="sa"}*) is a respectful term of
    address, but is normally used in a chaste sense. When
    [Raṭṭhapāla]{lang="sa"} calls his wives "sister", they faint in
    shock ([MN 82:23.4](https://suttacentral.net/mn82/en/sujato#23.4)).

[^790]: [Sikhaṇḍī]{lang="sa"} ("egg-crested") is the term for the
    centaur as seductive threat to women in Atharvaveda 4.37.7. It is
    also a poetic term for a peacock.

[^791]: It is rare for the Buddha to address a deity as "venerable"
    (*[āyasmā]{lang="sa"}*), a term usually reserved for monks.
    [Moggallāna]{lang="sa"} addresses Sakka as "the venerable Kosiya" at
    [MN 37:10.6](https://suttacentral.net/mn37/en/sujato#10.6);
    [Mahākassapa]{lang="sa"} calls him Kosiya without honorific at [Ud
    3.7:3.12](https://suttacentral.net/ud3.7/en/sujato#3.12). \| The
    word *kosiya* is explained by the commentaries as "owl", which, if
    correct, would have been the totem for a clan of that name. It is,
    however, a patronymic: Rig Veda 1.10.11 has *indra
    [kauśika]{lang="sa"}* which means "Indra, son of [Kuśika]{lang="sa"}
    (or [Kuśa]{lang="sa"})". *[Kuśa]{lang="sa"}* grass is critical to
    the performance of Vedic rites, and the label probably initially
    implied "Brahmanized", i.e. a king whose reign was authorized
    according to Vedic ritual. Kosiya is said to be a low class family
    name ([Bu Pc
    2:2.1.18](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-pc2/en/sujato#2.1.18)).

[^792]: A *[paricārikā]{lang="sa"}* is a maid attending on a nymph ([MN
    37:8.12](https://suttacentral.net/mn37/en/sujato#8.12)). \|
    [Bhūjati]{lang="sa"} (variant *[bhuñjatī]{lang="sa"}*) does not seem
    to appear elsewhere.

[^793]: Sakka pointedly ignores this, but perhaps his shame at
    disturbing the Buddha's meditation explains his oblique method of
    gaining an audience.

[^794]: This implies she was a stream-enterer.

[^795]: In Buddhism, sex is mutable between lives and even within one
    life ([Bu Pj
    1:10.6.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-pj1/en/sujato#10.6.1)).
    Later Buddhists sometimes held that the status of a male was
    preferable. Here this is not stated outright, but the narrative
    implies that she thought her station was improved when becoming a
    male. Notably, it is not the Buddha who says this, but Sakka,
    relaying the story of another deity. That the proponent was a woman,
    at least formerly, is a trope of Indian literature: a woman is the
    ideal misogynist (see eg. [Ja
    61](https://suttacentral.net/ja61/en/sujato) or
    [Śivapurāna]{lang="sa"} chapter 4). Here, though, the situation is
    nuanced, because despite her (presumed) inferior status, she has
    better rebirth than the monks she served.

[^796]: The realm of centaurs is "inferior" because the centaurs are
    known for their ribald sexuality.

[^797]: *[Kutomukhā]{lang="sa"}*, which occurs only in this sutta,
    contrasts with *[sammukhā]{lang="sa"}* ("in the presence of", "from
    the mouth of"). The commentary explains it as a rhetorical question,
    "Were you distracted or falling asleep?" Alternatively, it might
    mean "From whom did you learn the Buddha's teaching?" It recurs
    later in the verses. This is not the only such case:
    *[duddiṭṭharūpaṁ]{lang="sa"}* and *[kāyaṁ]{lang="sa"}
    [brahmapurohitaṁ]{lang="sa"}* are other examples of terms found
    nowhere else in the Pali canon, yet found here both in prose and in
    verse. Clearly one has been copied from the other; it seems to me
    that the rarity of these terms and the greater coherence of the
    verses suggests that the prose is a (somewhat altered) summary of
    the verses.

[^798]: The commentary explains the verb "gained"
    (*[paṭilabhiṁsu]{lang="sa"}*) as serving dual roles here: "gaining"
    of "absorption mindfulness" (*[jhānasatiṁ]{lang="sa"}*), upon which
    they "gain" rebirth in the [Brahmā]{lang="sa"} realm. However, the
    verses below speak of "recollecting" (*[anussaraṁ]{lang="sa"}*) the
    Buddha's teachings at this juncture. Compare [Bu NP
    23:1.1.20](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-bu-vb-np23/en/sujato#1.1.20),
    where the king "remembered" his long-forgotten promise
    (*[vissaritvā]{lang="sa"} cirena [satiṁ]{lang="sa"}
    [paṭilabhitvā]{lang="sa"}*). [AN
    4.191:2.24](https://suttacentral.net/an4.191/en/sujato#2.24) speaks
    of the "arising of memory" (*[satuppādo]{lang="sa"}*) in a deity
    when reminded of Dhamma passages they once recited as a monk. \| The
    phrase *[kāyaṁ]{lang="sa"} [brahmapurohitaṁ]{lang="sa"}* is
    syntactically incongruous; I think it was clumsily copied from the
    verses below, where the required verb (*[ajjhagaṁsu]{lang="sa"}*) is
    present. The commentary explains it as "a Minister of
    [Brahmā]{lang="sa"}'s body" (*[brahmapurohitasarīraṁ]{lang="sa"}*),
    but in such contexts *[kāya]{lang="sa"}* usually means "host".

[^799]: When bowing, it is a special sign of devotion to touch the feet
    of the one revered (eg. [MN
    89:9.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn89/en/sujato#9.1)).

[^800]: Here Gopaka shifts from speaking about them in third person to
    speaking to them in second person.

[^801]: *Tidiva* is an abbreviated reference to the thirty-three gods.

[^802]: I.e. she was a lay person.

[^803]: Up until now, the verses have been spoken by Gopaka, but here a
    narrator appears.

[^804]: Normally rebirth is described as passing away in one realm then
    being reborn in another. Yet here the gods of the thirty-three---in
    their traditional abode on Mount Meru---were watching them literally
    fly from one realm to another.

[^805]: The truncated *janind'* is not vocative *janinda* for Sakka, but
    nominative *janindo* for the Buddha.

[^806]: "Sakyan Sage" (*sakyamuni*) became a favorite epithet of the
    Buddha, but in early texts it appears only here and the Ratanasutta
    ([Snp 2.1:4.2](https://suttacentral.net/snp2.1/en/sujato#4.2)).

[^807]: "Sons" in a spiritual sense.

[^808]: The commentary says, "In this dispensation the explanation of
    the teaching is such that because of it, disciples become endowed
    with such qualities" (*ettha [sāsane]{lang="sa"}
    [evarūpā]{lang="sa"} [dhammappakāsanā]{lang="sa"}, [yāya]{lang="sa"}
    [sāvakā]{lang="sa"} etehi [guṇehi]{lang="sa"}
    [samannāgatā]{lang="sa"} honti*). This explains the feminine
    *[etādisī]{lang="sa"}*; *[pakasanā]{lang="sa"}*, which is normally
    neuter, is feminine here. This use is fairly common in later texts
    (it occurs at the conclusion of the [Ṭīkā]{lang="sa"} for this
    sutta).

[^809]: *[Kiṅkaṅkhati]{lang="sa"}* is a unique term for "doubt".

[^810]: The end point of Gopaka's speech is hard to determine, as there
    is no *-ti* to mark it. Various editions and translations either
    ignore the issue or end it in different places. The next line marks
    the beginning of Sakka's direct speech to the Buddha, and I think it
    makes the best sense to assume that up to here he has been relating
    the story he was told.

[^811]: This question hints at Sakka's own evolution from the battlegod
    of the Vedas to an acolyte of peace.

[^812]: Jealousy is wanting what others have, while stinginess is not
    wanting to share what you have.

[^813]: Compare with such contexts as the side branch of dependent
    origination at [DN
    15:9.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn15/en/sujato#9.1), the origins of
    disputes at [Snp 4.11](https://suttacentral.net/snp4.11/en/sujato),
    and the analysis of proliferation at [MN
    18](https://suttacentral.net/mn18/en/sujato). Note the constructive
    use of the "yes, and" method of questioning. Sakka finds wisdom due
    to his curiosity, neither being too-easily sated with a simple
    answer, nor quibbling that the answer is inadequate, but building on
    the foundations of understanding.

[^814]: Compare [Snp
    4.11:4.1](https://suttacentral.net/snp4.11/en/sujato#4.1).

[^815]: Here we depart from the normal sequence, which is that feelings
    give rise to craving (*[taṇhā]{lang="sa"}*). In [Snp
    4.11:6.1](https://suttacentral.net/snp4.11/en/sujato#6.1) it is said
    that pleasure and pain give rise to desire, not thought.

[^816]: "Judgments that emerge from the proliferation of perceptions"
    renders *[papañcasaññāsaṅkhā]{lang="sa"}*. Again the sequence
    departs from [MN
    18:16.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn18/en/sujato#16.1), which says
    that thoughts---which are normal and morally neutral psychological
    processes---give rise to "proliferation" (*[papañca]{lang="sa"}*),
    which is when craving and delusion cause thought to spin out of
    control. "Proliferation" then solidifies into "judgments" that fuel
    an individual's delusion of "self" persisting through time.

[^817]: *[Jaññā]{lang="sa"}* is 3rd singular optative (cp. [AN
    9.6:3.4](https://suttacentral.net/an9.6/en/sujato#3.4)).

[^818]: That is, second *[jhāna]{lang="sa"}* is better than first
    *[jhāna]{lang="sa"}*.

[^819]: Overwhelmingly, the suttas speak of happiness in the path to
    liberation. They do, however, also acknowledge that sometimes
    temporary states of sadness leading to disillusionment can spur a
    person on the path.

[^820]: It is hard to know what the text is getting at here, as there is
    no "sadness" in any *[jhāna]{lang="sa"}*.

[^821]: Here the Buddha emphasizes the psychological and spiritual
    underpinnings of the "monastic code" (*[pātimokkha]{lang="sa"}*).

[^822]: The suttas frequently describe three kinds of search: for
    sensual pleasures, for continued existence, and for a spiritual path
    (eg. [SN
    45.161:7.3](https://suttacentral.net/sn45.161/en/sujato#7.3)). The
    last search led the Buddha to awakening ([MN
    26:15.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn26/en/sujato#15.1)).

[^823]: Sakka switches his term of address for the Buddha from
    *[mārisa]{lang="sa"}* to the more respectful *bhante* here, then
    back to *[mārisa]{lang="sa"}* below. This passage is found at [MN
    114:24.5](https://suttacentral.net/mn114/en/sujato#24.5), where
    *bhante* is used, suggesting it was imported from there.

[^824]: For a more detailed analysis of this, see [MN
    152](https://suttacentral.net/mn152/en/sujato).

[^825]: The Pali *ekanta* ("single") here is a little tricky; it could
    mean either "the same" or "of one goal". The Sanskrit has just *eka*
    (*[ekakāmā]{lang="sa"} [ekacchandāḥ]{lang="sa"}*, "one desire, one
    wish"), while the commentary explains the first and last terms as
    "one goal" and the middle terms as "one".

[^826]: The Buddha points out that spiritual teachers, when faced with a
    world of experiential diversity, tend to prioritize their own
    experience or doctrine, dismissing others as less real or
    meaningful.

[^827]: This is the final substantive question of the series. It is
    picked up in [MN
    37:2.2](https://suttacentral.net/mn37/en/sujato#2.2) where Sakka, in
    a subsequent visit, inquires further as to the nature of those who
    have realized the ultimate goal.

[^828]: "Turbulence" is *[ejā]{lang="sa"}*, from a root meaning "motion,
    agitation", and hence the opposite of the "imperturbable" (*aneja*)
    peace of the Buddha ([SN
    35.90](https://suttacentral.net/sn35.90/en/sujato)).

[^829]: Compare [Ajātasattu]{lang="sa"}'s experience at [DN
    2:15.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn2/en/sujato#15.1).

[^830]: "Nectar" is *[ojā]{lang="sa"}*, in Vedic texts called *soma* or
    *[amṛta]{lang="sa"}* ("ambrosia" of immortality). The battle over
    nectar by gods (*deva*) and demons (*asura*) is a very ancient
    element of Indo-European mythology.

[^831]: Here Sakka makes a clean break from the violent delights he
    enjoyed in former (Vedic) times.

[^832]: *[Punarāyu]{lang="sa"}* is a unique term. It probably means that
    Sakka can extend his current fortunate birth due to the merit of
    Dhamma.

[^833]: To die "unconfused" or "consciously" is said to be a benefit of
    Dhamma practice (eg. [AN
    5.215:2.3](https://suttacentral.net/an5.215/en/sujato#2.3)).

[^834]: "According to method" (*[ñāyena]{lang="sa"}*) recalls
    *[ñāyapaṭipanno]{lang="sa"}* as a description of the noble ones. It
    means practicing according with the noble eightfold path, a "method"
    that yields definite results.

[^835]: This refers to the possibility of achieving perfection
    (*[arahattā]{lang="sa"}*).

[^836]: That is, after passing away as Sakka and being reborn as a
    human, he will subsequently return to the heaven realm.

[^837]: He predicts that his final life will be in this high realm that
    is inhabited by non-returners ([AN
    9.12:6.7](https://suttacentral.net/an9.12/en/sujato#6.7)).

[^838]: *Karomasi* is middle imperative 1st plural, "we should do",
    glossed by the commentary as "we should pay homage"
    (*[namakkāraṁ]{lang="sa"} karoma*).

[^839]: *[Samaṁ]{lang="sa"}* is an indeclinable in the sense "likewise".

[^840]: *[Sāmaṁ]{lang="sa"}* is an indeclinable in the sense "oneself,
    personally, one's own".

[^841]: A *gandharva* wedding must be a mutual love match.

[^842]: "Touching the ground with his hand" is a unique gesture in the
    early Pali, immortalized in later legend when the Buddha touches the
    ground to invoke the Earth Goddess as witness of his striving.

[^843]: Sakka is also said to have been accompanied by 80,000 deities at
    [SN 40.10:4.4](https://suttacentral.net/sn40.10/en/sujato#4.4). This
    is one of many indications that this is a late discourse, several of
    which have been touched on above: late or unique terminology; fancy
    literary styles; adoption of doctrinal passages from elsewhere in a
    sometimes clumsy fashion; and so on. In determining whether a sutta
    is late or early, we do not rely on a single definitive reason, but
    on a cluster of independent criteria which taken together are most
    easily explained in terms of historical development.

[^844]: This discourse is copied from [MN
    10](https://suttacentral.net/mn10/en/sujato). The section on the
    four noble truths has been expanded with material mostly drawn from
    [MN 141](https://suttacentral.net/mn141/en/sujato). These discourses
    are the most influential texts for modern Theravada meditation,
    prompting countless modern commentaries. Comparative study of the
    several parallel versions reveals that this discourse, while
    comprised almost entirely of early material, was compiled in this
    form as one of the latest texts in the Pali suttas. \| While
    mindfulness is always useful ([SN
    46.53:15.4](https://suttacentral.net/sn46.53/en/sujato#15.4)), the
    "establishment of mindfulness" (*[satipaṭṭhāna]{lang="sa"}*) refers
    especially to a conscious development of contemplative practices
    based on mindfulness, i.e. "mindfulness meditation".

[^845]: The phrase *[ekāyano]{lang="sa"} maggo* ("path to convergence")
    is given multiple meanings in commentaries and ancient translations.
    Outside of *[satipaṭṭhāna]{lang="sa"}*, it is used in only one
    context in Pali, where it means to "come together with" ([MN
    12:37.5](https://suttacentral.net/mn12/en/sujato#37.5)). At [SN
    47.18:3.4](https://suttacentral.net/sn47.18/en/sujato#3.4) the
    phrase is spoken by [Brahmā]{lang="sa"}, which suggests it was a
    Brahmanical term. At [Bṛhadāraṇyaka]{lang="sa"}
    [Upaniṣad]{lang="sa"} 2.4.11---a passage full of details shared with
    the suttas---it means a place where things unite or converge. Thus
    *[satipaṭṭhāna]{lang="sa"}* leads to everything "coming together as
    one". In other words, as seventh factor of the noble eightfold path,
    it leads to *[samādhi]{lang="sa"}*, the eighth factor ([SN
    45.1:3.9](https://suttacentral.net/sn45.1/en/sujato#3.9); see also
    [MN 44:12.3](https://suttacentral.net/mn44/en/sujato#12.3)).

[^846]: The idiom *[kāye]{lang="sa"} [kāyānupassī]{lang="sa"}*,
    literally "observes a body in the body" refers to focusing on a
    specific aspect of embodied experience, such as the breath, the
    postures, etc. \| "Keen" (or "ardent", *[ātāpī]{lang="sa"}*) implies
    effort, while "aware" (*[sampajāno]{lang="sa"}*) is the wisdom of
    understanding situation and context. \| "Covetousness and
    displeasure" (*[abhijjhādomanassaṁ]{lang="sa"}*) are the strong
    forms of desire and aversion that are overcome by sense restraint in
    preparation for meditation.

[^847]: "Feelings" (*[vedanā]{lang="sa"}*) are the basic tones of
    pleasant, painful, or neutral, not the complexes we call "emotions".

[^848]: "Mind" (*citta*) is simple awareness. In meditation contexts,
    "mind" is often similar in meaning to *[samādhi]{lang="sa"}*.

[^849]: "Principles" (*[dhammā]{lang="sa"}*) are the natural "systems"
    of cause and effect that underlie the "teachings". The renderings
    "mind objects" or "mental qualities" are incorrect, as many of the
    things spoken of in this section are neither mind objects nor mental
    qualities. "Phenomena" is a possible translation, but the emphasis
    is not on the "appearance" of things, but on the "principles"
    governing their conditional relations.

[^850]: The context here---a mendicant gone to the forest---establishes
    that this practice takes place in the wider context of the Gradual
    Training. Indeed, this whole sutta can be understood as an expansion
    of this phrase, mentioned briefly at [DN
    2:67.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn2/en/sujato#67.3).

[^851]: The most fundamental meditation instruction in Buddhism. Notice
    how the Buddha phrases it: not "concentrate on the breath" as an
    object, but rather "breathing" as an activity to which one brings
    mindfulness.

[^852]: The stages of breath meditation are not meant to be done
    deliberately, but to be observed and understood as the natural
    process of deepening meditation. When starting out, the breath is
    somewhat rough and coarse.

[^853]: Over time, the breath becomes more subtle and soft.

[^854]: Contextually the idiom "whole body" (*[sabbakāya]{lang="sa"}*)
    here refers to the breath, marking the fuller and more continuous
    awareness that arises with tranquility. Some practitioners, however,
    interpret it as the "whole physical body", broadening awareness to
    encompass the movement and settling of energies throughout the body.

[^855]: The "physical process" (*[kāyasaṅkhāraṁ]{lang="sa"}*) is the
    breath ([SN
    41.6:1.8](https://suttacentral.net/sn41.6/en/sujato#1.8)).

[^856]: Text has "long" and "short", but "deep" and "shallow" or "heavy"
    and "light" are more idiomatic for describing the breath in English.

[^857]: "Internally" is one's own body, "externally" the bodies of
    others, or external physical phenomena. This distinction is applied
    broadly in Buddhist meditation, but it is more relevant in some
    contexts than others. In the case of the breath, one is focusing on
    one's own breath, but when contemplating, say, a dead body, or the
    material elements, there is more of an external dimension. Starting
    with "me" in here and the "world" out there, this practice dissolves
    this distinction so that we see we are of the same nature as
    everything else.

[^858]: This is the *[vipassanā]{lang="sa"}* ("insight" or
    "discernment") dimension of meditation, observing not just the rise
    and fall of phenomena, but also their conditioned "nature" as being
    "liable" (*-dhamma*) to impermanence. The meaning of this passage is
    explained at ([SN
    47.42](https://suttacentral.net/sn47.42/en/sujato)). Apart from
    these passages, *[vipassanā]{lang="sa"}* in
    *[satipaṭṭhāna]{lang="sa"}* pertains specially to the observation of
    principles.

[^859]: Mindfulness meditation leads to a range of knowledges as
    detailed by Anuruddha at [SN
    52.6](https://suttacentral.net/sn52.6/en/sujato) and [SN
    52.11](https://suttacentral.net/sn52.11/en/sujato)--24. An arahant
    is "independent" of any attachment (eg. [MN
    143](https://suttacentral.net/mn143/en/sujato)), but
    *[satipaṭṭhāna]{lang="sa"}* is also taught to give up dependency on
    views of the past and future ([DN
    29:40.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn29/en/sujato#40.1)).

[^860]: In early Pali, this practice is found only in the two
    [Satipaṭṭhānasuttas]{lang="sa"} and the closely related
    [Kāyagatāsatisutta]{lang="sa"} ([MN
    119](https://suttacentral.net/mn119/en/sujato)). It is practiced by
    developing a reflexive awareness of one's posture and activity as it
    proceeds, often assisted by moving slowly and carefully.

[^861]: "Situational awareness" (*[sampajañña]{lang="sa"}*) understands
    the context and purpose of activities. The main examples here
    illustrate the activities of daily monastic life: leaving the
    monastery on almsround, restraint while in the town, care wearing
    the robes, then mindfully eating and going to the toilet.

[^862]: This practice is intended to counter sexual desire and
    obsession. The primary focus is on one's own body, rather than
    another's body, although that can be brought in also. By focusing on
    aspects of our body that we normally prefer to ignore, we move
    towards a healthy sense of acceptance and neutrality towards our
    body.

[^863]: Thirty-one parts are mentioned in early texts, later expanded to
    thirty-two with the addition of the "brain"
    (*[matthaluṅga]{lang="sa"}*).

[^864]: The "bag with openings at both ends" is the body. Not all the
    varieties of grains and beans can be positively identified.

[^865]: While meditation on the elements is commonly taught in early
    texts, this phrase is found only in the two
    [Satipaṭṭhānasuttas]{lang="sa"} and the
    [Kāyagatāsatisutta]{lang="sa"}. Detailed instructions are found in
    such suttas as [MN 28](https://suttacentral.net/mn28/en/sujato) and
    [MN 140](https://suttacentral.net/mn140/en/sujato). This meditation
    works in any posture, whereas breath meditation is best done
    sitting, to allow the breath to become still.

[^866]: The "elements" are the four states of matter as represented by
    their primary material example and their dominant property: earth as
    a solid with the property of resisting or upholding; water as a
    liquid with the property of binding; air as a gas with the property
    of movement; and fire ("heat" or "energy") as plasma with the
    property of transformation.

[^867]: This gruesome image shows that butchery of cows was a normal
    feature of ancient Indian life.

[^868]: Cremation was expensive and not available to everyone. Bodies
    might be left in the charnel ground for a variety of reasons, such
    as local customs, lack of funds, or in cases of inauspicious death
    such as murder or execution. This is still seen in some places
    today, and monastics occasionally take the opportunity to practice
    meditation beside a corpse. However the wording of the Pali sounds
    like an imaginative exercise.

[^869]: The observed corpse is not gendered. The purpose is not to
    become repulsed by an objectified other, but to understand the
    mortality of one's own body.

[^870]: It takes decades for bones to rot to powder, again suggesting it
    is an imaginative contemplation.

[^871]: Literally "a feeling among the feelings"; the practice shows
    that the meditator contemplates specific feelings as they occur.

[^872]: Pali employs direct quotes to indicate reflexive awareness: you
    feel the feeling and you know that you feel the feeling. It does not
    mean that you have to literally say "I feel a pleasant feeling",
    although some adopt that as a meditation method.

[^873]: Feelings "of the flesh" (*[sāmisa]{lang="sa"}*) are associated
    with the body and sensual desires ([SN
    36.31:4.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn36.31/en/sujato#4.1)).

[^874]: Feelings "not of the flesh" (*[nirāmisa]{lang="sa"}*) are
    associated with renunciation and especially with the
    *[jhānas]{lang="sa"}* and liberation ([SN
    36.31:5.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn36.31/en/sujato#5.1)).

[^875]: This would include the feelings of loss, doubt, and dejection
    that can occur during the spiritual path (see [MN
    44:28.6](https://suttacentral.net/mn44/en/sujato#28.6)).

[^876]: The feeling of the fourth *[jhāna]{lang="sa"}* and higher
    liberations ([SN
    36.31:8.2](https://suttacentral.net/sn36.31/en/sujato#8.2)).

[^877]: In Buddhist theory, awareness of the presence or absence of
    qualities such as greed is explained on three levels. There is the
    simple happenstance of whether greed is present at that time or not.
    Then there is the mind freed of greed through the power of
    absorption. Finally there is the liberation from greed which comes
    with full awakening.

[^878]: The mind is "constricted internally" due to dullness and
    "scattered externally" due to the distractions of desire ([SN
    51.20:18.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn51.20/en/sujato#18.1)).

[^879]: The following terms "expansive" (*mahaggata*), "supreme"
    (*anuttara*), "immersed" (*[samāhita]{lang="sa"}*), and "freed"
    (*vimutta*) all refer to states of absorption and/or awakening.

[^880]: The [Satipaṭṭhānavibhaṅga]{lang="sa"} of the Pali Abhidhamma
    only mentions the hindrances and awakening factors in this section
    ([Vb 7](https://suttacentral.net/vb7)). This, together with a range
    of other evidence, suggests that this was the original content of
    the observation of principles.

[^881]: Here causality is introduced. In the contemplation of mind, the
    meditator was aware of the presence or absence of desire in the
    mind. Now they look deeper, investigating the cause of desire and
    understanding how to be free of it forever. This contemplation of
    the "principles" of cause and effect is the distinctive feature of
    this section.

[^882]: The Buddhist schools debated whether this included physical
    tiredness or not. The Theravada argued that it was purely a mental
    laziness, as even the Buddha got sleepy.

[^883]: "Form" (*[rūpa]{lang="sa"}*) is one's own body and the external
    material world experienced through the senses. More subtly, it
    represents the "appearance" of physical phenomena, even when
    experienced solely in the mind as color, visions, etc.

[^884]: "Perception" (*[saññā]{lang="sa"}*) is the recognition or
    interpretation of experience in terms of meaningful wholes. We see,
    for example, "color" yet we perceive a "person". In the Vinaya we
    find many examples where a person perceived things in one way, yet
    they turned out to be something else.

[^885]: In the five aggregates, *[saṅkhārā]{lang="sa"}* is a synonym for
    "volition" (*[cetanā]{lang="sa"}*). The traditions later used it as
    a catch-all category for everything that does not fit in the other
    aggregates. In the suttas, however, the purpose of the aggregates is
    not to classify everything that exists, but to contemplate aspects
    of experience that we tend to identify as a "self".

[^886]: At [SN
    35.232:3.2](https://suttacentral.net/sn35.232/en/sujato#3.2) the
    "fetter that arises dependent on both" is identified as "desire and
    lust" (*[chandarāga]{lang="sa"}*).

[^887]: These seven factors that lead to awakening
    (*[bojjhaṅgā]{lang="sa"}*, [SN
    46.5](https://suttacentral.net/sn46.5/en/sujato)) are commonly
    presented in opposition to the five hindrances (eg. [SN
    46.2](https://suttacentral.net/sn46.2/en/sujato), [SN
    46.23](https://suttacentral.net/sn46.23/en/sujato), [SN
    46.55](https://suttacentral.net/sn46.55/en/sujato)).

[^888]: "Mindfulness" includes the recollection of the teachings ([SN
    46.3:1.8](https://suttacentral.net/sn46.3/en/sujato#1.8)) as well as
    mindfulness meditation.

[^889]: Likewise, this includes the inquiry into *dhammas* as
    "teachings" as well as "phenomena" or "principles".

[^890]: Due to their development of the two wings of *samatha* and
    *[vipassanā]{lang="sa"}* meditation as described in this sutta,
    practised in the context of the teaching and training as a whole,
    the meditator realizes the four noble truths at the moment of
    stream-entry.

[^891]: The sutta now proceeds in analytical fashion, digging deeper
    into the details of the four noble truths. The fundamental
    definitions were taught in the Buddha's first sermon ([SN
    56.11:4.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn56.11/en/sujato#4.1)).

[^892]: As at [DN 15.4.3](https://suttacentral.net/dn15.4.3/en/sujato),
    *[jāti]{lang="sa"}* is invariably defined as the rebirth of beings,
    not as simple arising. The same applies to old age and death.

[^893]: In this and following definitions we mainly find mere verbal
    variations of the basic term.

[^894]: *Dukkha* ("pain", "suffering") is here restricted to physical
    pain. Elsewhere it may be any kind of painful feeling, while in the
    four noble truths it is suffering of any sort, including subtle
    forms of existential suffering.

[^895]: *Domanassa* normally means "sadness", but sometimes it contrasts
    with desire, in which case it is a form of aversion.

[^896]: *Piya* often refers to those who are dear and beloved, but as
    shown here it can also mean simply anything that is liked.

[^897]: This is an example of "painful feeling not of the flesh". The
    Buddha is here denying the efficacy of prayer, invocation, or magic
    spells.

[^898]: The following list of properties that relate to the senses
    gradually moves from the more basic to the more sophisticated.

[^899]: "Eye consciousness" is aware only of "light"; it sees colors but
    does not interpret them.

[^900]: "Contact" or "stimulus" happens when the sense base, the sense
    object, and the sense consciousness all occur together.

[^901]: "Perception" interprets the "light" that is seen, organizing it
    in meaningful wholes. For example, the eye sees the color white,
    while perception recognizes that it is "white", and further, that
    that white color is in fact a "wall".

[^902]: To continue the example, once the light has been interpreted by
    perception as a "wall", we then make the choice to walk around it
    rather than through it. Choices therefore depend on perceptions.

[^903]: "Thought" is *vitakka*.

[^904]: "Consideration" is *[vicāra]{lang="sa"}*, a more sustained
    exercize of thought.

[^905]: Mindfulness is not a path in and of itself, but rather is the
    seventh factor of the eightfold path.

[^906]: The fourth noble truth is the path, while the first path factor
    is the noble truths. These two teachings are different perspectives
    on the same *dhamma*.

[^907]: *[Saṅkappa]{lang="sa"}* is normally a synonym of *vitakka* in
    the suttas, hence the rendering "right thought". It is, however, not
    just verbalized thought, but the direction in which one applies the
    mind. This factor is the emotional counterpart of right view,
    ensuring that the path is motivated by love and compassion.

[^908]: The first three of the five precepts. "Sexual misconduct" is the
    betrayal of trust in a sexual relationship.

[^909]: Defined for a monastic in the long section on ethics in the
    [Sāmaññaphalasutta]{lang="sa"} ([DN
    2:56.1](https://suttacentral.net/dn2/en/sujato#56.1)) and for a lay
    person as trade in weapons, living creatures, meat, intoxicants, and
    poisons ([AN
    5.177:1.3](https://suttacentral.net/an5.177/en/sujato#1.3)).

[^910]: The emphasis is on "develop in this way" (*[evaṁ]{lang="sa"}
    [bhāveyya]{lang="sa"}*), that is, with the full practice including
    deep absorption as the culmination of the path as a whole.

[^911]: A similar promise of results in at most seven years is found at
    [DN 25:22.9](https://suttacentral.net/dn25/en/sujato#22.9), [MN
    10:46.3](https://suttacentral.net/mn10/en/sujato#46.3), and [MN
    85:59.3](https://suttacentral.net/mn85/en/sujato#59.3); and at most
    ten years at [AN
    10.46:7.3](https://suttacentral.net/an10.46/en/sujato#7.3).

[^912]: [Kumārakassapa]{lang="sa"} was ordained at twenty ([Kd
    1:75.1.1](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd1/en/sujato#75.1.1)). He
    features in the Vammikasutta ([MN
    23](https://suttacentral.net/mn23/en/sujato)), and his verses are
    collected in the [Theragāthā]{lang="sa"} ([Thag
    2.41](https://suttacentral.net/thag2.41/en/sujato)). He was declared
    the foremost of those with brilliant speech ([AN
    1.217](https://suttacentral.net/an1.217/en/sujato)), apparently on
    the basis of this discourse. \| The [Pāyāsisuta]{lang="sa"} is the
    only major Buddhist text that has a Jain parallel,
    Paesi-[kahāṇayaṁ]{lang="sa"}, a similar dialogue between Paesi and
    [Keśin]{lang="sa"} in [Setavyā]{lang="sa"}.

[^913]: [Setavyā]{lang="sa"} was north-east of [Sāvatthī]{lang="sa"}.

[^914]: [Pāyāsi]{lang="sa"} was a *khattiya* yet he receives a
    *brahmadeyya*. The sense of *brahmadeyya* as a donation to brahmins
    is well attested in inscriptions, so I think this is likely a
    mistake in the text, rather than evidence that the practice was not
    for brahmins only.

[^915]: This is wrong view per [MN
    117:5.1](https://suttacentral.net/mn117/en/sujato#5.1).

[^916]: This idiom recurs at [AN
    6.38:1.5](https://suttacentral.net/an6.38/en/sujato#1.5) where, as
    here, it refers to a well-known view and cannot mean that they have
    never heard of it. There the text and commentary read
    *[māhaṁ]{lang="sa"}*, which with the aorist is prohibitive not
    negatory. It is an idiom expressing dislike.

[^917]: While the world "down here" is bogged down in its messy and
    ephemeral issues, the heavenly bodies proceed in their serene,
    glorious, stately indifference. Ignoring gravity, self-luminous, and
    apparently eternal, they operate according to what appears to be an
    entirely different set of rules, a "meta-physics". What Newton did
    in physics the Buddha did in spirituality: show that the heavens,
    despite appearances, operate with the same set of conditioned rules
    as apply down here.

[^918]: Kassapa's argument here doesn't directly establish his
    conclusion. It is possible that gods exist on an entirely separate
    plane that has nothing to do with kamma and rebirth.

[^919]: This is still a major factor in shaping belief. People will
    reject the opinions of experts and believe people that they know.

[^920]: Compare [Jaiminīya]{lang="sa"} [Brāhmaņa]{lang="sa"} 1.42, where
    [Varuṇa]{lang="sa"} asphyxiated his son [Bhṛgu]{lang="sa"} in order
    to send him on a journey to the "other world", inducing a near-death
    experience. [Bhṛgu]{lang="sa"} saw men cutting other men to pieces
    and eating them, and other sights both horrifying and beautiful, all
    the while wondering if what he saw was real, before his breath was
    returned to him.

[^921]: Follow PTS reading *[uddassetvā]{lang="sa"}*, which at [MN
    82:11.9](https://suttacentral.net/mn82/en/sujato#11.9) has the sense
    "visit".

[^922]: Kassapa, however, has not established the existence of an
    afterlife, he has merely refuted [Pāyāsi]{lang="sa"}'s argument. Per
    Occam's razor, the burden of proof lies on the one who wishes to
    establish the existence of the afterlife, not on the one who denies
    it. His arguments, however, become more persuasive if they are
    understood as building on the initial agreement on the divinity of
    the sun and moon. He knows that [Pāyāsi]{lang="sa"} accepts some
    form of other world, even if he says otherwise, so the argument
    hinges on whether [Pāyāsi]{lang="sa"}'s methods are sufficient to
    *disprove* the kind of other world that Kassapa proposes, i.e. one
    driven by kamma.

[^923]: Follow PTS reading *[ubbāhati]{lang="sa"}*. Cf. [AN
    3.93:6.4](https://suttacentral.net/an3.93/en/sujato#6.4) for the
    sense "transport".

[^924]: 36,000,000 years.

[^925]: This recognizes the relativity of time.

[^926]: [Pāyāsi]{lang="sa"} makes a good point; he only relies on
    sources that he knows he can trust.

[^927]: Kassapa establishes the empirical method by which these truths
    are known. Science extends knowledge by means of external
    instruments, while meditation extends the scope of consciousness. A
    non-scientist cannot understand how a scientist establishes their
    conclusions, and can only rely on trust in the scientific
    establishment. Likewise a non-meditator cannot understand the
    capacity of expanded consciousness.

[^928]: The commentary explains *[opabhoggā]{lang="sa"}* ("reward") as
    *[pādaparicārikā]{lang="sa"}* ("wife").

[^929]: Read *[opāṭesi]{lang="sa"}*.

[^930]: Prefer [Mahāsaṅgīti]{lang="sa"} reading *[yāva]{lang="sa"}
    [vijāyāmi]{lang="sa"}* over PTS *[yāva]{lang="sa"}
    [jānāmi]{lang="sa"}* ("until I know"); it echoes *[yāva]{lang="sa"}
    [vijāyāmi]{lang="sa"}* above. The phrase is not fully coherent,
    which is understandable given the circumstances.

[^931]: "Irrationally" is *ayoniso*, literally "not sourcewise". This
    passage gives a nice real world example of what it means: the means
    employed are unrelated to the end sought.

[^932]: [Pāyāsi]{lang="sa"}'s experiments were cruel, but no more so
    than many recorded in recent history.

[^933]: We assume that a soul must be immaterial and invisible, but
    clearly this was not always the case at the time. From [DN
    1](https://suttacentral.net/dn1/en/sujato) we know that there was an
    almost inexhaustible variety of views about the self or soul.

[^934]: This echoes [Upaniṣadic]{lang="sa"} discussions of the nature of
    the dream state and its relation to death. See for example
    [Bṛhadāraṇyaka]{lang="sa"} [Upaniṣad]{lang="sa"} 2.1.18, which says
    one might become a brahmin or king in a dream.

[^935]: [Bṛhadāraṇyaka]{lang="sa"} [Upaniṣad]{lang="sa"} 4.3.19
    describes the soul returning to the body like a tired hawk returning
    to the nest.

[^936]: Aside from the cruelty, this echoes the dictum attributed to
    Galileo: "Measure what is measurable, and make measurable what is
    not so." Given sufficient precision, this method could be effective
    in testing for the existence for a physical soul that has mass.

[^937]: A dead body will, if anything, weigh less due to excretion and
    dehydration.

[^938]: The misconception that iron is lighter when heated is repeated
    elsewhere (eg. [SN
    51.22:4.1](https://suttacentral.net/sn51.22/en/sujato#4.1)). In
    fact, assuming there are no chemical reactions, it will be very
    slightly heavier due to relativistic effects, yet less dense and
    hence more buoyant. These changes are too small to be detected by
    [Pāyāsi]{lang="sa"}'s methods, though.

[^939]: The commentary explains *[āmato]{lang="sa"}* as *addhamato*
    ("half-dead").

[^940]: At [AN 9.37](https://suttacentral.net/an9.37/en/sujato) this
    rather abrupt insertion more aptly describes a deep meditation.

[^941]: [Bṛhadāraṇyaka]{lang="sa"} [Upaniṣad]{lang="sa"} 2.4.8 employs
    the same metaphor in the search for the soul.

[^942]: Accept PTS reading *gavesasi*.

[^943]: [Pāyāsi]{lang="sa"} honestly acknowledges the role of social
    conformity and shame in shaping views.

[^944]: Although this became the first story of the [Jātaka]{lang="sa"}
    collection ([Ja 1](https://suttacentral.net/ja1/en/sujato)), here it
    is not a [Jātaka]{lang="sa"}, as it is not framed as a past life of
    the Buddha.

[^945]: While the places are not specified, the journey from "east to
    west" across desolate lands suggests they may have been venturing
    from the rich plains of the Ganges west across the Thar Desert of
    Rajasthan.

[^946]: Read with PTS *gadrabharathena* ("donkey cart") for *bhadrena
    rathena* ("fine cart").

[^947]: The obviously suspicious stranger depicts the genuine dangers of
    trade in unknown regions, warning the traveler of raiding tribes as
    well as supernatural creatures.

[^948]: Again the criteria that only those who are known may be trusted.

[^949]: The ancient Indian game of dice involved casting an handful of
    *[vibhītaka]{lang="sa"}* seeds (also known as "bedda nuts", from
    Terminalia bellirica). A number divisible by four was "perfect"
    (*[kaṭa]{lang="sa"}*), so the fifth seed meant a "losing" throw
    (*kali*; cf. *[apaṇṇaka]{lang="sa"}* in [MN
    60](https://suttacentral.net/mn60/en/sujato) and notes).

[^950]: *[Pajohissāmi]{lang="sa"}* is related to Sanskrit *juhoti*,
    usually used in the sense "to offer as libation", a meaning accepted
    by the commentary here. More likely it simply means to "roll out"
    (like pouring a sacrifice) by analogy.

[^951]: *[Gāmapaṭṭaṁ]{lang="sa"}* (variants *-[padaṁ]{lang="sa"}*,
    *-[paddhanaṁ]{lang="sa"}*, *-[patthaṁ]{lang="sa"}*,
    *[pajjaṁ]{lang="sa"}*) is explained by the commentary as an
    abandoned village site.

[^952]: The sunk cost fallacy.

[^953]: Say what you will about [Pāyāsi]{lang="sa"}, he had character.

[^954]: Compare [DN 5:4.5](https://suttacentral.net/dn5/en/sujato#4.5).

[^955]: For *[guḷavālakāni]{lang="sa"}*, *[guḷa]{lang="sa"}* is "ball",
    *[vāla]{lang="sa"}* is "tail"; compare *[macchavāḷaka]{lang="sa"}*
    ("fish-tailed") at [Kd
    15:29.4.2](https://suttacentral.net/pli-tv-kd15/en/sujato#29.4.2).

[^956]: Cp. [AN 5.147](https://suttacentral.net/an5.147/en/sujato), [AN
    9.20](https://suttacentral.net/an9.20/en/sujato).

[^957]: *[Divāvihāra]{lang="sa"}* is the "day's meditation", while
    *[divāseyya]{lang="sa"}* is "siesta".
